So it is a sphere, or (with boundaries) more than one, so it is a polygon, and in both cases that shape is natural to it because it is based on its nature without there being any extraneous influence. Then the natural shapes of composite bodies are not regulated, due to their variation according to the variation of their parts in their natures and quantities, and according to their specific forms; therefore he did not discuss them. (And) he said: (The natural shape of the simple) of bodies is (the sphere), and that is (because for it), i.e., for the simple body in the sense intended here, (one power), i.e., one nature, and one power does not act in the one matter that belongs to the simple (except) one act, i.e., not different in kind. (And every shape other than the sphere has actions different in kind. For a polygonal shape has a side that is a line, another a corner, a surface, or a point, and these are things of different realities, so arbitrariness is required because the recipient and the agent are united in all.) And he doubted what was mentioned that the natural shape of the simple is the sphere, (with four aspects). (First: The earth is simple) according to their view, (and it is not spherical) because of the mountains, hills, valleys, and depressions on and in it. (And their statement) in repelling this question is that what you mentioned, the irregularities of the earth and its roughnesses on its surface, (have no magnitude relative to it, so those roughnesses on the earth are like small warts on a large sphere). For they have shown that if a mountain is half a parasang in height, the ratio of its length to the diameter of the earth is like the ratio of five-sevenths of the width of a medium-sized barley grain to a sphere whose diameter is a cubit. And on this... His saying: (so it is) (a sphere, i.e., its shape is a sphere, and likewise in his saying: so it is a polygon. His saying: (And the one power, etc.) i.e., the one power insofar as it is one does not act in the one insofar as it is one except one act, and this premise is self-evident. His saying: (First) This is a summary refutation; the judgment lags behind the proof concerning the earth, and likewise the third and the second, and the fourth is like their denial by his saying: The one agent does not act in one matter except one emptiness? It is more appropriate to make the third the second and the second the third. (Two points) His saying: (And every shape other than the sphere has different actions) This has been refuted by the elliptical shape, for it is not a true sphere, even though there is only one surface. We say: We do not accept that; rather, in its two sides that surface ends, so there are actions different in kind. There is a problem in this, and the refutation is answered by the band that is in the form of a circular ring, as will come. It is possible to answer the refutation by saying that in the elliptical shape there is length other than curvature, and in the mentioned band there is a cavity other than curvature, so in both there are different actions, so reflect. His saying: (to the diameter of the earth like the ratio of five-sevenths, etc.) The diameter of a sphere is the straight line passing through the center of the sphere and reaching its two ends. And his saying: khams (five) with a damma on the kha', and likewise his saying: sab' (seven) with a damma on the sin, and his saying: thulth (third) with a damma on the tha'. And the cubit is twenty-four fingers, and the width of each finger is three barley grains. It is the line.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the natural shape of a simple body is a sphere, followed by objections and responses. The proof begins by noting that from finitude the body becomes either a sphere (one boundary) or a polygon (multiple boundaries), but then argues that for a simple body (which has one nature and one matter) only a sphere is possible because a single power acting on a single matter can produce only one kind of action. Polygons require different actions (lines, angles, etc.) which would imply multiple actions from a single power—an absurdity. The author then presents four doubts (shukūk). Only the first is detailed: the earth is considered simple but is not perfectly spherical due to mountains and valleys. The response is that these irregularities are negligible relative to the whole earth, like small warts on a large sphere; the ratio of a mountain's height to the earth's diameter is minuscule. The marginal commentary further discusses the first objection, noting it is a summary refutation, and addresses a counterexample using the elliptical shape and the band (nitaq) form, arguing that they still involve multiple actions due to length or cavity. The passage ends with a technical explanation of the ratio and definitions of diameter, cubit, etc.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A polygonal shape could be natural for a simple body because it is also a shape.
Correction: A polygon has different types of boundaries (lines, angles) which would require different actions from the single nature, contradicting the unity of the agent and matter.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that a polygon cannot be the natural shape of a simple body?
A) Because a polygon has more than one boundary.
B) Because a polygon requires different types of actions (lines, angles) which a single power cannot produce.
C) Because composite bodies have polygons.
D) Because the sphere is the most perfect shape.

Key Takeaway
The natural shape of a simple body is a sphere because a single power in a single matter can only produce one kind of action, and only the sphere has a uniform surface.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You learned that a single nature can only produce one kind of action. Reflect: have you ever claimed to have one identity (e.g., Muslim) but allowed your actions to be inconsistent, like a polygon with different sides? Which area of your life shows the most contradiction, and what is one step you can take today to make your actions uniform with your claim?
?
The proof shows that a simple body's shape is determined by its own nature, not by external factors. Think about a time you blamed external circumstances for your shortcomings. How might you instead admit that it is your own inner nature—not the situation—that determines your response? Write down one specific situation where you will take full responsibility, starting now.
?
If your soul were a shape, would it be a sphere—consistent and whole—or a polygon—fractured and varying with each context? What is the one relationship or commitment that reveals your true shape to yourself? Will you face it, or will you keep adding more sides to avoid the single, unified shape you were meant to be?

The Objection from Earth's Mountains
Objection and response regarding the earth's mountains.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that the earth is not perfectly spherical due to mountains and how theologians respond by showing these irregularities are negligible.

Core Idea
Mountains and valleys are like warts on a huge sphere—too small to ruin its spherical shape.

After establishing that simple bodies must be spherical, the author considers an objection: the earth, considered a simple body, is not perfectly spherical due to mountains and valleys. The response is that these irregularities are negligible in proportion to the earth's size. The author provides a specific ratio to demonstrate this.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first doubt: the earth is simple but not spherical because of mountains and valleys.
Objectors use the earth as a counterexample.
2
Understand the response
The response states that these irregularities have no magnitude relative to the whole earth, like small warts on a large sphere.
The author gives a precise ratio: if a mountain is half a parasang high, its ratio to the earth's diameter is like five-sevenths of a barley grain to a sphere of one cubit.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Thus, the presence of mountains does not negate the spherical shape; they are surface features that do not affect the overall shape.

So it is a sphere, or (with boundaries) more than one, so it is a polygon, and in both cases that shape is natural to it because it is based on its nature without there being any extraneous influence. Then the natural shapes of composite bodies are not regulated, due to their variation according to the variation of their parts in their natures and quantities, and according to their specific forms; therefore he did not discuss them. (And) he said: (The natural shape of the simple) of bodies is (the sphere), and that is (because for it), i.e., for the simple body in the sense intended here, (one power), i.e., one nature, and one power does not act in the one matter that belongs to the simple (except) one act, i.e., not different in kind. It is more appropriate to make the third the second and the second the third. (Two points) His saying: (And every shape other than the sphere has different actions) This has been refuted by the elliptical shape, for it is not a true sphere, even though there is only one surface. We say: We do not accept that; rather, in its two sides that surface ends, so there are actions different in kind. There is a problem in this, and the refutation is answered by the band that is in the form of a circular ring, as will come. His saying: (to the diameter of the earth like the ratio of five-sevenths, etc.) The diameter of a sphere is the straight line passing through the center of the sphere and reaching its two ends. And his saying: khams (five) with a damma on the kha', and likewise his saying: sab' (seven) with a damma on the sin, and his saying: thulth (third) with a damma on the tha'. And the cubit is twenty-four fingers, and the width of each finger is three barley grains. It is the line.

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the natural shape of a simple body is a sphere, followed by objections and responses. The proof begins by noting that from finitude the body becomes either a sphere (one boundary) or a polygon (multiple boundaries), but then argues that for a simple body (which has one nature and one matter) only a sphere is possible because a single power acting on a single matter can produce only one kind of action. Polygons require different actions (lines, angles, etc.) which would imply multiple actions from a single power—an absurdity. The author then presents four doubts (shukūk). Only the first is detailed: the earth is considered simple but is not perfectly spherical due to mountains and valleys. The response is that these irregularities are negligible relative to the whole earth, like small warts on a large sphere; the ratio of a mountain's height to the earth's diameter is minuscule. The marginal commentary further discusses the first objection, noting it is a summary refutation, and addresses a counterexample using the elliptical shape and the band (nitaq) form, arguing that they still involve multiple actions due to length or cavity. The passage ends with a technical explanation of the ratio and definitions of diameter, cubit, etc.

جاورسة

Misconception: Mountains prove the earth is not spherical.
Correction: Mountains are tiny relative to earth's size, so earth is essentially spherical.

A) He argues that mountains are also spherical.
B) He says mountains are not part of the earth.
C) He shows that mountains are negligible in size compared to the earth.
D) He denies that the earth is simple.

Key Takeaway
The presence of mountains does not negate the spherical shape of the earth because their size is negligible relative to the earth's diameter.

The passage shows that even apparent imperfections in creation are negligible when seen in the whole. Is there an area of your faith where you are letting a minor doubt or difficulty shake your trust in Allah's perfection? What would it take to see that doubt as a mere 'wart' on a perfect sphere?

Proof That the Natural Shape of a Simple Body Is a Sphere
Al-Iji proves that the natural shape of a simple body is the sphere and refutes a counterexample.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that the natural shape of a simple body must be a sphere, based on the unity of power and action.

Core Idea
A single nature can produce only one shape: the sphere.

Al-Iji presents a foundational argument in natural philosophy: simple bodies, having a single nature, must assume a single natural shape—the sphere. This lesson walks through the proof and addresses a key counterexample.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get the overall argument. Pay attention to the structure: claim, premise, proof, and rebuttal.
2
Understand the core premise
Identify the key premise: one power (nature) acting on one matter can only produce one type of action. This is said to be self-evident.
The author calls this premise 'بديهية' (self-evident).
3
See why only the sphere works
Notice how the author argues that any shape other than the sphere involves multiple types of actions (e.g., a polygon has lines, angles, surfaces), which violates the premise.
The polygon example shows that different boundaries would require different actions.
4
Examine the counterexample and response
The elliptical shape is presented as a potential counterexample. The author argues that it still has different actions due to its endpoints. Similarly, a ring has a cavity introducing different actions.
Reflect on whether you find the response convincing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيكون كرة (أو حدود ) أكثر من واحد فيكون مضلعاً، وعلى التقديرين كان ذلك الشكل طبيعياً له لاستناده إلى طبيعته من غير أن يكون هناك تأثير غريب. ثم إن الأشكال الطبيعية للأجسام المركبة غير منضبطة لاختلافها بحسب اختلاف أجزائها في طبائعها ومقاديرها وبحسب صورها النوعية فلذلك لم يتعرض لها. (و) قال : ( الشكل الطبيعي للبسيط) من الأجسام هو ( الكرة ) وذلك ( لأن له ) أي للجسم البسيط بالمعنى المراد في هذا المقام (قوة ) أي طبيعة ) واحدة والقوة الواحدة لا تفعل في المادة الواحدة التي للبسيط (إلا) فعلاً واحداً) أي غير مختلف بالنوع ( وكل شكل سوى الكرة ففيه أفعال مختلفة أنواعها، فإن المضلع من الأشكال يكون جانباً منه خطاً وآخر زاوية أو سطحاً أو نقطة، وهي أمور متخالفة الحقائق، فيلزم التحكم لأن القابل والفاعل في الكل متحدان.

قوله : ( فيكون (كرة أي شكله كرة وكذا في قوله : فيكون مضلعاً. قوله : ( والقوة الواحدة إلخ) أي القوة الواحدة من حيث أنها واحدة لا تفعل في الواحدة من حيث أنها واحد إلا فعلا واحداً وهذه المقدمة بديهية.

قوله : ( وكل شكل سوى الكرة ففيه أفعال مختلفة قد نقض هذا بالشكل الإهليليجي، فإنه ليس كرة حقيقية مع أنه ليس هناك إلا سطح واحد . قلنا لا نسلم ذلك بل كان في جانبيه ينتهي إليها ذلك السطح، فهناك أفعال مختلفة بالنوع، وفيه نظر ويرد النقض بالنطاق الذي في صورة حلقة مدورة كما سيجيء، ويمكن أن يجاب عن النقض بأن في الشكل الإهليليجي طولاً غير الاستدارة، وفي النطاق المذكور جوفاً غير الاستدارة، ففيهما أفعال مختلفة، فتأمل .
So it becomes a sphere, or if boundaries are more than one, it becomes a polygon; and in both cases that shape is natural to it because it is based on its nature without any foreign influence. Then the natural shapes of composite bodies are not fixed because they differ according to the differences of their parts in natures and magnitudes and according to their specific forms, so he did not discuss them. And he said: The natural shape of a simple body is the sphere. That is because it (the simple body in the intended sense here) has one power (i.e., nature), and the one power acts upon the one matter of the simple body only with one action (i.e., not differing in type). And every shape other than the sphere contains actions of different types. For example, a polygon has a line on one side and an angle or surface or point on another, which are things of different realities; thus the rule would be arbitrary because the receiver and the agent are the same in all. He said: (so it becomes) a sphere, i.e., its shape is a sphere, and similarly in his saying: so it becomes a polygon. He said: (and the one power etc.), i.e., the one power insofar as it is one does not act upon the one insofar as it is one except with one action, and this premise is self-evident. He said: (and every shape other than the sphere contains different actions) This was refuted by the elliptical shape, for it is not a true sphere while there is only one surface. We say: we do not accept that; rather, at its two sides that surface ends, so there are actions differing in type. But there is consideration, and the refutation is rejected by the ring in the form of a round hoop as will come. And it is possible to answer the refutation by saying that in the elliptical shape there is length besides curvature, and in the mentioned ring there is a cavity besides curvature, so in both there are different actions. So reflect.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the natural shape of a simple body is the sphere. The author begins by stating that if a body has more than one boundary it becomes a polygon, but both polygon and sphere are natural shapes based on the body's nature without external influence. Then he notes that composite bodies have irregular natural shapes due to variation in their parts, so they are not discussed. The main proof follows: a simple body has a single nature (power). A single power acting on a single matter can produce only one type of action. Every shape other than the sphere involves multiple types of action—for example, a polygon has lines, angles, surfaces, or points, which are different realities. Since the agent and receiver are the same, having multiple actions would be arbitrary. Therefore, the sphere is the only shape that fulfills the condition of a single action. The commentary then explains the meaning of 'one power' and 'one action', calling the premise self-evident. It also addresses a potential counterexample: the elliptical shape, which has only one surface but is not a sphere. The response argues that the elliptical shape actually has different actions because its two sides involve endpoints, and similarly a ring (hollow torus) has different actions due to its cavity. Thus the proof stands.

بسيط
كرة
قوة
مادة
فعل
مضلع
شكل إهليليجي
نطاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The elliptical shape has only one surface, so it seems to contradict the proof.
Correction: The elliptical shape still involves different actions due to its endpoints (length vs. curvature), so it does not have a single type of action.

Checkpoint
According to the proof, why must the natural shape of a simple body be a sphere?
A) Because the sphere is the most perfect and beautiful shape.
B) Because a single power acting on a single matter can only produce one type of action, and only the sphere involves a single type of action.
C) Because all simple bodies observed in nature (e.g., stars) are spherical.
D) Because composite bodies are not spherical, so simple bodies must be.

Key Takeaway
A simple body's single nature forces its natural shape to be the sphere.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your fitrah (innate nature) is single, from the One Creator. But your life often feels like a polygon—pulled in many directions. Where are you letting external influences create 'different actions' in your soul, and what would it take to return to the perfect sphere of tawhid?
?
Al-Iji proves that one power in one matter yields one action. Your soul's matter—your time, energy, and body—is entrusted to you. Are your daily actions unified under one purpose (seeking Allah's pleasure), or are they scattered like the sides of a polygon? What is the one action your soul is meant to produce, and what must you eliminate to achieve it?
?
The objector thought the elliptical shape was a single surface, but it hid length besides curvature. Where in your character do you appear 'spherical' on the outside—correct in ritual or image—but inwardly have hidden elongations of pride, selfishness, or distraction? What one hidden complexity must you bring to Allah in sincere repentance?

The Sphericity of Earth: Objection and Defense
Objection and detailed defense of Earth's spherical nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Iji defends the spherical shape of the Earth against the objection of mountains and valleys, and how he uses the concepts of accidental versus essential acts in nature.

Core Idea
Earth's imperfect surface doesn't negate its natural spherical tendency.

In this passage, al-Iji addresses a classic objection: if the Earth is a simple body, why isn't it perfectly spherical? The objection points to mountains and valleys. Al-Iji first shows why a common response—that these are negligible—fails, then provides a deeper answer involving external causes and the preserving role of dryness. The ensuing discussion clarifies how nature can require a shape while accidentally preserving a different one.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the opening part where the objector states that Earth is simple but not spherical due to mountains and valleys.
Notice the four faces of doubt but focus on this first one.
2
Examine the attempted dismissal
Some respond that roughnesses are negligible like pimples on a large sphere. Al-Iji refutes this because true sphericity admits no degrees.
The mathematical ratio shows mountains are tiny relative to Earth.
3
Grasp the proper response
Al-Iji argues that Earth's natural shape is a sphere, but external forces like wind and rain deformed it. Dryness then preserves the forced shape.
This explanation allows imperfection without denying natural tendency.
4
Analyze the sub-objection and answer
A further objection: if dryness preserves the forced shape and is from nature, then nature would contradict itself. The answer distinguishes between essential and accidental acts.
Nature requires both sphere and a preserving quality; the preservation of non-sphere is accidental.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

( وقولهم ) في دفع هذا السؤال أن ما ذكرتموه تضاريس الأرض ( وخشوناتها ) الواقعة على ظاهرها و( لا قدر لها بالنسبة إليها فهي أي تلك الخشونات على الأرض ( كجاورسة على كرة كبيرة ) إذ قد بيّنوا أن الجبل إذا كان ارتفاعه نصف فرسخ يكون نسبة طوله إلى قطر الأرض كنسبة خمس سبع عرض شعيرة معتدلة إلى كرة قطرها ذراع. وعلى هذا

قوله : (الأول) هذا النقض إجمالي يتخلف الحكم عن الدليل في الأرض، وكذا الثالث والثاني ،والرابع كمنعهم بقوله : إن الفاعل الواحد لا يفعل في مادة واحدة إلا خلاء واحداً، والأنسب أن يجعل الثالث ثانياً والثاني ثالثاً.

قوله: (إلى قطر الأرض كنسبة خمس سبع إلخ) قطر الكرة بضم المستقيم المار بمركز الكرة واصلاً إلى طرفيها، وقوله : خمس بضم الخاء وكذا قوله : سبع بضم السين، وقوله : ثلث بضم الثاء والذراع أربعة وعشرون إصبعاً ، وعرض كل إصبع ثلاث شعيرات

تكون نسبة طول أعظم جبل عليها وهو ما ارتفاعه فرسخان وثلث كنسبة سبع عرض تلك الشعيرة إلى الذراع تقريباً، فلا تخرجها تلك الخشونات التي لا قدر لها بالنسبة إليها ( عن كونها كروية بجملتها لا يغني ) أي لا يفيد قولهم المذكور اندفاع ذلك السؤال ( إذ الكروية ) الحقيقية ( لا تقبل الأشد والأضعف ) حتى يتصور وجود الكروية الضعيفة في الأرض مع تلك الخشونات القادحة في كمال الكروية. فإذن حقيقة الكروية منتفية عنها قطعاً، بل وجه دفعه أن يقال : شكلها الطبيعي هو الكرة، إلا أنه وقعت هناك أسباب خارجة عنها كالرياح والأمطار والسيول فانثلم بها جزء من الأرض، ثم إن اليبوسة التي فيها حافظة لما حصل لها من الأشكال فلا جرم، ففي شكل الأرض على ذلك الانثلام المقتضي لتلك الخشونات، فيكون خروجها عن شكلها الطبيعي بتلك الأسباب وذلك لا يقدح في اقتضاء طبيعتها الشكل الكروي كما ادعيناه، فإن قيل : كون اليبوسة المستندة إلى طبيعة الأرض حافظة للشكل القسري المانع عن الشكل الطبيعي يقتضي كون الطبيعة الواحدة مقتضية لشيء، ولما لم يمنع من حصول ذلك الشيء وذلك باطل قطعاً، أجيب : بأن الطبيعة اقتضت شكلاً مخصوصاً واقتضت أيضاً كيفية حافظة للشكل مطلقاً فهذا الاقتضاء لا يخالف الاقتضاء الأول، بل يؤكده لو خليت وطبيعتها، لكن لما أزال القاسر الشكل ولم يزل الكيفية صارت الكيفية حافظة للشكل القسري ومانعة بالعرض عن العود إلى الشكل الطبيعي، ولا استحالة في ذلك

قوله : (أجيب إلخ خلاصته أن ما يمنعه اليبوسة عن الشكل الطبيعي فعل عرضي لا ذاتي، حتى ينافي اقتضاء الطبيعة لها .
And he doubted what was mentioned that the natural shape of a simple body is the sphere by four faces: First: The Earth is simple according to their view, and it is not spherical because of the mountains, hills, valleys, and lowlands on and in it. And their statement in response to this objection is that what you mentioned—the Earth's roughnesses and unevenness—have no measure relative to it; they are like pimples on a large sphere. For they demonstrated that if a mountain's height is half a league, the ratio of its length to the Earth's diameter is like the ratio of five-sevenths of the width of a medium barley grain to a sphere whose diameter is a cubit. Thus... He said: (First) This is a summary of the objection: the ruling fails to apply to the Earth based on the proof, and similarly the third and second, and the fourth like their denial by saying: the one agent does not act upon one matter except a single void. And it is more appropriate to make the third the second and the second the third. He said: (to the Earth's diameter as the ratio of five-sevenths etc.) The diameter of a sphere is the straight line passing through its center reaching its two ends. And his saying: five with damma, and similarly his saying: seven with damma, and his saying: third with damma. The cubit is twenty-four fingers, and the width of each finger is three barley grains. Then the ratio of the length of the greatest mountain on it, whose height is two and a third leagues, is approximately the ratio of seven widths of that barley grain to the cubit. So those roughnesses which have no measure relative to it do not remove it from being spherical as a whole. But that does not suffice, i.e., their mentioned statement does not repulse that question, because true sphericity does not admit intensity and weakness, such that a weak sphericity could be imagined in the Earth with those roughnesses that impair the perfection of sphericity. Therefore, the reality of sphericity is definitely negated from it. Rather, the way to repulse it is to say: its natural shape is the sphere, except that external causes like winds, rains, and torrents occurred and eroded part of the Earth, and then the dryness in it preserves whatever shapes it acquires—so necessarily, the Earth's shape is based on that erosion which necessitates those roughnesses; thus its departure from its natural shape is due to those causes, and that does not impugn its nature's requiring the spherical shape as we claimed. For if it is said: The dryness based on the Earth's nature being a preserver of the forced shape that prevents the natural shape implies that one nature requires something and at the same time does not prevent that thing from occurring—and that is absolutely false. The answer is: Nature required a specific shape and also required a quality that preserves shape absolutely; this requirement does not contradict the first requirement, but rather confirms it. If left to its nature, it would return, but when the coercer removed the shape and did not remove the quality, the quality became a preserver of the forced shape and accidentally prevented return to the natural shape. There is no impossibility in that. He said: (The answer etc.) Its summary is that what the dryness prevents from the natural shape is an accidental act, not essential, so as to contradict nature's requirement of it.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer concerning the spherical shape of the Earth. The objection is based on the observation that the Earth is considered a simple body but is not perfectly spherical due to mountains, valleys, etc. The opponent first tries to dismiss this by saying these features are negligible compared to the Earth's size, like pimples on a large sphere. The author then refutes this dismissal: true sphericity does not admit degrees; a sphere is either perfect or not. Since the Earth has irregularities that impair perfect sphericity, it cannot be called spherical in the true sense. The answer is that the Earth's natural shape is indeed spherical, but external forces (wind, rain, floods) have eroded it. The Earth's dryness then preserves the resulting forced shape, preventing it from returning to its natural sphere. A further objection states that if dryness preserves the forced shape and it is part of Earth's nature, then nature would be requiring both the spherical shape and its preservation, which seems contradictory. The reply explains that nature requires both a specific shape (sphere) and a general preserving quality. When an external force removes the sphere, the preserving quality maintains the new shape accidentally, not essentially. This does not contradict nature's primary requirement; rather, it confirms it because if left alone, the Earth would return to a sphere. The commentary summarizes that the prevention by dryness is accidental, not essential.

Key Terms
اعتراض (objection)
أرض (Earth)
كرة (sphere)
جبل (mountain)
نسبة (ratio)
يبوسة (dryness)
شكل قسري (forced shape)
طبيعة (nature)
فعل عرضي (accidental act)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection is that the Earth is not spherical at all.
Correction: The objection is that the argument for sphericity fails because Earth is imperfect; the response shows the argument only requires a natural tendency, not actual perfection.

Checkpoint
What is the final response to the objection that the Earth is not perfectly spherical due to mountains?
A) The mountains are too small to affect the overall sphericity, so the Earth is still a sphere.
B) The Earth's natural shape is a sphere, but external causes deformed it, and dryness preserved the deformation, preventing return to sphericity.
C) The Earth is not a simple body because it has multiple natures.
D) The proof only applies to celestial bodies, not the Earth.

Key Takeaway
The Earth's natural shape is spherical, but external forces can deform it; this does not invalidate nature's inherent tendency, as the preserving quality acts accidentally, not essentially.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Just as the Earth's natural shape is spherical but external forces have marred it, what external forces in your life have distorted your natural disposition (fitrah) towards Allah? Are you conscious of how your environment has pressed you into a shape that is not your original one? Will you actively seek to return to that natural curve of submission, or are you content with the forced shape you've become comfortable with?
?
The dryness of the Earth preserves whatever shape it acquires, even if it is forced. What 'dryness'—what hardened habit, pride, or attachment—is preserving a form in your character that contradicts your natural inclination to obey Allah? Can you identify one specific attitude that you have allowed to set because it feels stable, even though it keeps you from your natural state of humility? Write down one actionable step to break that preservation this week.
?
In the passage, the roughness of mountains is called 'nothing relative to the whole Earth.' Consider the sins or shortcomings you dismiss as 'negligible'—are they truly insignificant in the sight of Allah? Does their smallness in your eyes excuse them? How would you feel if Allah judged you only by those 'small roughnesses'? Are you willing to repent sincerely, not ignoring any, and seek the perfect sphere of taqwa?

Second Objection: Celestial Spheres Have Cavities for Stars
Al-Iji presents the objection and its rebuttal via two cosmological models.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection that celestial spheres must have cavities for stars is not universally valid, depending on the cosmological model.

Core Idea
The stars-in-cavities objection only holds if stars are independent bodies, not integral parts of the spheres.

This passage presents a specific objection against the necessary spherical shape of simple bodies: starry spheres appear to have cavities for stars. But the objection is not as straightforward as it seems. It rests on a particular view of how stars relate to their spheres. By exploring two competing ancient cosmological models, you will learn how this objection is rebutted.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and its description
Read the passage carefully. Notice how the objection describes cavities of different sizes and positions, seemingly contradicting perfect sphericity.
The objection assumes stars are separate bodies embedded in the spheres.
2
Understand the two cosmological views
The commentary explains two competing views: (1) stars have their own souls and movements, (2) stars are connected parts of the spheres. Identify the differences.
The second view compares stars to colored pieces of wood—different in appearance but not causing cavities.
3
See how the objection is refuted
Under the second view, there are no actual cavities or positional differences; the apparent fixity of stars is just in the imagination. This dissolves the objection.
The objection only challenges sphericity under the first view, which is not universally accepted.
4
Examine additional clarifications
The commentary also addresses a potential mathematical inconsistency (the 'approximately' phrase) and explains the meaning of 'bil-`ard' (by accident).
These clarifications strengthen the overall argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني: الأفلاك المكوكبة فيها نقر) أي حفر ترتكز الكواكب فيها ( مختلفة بالقدر) لأنها مساوية لمقادير الكواكب المختلفة الأقدار المالئة لتلك النقر ( والوضع) أي مختلفة بالوضع أيضاً لأن تلك النقر موجودة

قوله : (الأفلاك المكوكبة فيها إلخ) هذا على مذهب قوم أثبتوا للكواكب نفوساً محركة إياها وحركات وضعية على أنفسها كما أثبتوا لأفلاكها، وأما على مذهب قوم أثبتوا لكل فلك الأفلاك نفسا محركة وأن الكواكب أجزاء متصلة بالأفلاك غير متحركة ممتازة عنها، بالإشارة والشكل فهي كأبعاض خشب مختلفة بألوانها، فلا نقر ولا اختلاف في الموضع، ولا ارتكاز إلا بالوهم . مضمومة بطون بعضها ببطون بعض ، وقوله : تقريباً كانه إشارة إلى دفع ما يقال من أن هذا الكلام مخالف لما ذكره آنفاً من قوله : إذا كان ارتفاعه نصف فرسخ يكون . وقوله بالعرض بفتح العين والراء المهملتين.
The second face: The starry spheres have cavities, i.e., holes in which the stars are fixed, differing in size because they correspond to the sizes of the stars, which differ, filling those cavities, and differing in position because those cavities exist in various places. His saying: (The starry spheres etc.) This is according to the school of people who posited for the stars moving souls and positional movements of their own, just as they posited for their spheres. But according to the school of people who posited for each sphere of the spheres a moving soul and that the stars are connected parts of the spheres, not moving, distinguished from them by indication and shape, they are like pieces of wood differing in colors. So there are no cavities, no difference in position, and no fixing except in imagination. Some are joined belly to belly. And his saying: 'approximately' seems to be an indication to repulse what is said that this statement contradicts what he mentioned earlier: 'if its height is half a league, it becomes...'. And his saying: 'by accident' (bil-`ard) with fatha on `ayn and ra'.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection to the claim that the natural shape of a simple body is the sphere. The objection concerns starry celestial spheres: they have cavities (nqar) in which stars are fixed, and these cavities vary in size and position. This would mean the spheres are not perfect spheres. However, the commentary notes that this objection depends on a particular cosmological view in which stars have their own souls and independent movements. On an alternative view, stars are integral parts of the spheres, like colored patches in wood, not separate bodies. In that case, there are no cavities, no variation in position, and the fixing is only imagined. The commentary also clarifies a mathematical point about a previous ratio, and explains the meaning of 'by accident' (bil-`ard). Thus the objection is not universally valid; it only arises under a specific theoretical framework.

Key Terms
فلك (celestial sphere)
نقر (cavity)
كوكب (star/planet)
نفس محركة (moving soul)
جزء متصل (connected part)
وهم (imagination)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection about cavities is a universal refutation of the spherical shape of celestial spheres.
Correction: The objection only holds under the view that stars are independent bodies. Under the alternative view that stars are integral parts of spheres, there are no cavities, so the objection does not apply.

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, under which view does the objection about cavities in celestial spheres lose its force?
A) When stars are considered to have their own moving souls.
B) When stars are considered as connected parts of the spheres, like pieces of wood differing in colors.
C) When the spheres are made of a different substance.
D) When the cavities are too small to matter.

Key Takeaway
The objection that celestial spheres have cavities for stars is not a universal refutation; it depends on whether stars are independent bodies or integral parts of the spheres.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows that an objection only holds if you accept a certain framework. What framework do you rely on to make sense of your life—perhaps about success, hardship, or purpose—that might be limiting your understanding of Allah's creation? Will you honestly examine it?
?
When you look at the stars, do you see them as independent entities or as part of a larger, coherent system designed by Allah? How does this affect your sense of awe and tawheed?
?
The author distinguishes between what is real and what is only in the imagination. Is there any fear, doubt, or desire in your heart that you have treated as a 'real cavity' when it is only a product of your own assumptions? What step will you take today to replace it with the truth from revelation?

The Spherical Shape of the Earth and External Causes
Author refutes a weak objection and gives the proper defense of sphericity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the presence of mountains does not disprove the earth's spherical nature and how external causes account for surface irregularities.

Core Idea
Mountains are accidental breaks from external causes, not refutations of sphericity.

The author addresses a common objection: mountains seem to violate the earth's spherical shape. He first dismisses a weak argument based on relative size, then provides the correct explanation: the earth's natural shape is spherical, but external factors like wind and water have caused deformations that are preserved by dryness. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the two parts: the dismissal of the ratio argument and the positive explanation.
The ratio argument is presented but immediately called insufficient.
2
Understand the insufficiency of the ratio objection
The objector says mountains are tiny relative to earth, so sphericity holds. But the author says true sphericity doesn't admit degrees; any roughness destroys perfect sphericity.
This shows the objector's own logic is flawed if they accept perfect sphericity.
3
Learn the correct defense
The proper answer: the earth's natural inclination is spherical, but external forces (wind, rain, floods) broke parts, and the earth's dryness preserves those broken shapes. So the current shape is a deformed sphere, not contradicting the natural tendency.
This avoids claiming the earth is perfectly spherical despite mountains.
4
Reflect on the key distinction
The author distinguishes between natural shape (what nature tends toward) and accidental shape (caused by external factors). This distinction preserves both the spherical tendency and the observed irregularities.
This is a common pattern in kalam: defending a natural property while acknowledging accidental deviations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تكون نسبة طول أعظم جبل عليها وهو ما ارتفاعه فرسخان وثلث كنسبة سبع عرض تلك الشعيرة إلى الذراع تقريباً، فلا تخرجها تلك الخشونات التي لا قدر لها بالنسبة إليها ( عن كونها كروية بجملتها لا يغني ) أي لا يفيد قولهم المذكور اندفاع ذلك السؤال ( إذ الكروية ) الحقيقية ( لا تقبل الأشد والأضعف ) حتى يتصور وجود الكروية الضعيفة في الأرض مع تلك الخشونات القادحة في كمال الكروية. فإذن حقيقة الكروية منتفية عنها قطعاً، بل وجه دفعه أن يقال : شكلها الطبيعي هو الكرة، إلا أنه وقعت هناك أسباب خارجة عنها كالرياح والأمطار والسيول فانثلم بها جزء من الأرض، ثم إن اليبوسة التي فيها حافظة لما حصل لها من الأشكال فلا جرم، ففي شكل الأرض على ذلك الانثلام المقتضي لتلك الخشونات، فيكون خروجها عن شكلها الطبيعي بتلك الأسباب وذلك لا يقدح في اقتضاء طبيعتها الشكل الكروي كما ادعيناه، فإن قيل : كون اليبوسة المستندة إلى طبيعة الأرض حافظة للشكل القسري المانع عن الشكل الطبيعي يقتضي كون الطبيعة الواحدة مقتضية لشيء، ولما لم يمنع من حصول ذلك الشيء وذلك باطل قطعاً، أجيب : بأن الطبيعة اقتضت شكلاً مخصوصاً واقتضت أيضاً كيفية حافظة للشكل مطلقاً فهذا الاقتضاء لا يخالف الاقتضاء الأول، بل يؤكده لو خليت وطبيعتها، لكن لما أزال القاسر الشكل ولم يزل الكيفية صارت الكيفية حافظة للشكل القسري ومانعة بالعرض عن العود إلى الشكل الطبيعي، ولا استحالة في ذلك
The proportion of the length of the greatest mountain on it, whose height is two and a third parasangs, is approximately like the proportion of a seventh of the width of that barley grain to the cubit, so those roughnesses which have no magnitude relative to it do not make it (not being spherical in its entirety) sufficient, i.e., their mentioned statement does not benefit the repulsion of that question, since (true) sphericality (does not admit of more and less) such that weak sphericality could be imagined in the earth with those roughnesses that are detrimental to the perfection of sphericality. Therefore, the reality of sphericality is completely negated from it. Rather, the way to repel it is to say: Its natural shape is the sphere, except that there occurred external causes like winds, rains, and floods, so a part of the earth was broken off. Then the dryness in it preserves what shapes have occurred to it. So inevitably, the earth's shape is upon that breakage necessitating those roughnesses, and thus its deviation from its natural shape is due to those causes, and that does not harm its nature's requiring the spherical shape as we have claimed. If it is said: The dryness based on the earth's nature preserving the forced shape that prevents the natural shape requires that one nature necessitates a thing and does not prevent that thing from occurring, and that is absolutely false. It is answered: Nature necessitated a specific shape and also necessitated a quality that preserves shape absolutely; this necessitation does not oppose the first necessitation, rather it confirms it if left to its nature. But when the remover removed the shape and did not remove the quality, the quality became a preserver of the forced shape and an accidental preventer of returning to the natural shape, and there is no impossibility in that.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage is a defense of the earth's spherical shape despite the presence of mountains. It begins with an objection: the ratio of the largest mountain's height to the earth's size is negligible, so mountains do not negate overall sphericity. However, the author says this objection is insufficient because true sphericity does not admit degrees; rough surfaces would compromise perfect sphericity. So the correct answer is that the earth's natural shape is spherical, but external causes (wind, rain, floods) have broken parts of it, and the earth's dryness preserves the resulting shapes. Thus, the deviation from the perfect sphere is due to external factors and does not disprove the natural tendency. Then a further objection: the dryness itself is from the earth's nature, so the same nature that demands sphericity also preserves a forced shape, which is contradictory. The answer: nature demands both a specific shape (sphere) and a quality that preserves shape in general. When the removing agent removes the shape but leaves the quality, the quality preserves the forced shape accidentally, preventing return to the natural shape. This is not impossible.

Key Terms
الكرة
الشكل الطبيعي
الخشونات
اليبوسة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mountains disprove the earth's spherical shape because they are large irregularities.
Correction: Mountains are tiny relative to the earth's size, but more importantly, they are accidental deformations from external causes and do not negate the natural spherical tendency.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the argument that mountains are too small to affect sphericity?
A) The mountains are actually larger than claimed.
B) True sphericality does not admit degrees of perfection.
C) The ratio calculation is incorrect.
D) The earth is not spherical at all.

Key Takeaway
The earth's natural shape is spherical, but the observed landforms are accidental deformations preserved by dryness, not counterexamples to the spherical nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know that your fíṭrah—your natural disposition—is pure submission to Allah. But what external 'winds and rains'—society, media, desires—have broken off pieces of that original shape? Name one such cause, and decide today one concrete step to return to your natural state.
?
The author says true sphericality has no degrees: you are either spherical or not. In your spiritual life, have you settled for 'partial righteousness'—thinking small sins or occasional neglect don't spoil your core? Where are you accepting imperfection as 'close enough'? What would it take for you to stop compromising?
?
Dryness preserves the breaks—your bad habits and attachments become hardened over time. What repeated 'accidents' have become second nature to you? Are you content to let yesterday's deformations become today's character? Ask Allah for the rain of His mercy to soften your heart, then act to reshape it.

Dryness Preserving Forced Shape: Objection and Answer
Author answers an objection about nature's contradictory demands.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that dryness (from earth's nature) preserving a forced shape contradicts the natural tendency to sphericity, and how the author resolves it by distinguishing between two exigencies of nature.

Core Idea
Nature's two exigencies—shape and preservation—explain how forced shapes persist.

A sharp objection arises: if dryness comes from the earth's nature, and dryness preserves the forced (non-spherical) shape, then the same nature that demands sphericality also prevents it. The author answers by explaining that nature has two demands: a specific shape and a general preserving quality. When the shape is removed by force but the preserving quality remains, it accidentally sustains the forced shape. This lesson unpacks that subtle reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the objection: dryness is from the earth's nature, yet it preserves the forced shape. This seems to imply one nature demands both sphericality and its opposite.
The objector sees a contradiction in the same nature having two opposing effects.
2
Understand the answer's core distinction
The answer distinguishes between what nature directly demands (a specific shape) and a secondary quality (preserving shape in general). These are not contradictory.
Nature's 'preserving shape absolutely' is a general property, not specific to any shape.
3
Follow the scenario of removal
When an external force removes the natural spherical shape but leaves the preserving quality intact, that quality now preserves the forced shape. This is accidental (بالعرض), not a contradiction.
The key is that the preserving quality was originally meant to keep the spherical shape, but once that shape is gone, it keeps whatever shape remains.
4
Reflect on the logical resolution
There is no impossibility because two different existential demands (one for shape, one for preservation) can coexist and produce a combined effect under accidental circumstances.
This argument shows the depth of kalam reasoning about natural properties.

Source Text

تكون نسبة طول أعظم جبل عليها وهو ما ارتفاعه فرسخان وثلث كنسبة سبع عرض تلك الشعيرة إلى الذراع تقريباً، فلا تخرجها تلك الخشونات التي لا قدر لها بالنسبة إليها ( عن كونها كروية بجملتها لا يغني ) أي لا يفيد قولهم المذكور اندفاع ذلك السؤال ( إذ الكروية ) الحقيقية ( لا تقبل الأشد والأضعف ) حتى يتصور وجود الكروية الضعيفة في الأرض مع تلك الخشونات القادحة في كمال الكروية. فإذن حقيقة الكروية منتفية عنها قطعاً، بل وجه دفعه أن يقال : شكلها الطبيعي هو الكرة، إلا أنه وقعت هناك أسباب خارجة عنها كالرياح والأمطار والسيول فانثلم بها جزء من الأرض، ثم إن اليبوسة التي فيها حافظة لما حصل لها من الأشكال فلا جرم، ففي شكل الأرض على ذلك الانثلام المقتضي لتلك الخشونات، فيكون خروجها عن شكلها الطبيعي بتلك الأسباب وذلك لا يقدح في اقتضاء طبيعتها الشكل الكروي كما ادعيناه، فإن قيل : كون اليبوسة المستندة إلى طبيعة الأرض حافظة للشكل القسري المانع عن الشكل الطبيعي يقتضي كون الطبيعة الواحدة مقتضية لشيء، ولما لم يمنع من حصول ذلك الشيء وذلك باطل قطعاً، أجيب : بأن الطبيعة اقتضت شكلاً مخصوصاً واقتضت أيضاً كيفية حافظة للشكل مطلقاً فهذا الاقتضاء لا يخالف الاقتضاء الأول، بل يؤكده لو خليت وطبيعتها، لكن لما أزال القاسر الشكل ولم يزل الكيفية صارت الكيفية حافظة للشكل القسري ومانعة بالعرض عن العود إلى الشكل الطبيعي، ولا استحالة في ذلك
The proportion of the length of the greatest mountain on it, whose height is two and a third parasangs, is approximately like the proportion of a seventh of the width of that barley grain to the cubit, so those roughnesses which have no magnitude relative to it do not make it (not being spherical in its entirety) sufficient, i.e., their mentioned statement does not benefit the repulsion of that question, since (true) sphericality (does not admit of more and less) such that weak sphericality could be imagined in the earth with those roughnesses that are detrimental to the perfection of sphericality. Therefore, the reality of sphericality is completely negated from it. Rather, the way to repel it is to say: Its natural shape is the sphere, except that there occurred external causes like winds, rains, and floods, so a part of the earth was broken off. Then the dryness in it preserves what shapes have occurred to it. So inevitably, the earth's shape is upon that breakage necessitating those roughnesses, and thus its deviation from its natural shape is due to those causes, and that does not harm its nature's requiring the spherical shape as we have claimed. If it is said: The dryness based on the earth's nature preserving the forced shape that prevents the natural shape requires that one nature necessitates a thing and does not prevent that thing from occurring, and that is absolutely false. It is answered: Nature necessitated a specific shape and also necessitated a quality that preserves shape absolutely; this necessitation does not oppose the first necessitation, rather it confirms it if left to its nature. But when the remover removed the shape and did not remove the quality, the quality became a preserver of the forced shape and an accidental preventer of returning to the natural shape, and there is no impossibility in that.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage is a defense of the earth's spherical shape despite the presence of mountains. It begins with an objection: the ratio of the largest mountain's height to the earth's size is negligible, so mountains do not negate overall sphericity. However, the author says this objection is insufficient because true sphericity does not admit degrees; rough surfaces would compromise perfect sphericity. So the correct answer is that the earth's natural shape is spherical, but external causes (wind, rain, floods) have broken parts of it, and the earth's dryness preserves the resulting shapes. Thus, the deviation from the perfect sphere is due to external factors and does not disprove the natural tendency. Then a further objection: the dryness itself is from the earth's nature, so the same nature that demands sphericity also preserves a forced shape, which is contradictory. The answer: nature demands both a specific shape (sphere) and a quality that preserves shape in general. When the removing agent removes the shape but leaves the quality, the quality preserves the forced shape accidentally, preventing return to the natural shape. This is not impossible.

Key Terms
اليبوسة
الشكل القسري
بالعرض
الاقتضاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If the same nature demands sphericality and preserves forced shape, then nature is contradictory and the spherical claim is false.
Correction: Nature has two different demands: one for a specific shape and another for shape preservation in general. These are not contradictory because the preserving quality is general and its effect depends on circumstances.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, why does the preserving quality end up preserving the forced shape?
A) The quality itself changed to favor the forced shape.
B) The remover removed the spherical shape but left the quality, so it preserves whatever shape remains.
C) Nature decided to abandon sphericality.
D) The forced shape is actually the natural shape.

Key Takeaway
A single nature can have multiple exigencies that produce apparent contradictions under accidental circumstances, but no logical impossibility arises.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reflect on a time when you felt your own nature (your fitrah or innate disposition) seemed to demand two conflicting things. How can the lesson's insight—that a single nature can have multiple non-contradictory exigencies—help you reconcile internal conflicts instead of seeing them as signs of hypocrisy or weakness? What step will you take this week to embrace the complexity of your own created nature without despair?
?
The preserving quality of earth remains even when the natural shape is removed. Consider a virtue or good habit you have developed that seemed to 'preserve' something valuable even when circumstances changed. Are you relying on that preservation accidentally or intentionally? How can you ensure that your preserving qualities are grounded in your nature's true exigencies rather than external pressures?

The Natural Spherical Shape and Water Capacity
Al-Iji explains the corollary that closer to center means more water due to spherical surface.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a vessel closer to the center of the world can hold more water, based on the principle that water surfaces are spherical arcs centered at the world's center.

Core Idea
Water surface curvature explains why vessels near the center hold more water.

In this corollary, al-Iji builds on the spherical shape of simple bodies to explain a practical consequence: the capacity of a container depends on its distance from the world’s center. By considering the water surface as an arc of a sphere, he shows geometrically that a closer vessel has a deeper arc, allowing more water to be held. This lesson unpacks the geometric reasoning behind this claim.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claim
Read the passage and note the assertion: a vessel closer to the world's center holds more water than one farther away, given the spherical shape of simple bodies.
The 'center' here means the center of the universe.
2
Visualize the geometry
Imagine the water surface as an arc of a circle (or sphere) centered at the world's center. The vessel's mouth is a chord. Two circles with the same center pass through the ends of the chord; the smaller circle (closer vessel) has a deeper arc.
This concavity is key to the difference in water capacity.
3
Connect to water capacity
The deeper arc creates a crescent-shaped region between the two arcs. This region fills with water when the vessel is closer, adding to the volume compared to a farther vessel.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فرع) على القول بأن الشكل الطبيعي للبسيط هو الكرة ) فالإناء كلما كان أقرب إلى المركز) أي مركز العالم الذي هو وسط الكل كما إذا كان في قعر بئر مثلاً ) كان أكثر احتمالاً للماء) مما إذا كان أبعد عنه كرأس جبل ) وذلك لأن ظاهر سطحه ) أي سطح الماء إذا خلي وطبعه في أي موضع فرض ( قطعة من دائرة ( بل من سطح كرة ( مركزها مركز العالم ) قطعة المرصد الأول - الفصل الثاني : القسم الأول : المقصد الأول : في رصد الأفلاك لأنه بسيط سيال تقتضي طبيعته تساوي بعد سطحه الظاهر عن المركز حتى يكون من سطح كروي، وإنما ذكر الدائرة لأنها أسهل في التصور. ولما كان مقدار رأس الإناء شيئتاً واحداً يمر بطرفيه دائرتان مرکزهما ،واحد، وإحداهما أكبر من الأخرى، كانت القوس الواقعة على طرفيه من الدائرة الصغرى أكثر تحدباً وتقعراً من القوس الواقعة عليهما من الدائرة الكبرى كما يشهد به التخيل من كل ذي فطرة سليمة وكانت القوسان محيطتين بشكل هلالي يملؤه الماء إذا كان الإناء أقرب ويخلو عنه إذا كان أبعد فيزيد الأول على الثاني بذلك القدر من الماء أعني بما ماء بين قطعتين من سطحين كرويين يرتسمان على رأس الإناء من توهم حركتي القوسين عليه يمنة ويسرة وإلى ما لخصناه أشار بقوله : ( وكلما كانت الدائرة أصغر كان التقعير فيها أكبر بالنسبة إلى وتر واحد ) هو امتداد رأس الإناء ).
Branch: on the view that the natural shape of the simple is the sphere, so the vessel, the closer it is to the center—i.e., the center of the world which is the middle of the whole, as if it is at the bottom of a well for example—the more capable it is of containing water than when it is farther away, like the top of a mountain. That is because the apparent surface of it—i.e., the surface of water when left to its nature in any assumed place—is a segment of a circle, rather of a spherical surface whose center is the center of the world. A piece: The first observatory, second chapter, first section, first aim: on the observation of spheres. Because it is a simple fluid, its nature requires the equality of the distance of its apparent surface from the center so that it is from a spherical surface, and he mentioned the circle because it is easier to imagine. And since the measure of the vessel's mouth is one thing, two circles with one center pass through its two ends, and one of them is larger than the other, the arc falling on its two ends from the smaller circle is more convex and concave than the arc falling on them from the larger circle, as is witnessed by imagination of anyone with sound nature. And the two arcs encircle a crescent shape filled with water when the vessel is closer, and empty of it when it is farther; so the first increases over the second by that amount of water, that is, by the water between two segments of two spherical surfaces that are traced on the mouth of the vessel from the imagined movement of the two arcs on it right and left. And to what we have summarized he pointed with his words: 'and whenever the circle is smaller, the concavity in it is larger relative to one chord'—that is the extension of the vessel's mouth.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage is a corollary (فرع) explaining a consequence of the view that simple bodies naturally assume a spherical shape. The author argues that a vessel closer to the world's center can hold more water because the water surface is an arc of a circle (or spherical surface) centered at the world's center. The commentator elaborates with geometry: for a fixed chord (the vessel's mouth), a smaller circle yields a deeper arc, so the water level rises higher, creating a crescent-shaped region that fills with water. This is an astronomical-physical demonstration of the spherical shape principle.

Key Terms

كرة
مركز العالم
سطح كروي
تقعير
وتر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Water surface is flat.
Correction: The water surface is actually curved, forming part of a sphere centered at the world's center, due to water's nature as a simple fluid.

Checkpoint
Why does a vessel closer to the world's center hold more water?
A) Because water is heavier at the center
B) Because the water surface curvature is greater and the arc is deeper
C) Because the vessel is deeper
D) Because of air pressure

Key Takeaway
The closer a vessel is to the world's center, the more water it can hold because the water surface's curvature deepens, creating a larger volume.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author says anyone with 'sound nature' (فطرة سليمة) can visualize the geometry. Is your fitrah clear enough to perceive the signs of divine order in creation, or have doubts and distractions clouded it? What will you do today to polish your heart's perception?
?
Water, left to its nature, immediately forms a sphere centered on its true center. Your soul also has a natural pull toward its Creator. Are you aware of that pull, or have you pulled yourself away by pursuing other centers? What is one attachment you need to release to let your soul return to its natural orientation toward God?

Classification of Bodies: Simple and Compound
Iji classifies bodies into five divisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Iji classifies bodies into five categories based on simplicity and composition, including the subcategories of celestial and elemental bodies.

Core Idea
All bodies are either simple or compound, with five total divisions.

Iji provides a systematic classification of bodies that serves as a foundation for later discussions on the nature of celestial and terrestrial substances. This passage outlines the five categories of bodies, distinguishing between simple bodies (celestial and elemental) and compound bodies (with or without temperament). Understanding this classification is essential for grasping subsequent arguments about matter and form.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get an overview of the classification.
2
Identify the main categories
Notice that bodies are first divided into simple and compound. Simple bodies are further divided into celestial (two types) and elemental (one type).
The passage says 'three for the simple, two for the compound.'
3
Explore the subcategories
Celestial simple bodies include the spheres and planets; elemental simple bodies are the four elements. Compound bodies are divided into those with temperament (e.g., living beings) and those without (e.g., inanimate objects).
Temperament refers to a balanced mixture of elements.
4
Total the divisions
Add them up: simple has three (two celestial + one elemental), compound has two. Total five divisions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الجسم البسيط ) أي الذي لا تتركب حقيقته من أجسام مختلفة الطبائع كما نبهناك عليه . ) ينقسم إلى فلكي ،وعنصري فالفلكي الأفلاك والكواكب ) فهو قسمان : ( والعنصري : العناصر الأربعة وهذا قسم واحد ( والمركب ينقسم إلى ما له مزاج، وإلى ما لا مزاج له، فهذه خمسة أقسام ثلاثة للبسيط، واثنان للمركب.
Then the simple body—i.e., that whose reality is not composed of bodies of different natures, as we have reminded you—is divided into celestial and elemental. The celestial: the spheres and the planets, so it is two divisions. The elemental: the four elements, and this is one division. And the compound is divided into what has a temperament and what has no temperament. So these are five divisions: three for the simple, and two for the compound.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage provides a classification of bodies. First, simple bodies (whose essence is not composed of different natures) are divided into two main types: celestial and elemental. The celestial is further subdivided into spheres and planets (two divisions), while the elemental consists of the four elements (one division). Together, simples have three subdivisions. Compound bodies are divided into those with temperament (like living things) and those without (like artificial objects). Thus there are five total categories: three for simples, two for compounds.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All compound bodies have a temperament (balanced mixture of elements).
Correction: In fact, compound bodies are divided into those with temperament (e.g., living beings) and those without (e.g., artificial objects).

Checkpoint

A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) Four

Key Takeaway
Bodies are classified into five divisions: three for simple (celestial and elemental) and two for compound (with and without temperament).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your body is a compound with temperament—a balanced mixture of elements. If that balance is disturbed, you fall ill. How does this fragile equilibrium remind you of your utter dependence on the One who sustains it? What practical step will you take today to care for this trust (amānah) that is your body?
?
The spheres and planets are simple, changeless bodies, while your compound body is constantly changing and will eventually decay. How does this contrast make you feel about the temporary nature of your existence? Knowing that your body lacks the permanence of the celestial, what have you postponed doing that you must start now before your temperament dissolves?

The Nine Universal Spheres and Their Subdivisions
Al-Iji enumerates the spheres and defines universal vs partial.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the classical philosophers identified nine universal spheres, but when counting partial spheres (epicycles and eccentrics), the total is twenty-four spheres.

Core Idea
Nine universal spheres contain twenty-four spheres when including partial ones.

In this passage, al-Iji introduces the heavenly spheres as understood by ancient philosophers. He explains that the nine universal spheres observed by astronomers are not the full count; they include partial spheres, bringing the total to twenty-four. This lesson will help you grasp the structure of the cosmos according to classical astronomy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then follow the English translation. Notice the numbers and categories mentioned.
The text uses technical terms like 'falk kullī' (universal sphere) and 'falk juzʼī' (partial sphere).
2
Identify the three types of spheres
The passage states that the nine universal spheres comprise 24 total spheres: 9 universal, 6 epicycles, and 8 eccentrics. Locate these numbers in the text.
3
Understand the 'universal' vs 'partial' distinction
Universal spheres are wholes that encompass others; partial spheres are parts of another sphere. The commentary clarifies that 'universal' applies primarily to the highest sphere and the sphere of fixed stars.
This distinction is key to avoiding the misconception that only nine spheres exist.
4
Connect to historical context
The passage references the 'Tadhkira' and 'Sharh al-Isharat' as sources. Recognize that this is part of a long tradition of Islamic astronomy building on Ptolemaic models.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ القسم الأول : في الأفلاك ] ) في الأفلاك وفيه مقاصد ) ستة . المقصد الأول : في رصد الأفلاك ] إن الحكماء ) زعموا أن الأفلاك ( الكلية ( الثابتة بالرصد تسعة تشتمل ) هذه التسعة ( على أربعة وعشرين فلكاً ) أي هي مع ما في ضمنها من الأفلاك الجزئية هذا العدد. فتسعة من الأفلاك كما سيتلى عليك كلية وستة تداوير، وثمانية خارجة قوله : ( إن الأفلاك الكلية أي الأفلاك التي هو كل لاشتمالها على الأفلاك اشتمال الكل على الجزء، وكذا الجزئية ما يكون جزءاً لفلك آخر ، فالنسبة في كلا الموضعين نسبة العام إلى العام، وفي التذكرة أثبت أهل العلم تسعة أفلاك في بادئ نظرهم اثنين منها للحركتين الأوليين وسبعة سيارات السبع يسمى كل فلك منها الفلك الكلي للكواكب، وكثرة الكوكب لتضمنه جميع حركاته، فعلم من ذلك أن إطلاق الكلية على الفلك الأعظم، وفلك الثوابت بطريق التغليب لاشتراكهما إياها في ضبط الحركة وعدم كونها لفلك آخر. قوله : ( فتسعة إلخ ) هذا موافق لما في شرح الإشارات من أن المتأخرين أثبتوا لكل كوكب مثل الفلك البروج كرة مركزها مركز العالم بماس بمحدبه مقعر ما فوقه وبمعقره محدب ما
First Section: On the Spheres. It contains six aims. The first aim: On the observation of the spheres. The philosophers claimed that the universal spheres established by observation are nine; these nine comprise twenty-four spheres, i.e., they along with the partial spheres that are within them are this number. So nine of the spheres, as will be recited to you, are universal, six are epicycles, and eight are eccentrics. His words: 'the universal spheres' meaning the spheres that are a whole because they encompass spheres in the way of the whole encompassing the part, and likewise the partial are those that are part of another sphere; so the relation in both positions is that of the general to the general. And in the Tadhkira, the people of science established nine spheres in their initial view, two of them for the two first motions, and seven for the seven planets; each sphere is called the universal sphere for the planets, and the abundance of the planet because it contains all its motions. So it is known from that that the term 'universal' is applied to the highest sphere and the sphere of the fixed stars by way of predominance because both of them share in controlling motion and not being for another sphere. His words: 'nine etc.' this agrees with what is in Sharh al-Isharat that the later scholars established for each planet a sphere like the zodiac sphere, a sphere whose center is the center of the world, touching with its convexity the concavity of what is above it and with its concavity the convexity of what is below it.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage opens the section on spheres. It states that the ancient philosophers determined by observation that there are nine universal spheres, which together with partial spheres (epicycles and eccentrics) total twenty-four. The commentator clarifies the distinction between 'universal' and 'partial' spheres, explains that the term 'universal' applies primarily to the highest sphere and the sphere of fixed stars due to their role in controlling motion, and references the 'Tadhkira' and 'Sharh al-Isharat' to support the enumeration.

بروج

Misconception: There are only nine spheres in the cosmos.
Correction: The nine universal spheres contain additional partial spheres (epicycles and eccentrics), totaling twenty-four spheres.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

This prioritization invites you to consider: In your spiritual life, what is the 'highest sphere' — the first and most important thing that sets everything else in motion? Is it your relationship with God, or has something else taken that place? What would it look like to recenter your life around that primary motion?

Refuting the Objection on the Number of Spheres
The author refutes an objection that the number of spheres should be 25.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the count of 24 partial spheres against an objection that the number should be 25.

Core Idea
The Maa'il sphere is universal, not partial—preserving the count of 24.

The author has previously stated that the nine universal spheres contain 24 partial spheres. An objector challenges this, claiming the total should be 25. Read on to see how the author resolves the discrepancy by correctly classifying the Maa'il sphere.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to the objector's two points and the author's response.
2
Identify the two objections
The objector raises two issues: first, that the moon has concentric partial spheres, not just epicycles; second, that the total count should be 25.
3
Understand the refutation
Note how the author rebuts by explaining that the Maa'il sphere is part of the universal spheres, not the partial ones, so the total remains 24.
This resolves the apparent discrepancy.

There is a problem. First, because it is explicit that the partial spheres are only epicycles and eccentrics, but this is a mistake, for among the partial spheres of the moon are Jauzahar and Maa'il, two concentric spheres. Second, because the number of spheres according to the well-known view amounts to twenty-five, not that all are from the subservient with the moon? or one is missing. The epicycles are six, and for each of the planets there is an eccentric sphere except Mercury which has two eccentrics, so the eccentrics are eight, and for the moon there are two other concentric spheres as mentioned, so the number of partial spheres becomes sixteen, and with the nine universal spheres it reaches twenty-five. The way to refute this is that it has become clear to you that the Maa'il sphere is counted among the universal spheres, so with the Maa'il they are nine, and that the universal spheres contain only the epicycles and eccentrics.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes an objection to the earlier claim that the nine universal spheres contain 24 partial spheres. The objector raises two points: (1) The claim that partial spheres are only epicycles and eccentrics is wrong, because the moon has two concentric partial spheres (Jauzahar and Maa'il). (2) The commonly accepted total number is 25, not 24, because when you count all partial spheres (6 epicycles, 8 eccentrics, 2 for the moon = 16) plus the 9 universals gives 25. The refutation explains that the Maa'il sphere is actually counted among the 9 universal spheres, not among the partial ones. Therefore the universal spheres remain 9 (including Maa'il), and the partial spheres are only the epicycles and eccentrics (excluding the moon's concentric spheres which are accounted for in the universal spheres). Thus the total remains 24.

Proof of Multiple Spheres from Different Motions
The author proves multiple spheres from different motions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the observed differences in celestial motions logically require the existence of multiple spheres, based on the principle that a single body cannot possess two essential motions.

Core Idea
Different motions need different movers: one sphere cannot perform two essential motions.

This lesson presents a foundational proof from Ptolemaic astronomy: the variety of celestial motions—in direction, speed, or both—proves there must be multiple spheres. The argument hinges on a simple logical principle that a single body cannot undergo two intrinsic motions. By the end, you'll see how this reasoning establishes the multiplicity of heavenly spheres.

Guided Path
1
Read and identify the claim
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the author begins by stating that different motions indicate multiple spheres.
The passage is very concise — every word matters.
2
Analyze the logical principle
The text says 'it is impossible for one body to move with two essential motions.' Reflect on why this principle is key: a sphere can only have one intrinsic motion.
Essential motion means natural motion belonging to the body itself.
3
Connect observation to conclusion
Since motions differ in direction, speed, or both, they must belong to different spheres. Each sphere is a separate 'mover' for its own motion.
This is a direct application of the principle.
4
Consider the types of differences
The argument covers three cases: difference in direction (e.g., east vs. west), in speed (fast vs. slow), or both. This makes the proof comprehensive.
Think of actual celestial motions: planets move eastward at varying speeds, while the fixed stars move westward.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا دل على وجودها الحركات المختلفة في الجهة أو السرعة والبطء أو فيهما معا فإنه لا بد لها لتلك الحركات من محال متعددة إذ يستحيل أن يتحرك جسم واحد حركتين ذاتيتين بل لا بد لكل حركة ذاتية من متحرك على حدة
They said: The different motions, whether in direction or speed or both, indicate their existence. For those motions must have multiple places, since it is impossible for one body to move with two essential motions. Rather, each essential motion must have a separate mover.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage presents an argument proving the multiplicity of celestial spheres. The author quotes the sages: the observed differences in celestial motions—whether in direction (e.g., eastward vs. westward), speed (fast vs. slow), or both—indicate that these motions belong to distinct spheres. The logical reason is that a single body cannot undergo two essential (intrinsic) motions simultaneously. Therefore, each distinct essential motion must have its own sphere (the body that moves with that motion). This is a foundational proof in Ptolemaic astronomy for the existence of multiple spheres.

Key Takeaway
Different motions in the heavens require different spheres, because one sphere cannot move with two essential motions.
