The Blueness of the Sky: Real or Imagined?
The author argues against the view that sky blueness is merely an illusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the blueness of the sky could be a real color of a celestial sphere and not merely an optical illusion.

Core Idea
The sky's blueness may be a real color, not just an illusion from depth.

We often assume the blue sky is an optical illusion, like the sea appearing blue. But what if it's actually a real color of a celestial sphere? This passage presents an objection and a response, opening up that possibility. Let's read the original argument and see how it challenges the common view.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the objection and the author's reply.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Identify the objection
The objection claims blueness is an imagined color due to depth, like the sea. Find that part in the text.
Look for the phrase 'كما في ماء البحر'.
3
Understand the response
The author replies that blueness could also be real, and there is no proof it is only imaginary. Reflect on this logical move.
The response opens the door to a real color of the spheres.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الزرقة ( الصافية ) المرئية لونه؟ لا يقال : ذلك ( أي لون الزرقة ) أمر يحس به في الشفاف إذا بعد عمقه، كما في ماء البحر فإنه يرى أزرق متفاوت الزرقة بتفاوت قعره قرباً وبعداً، فالزرقة المذكورة لون يتخيل في الجو الذي بين السماء والأرض، لأنه شفاف بعد عمقه ( لأنا نقول : ( الزرقة قد تكون لوناً متخيلاً كما ذكرتم و( قد تكون ) أيضاً (لوناً حقيقياً قائماً بالأجسام ( وأما الدليل) القائم (على أنه لا يحدث إلا بذلك الطريق التخيلي ) أي لا دليل على ذلك، فجاز أن تكون تلك الزرقة المرئية لوناً حقيقاً لأحد الفلكين
The blueness (the pure) visible its color? It is not said: that (i.e., the color of blueness) is a thing perceived in the transparent when its depth extends, as in sea water, for it is seen blue, varying in blueness according to the variation of its bottom in nearness and distance. So the mentioned blueness is an imagined color in the air between the sky and the earth, because it is transparent with deep depth. (To which we say:) Blueness may be an imagined color as you mentioned, and it may also be a real color subsisting in bodies. (As for the proof) established that it does not occur except by that imaginary route — i.e., there is no proof of that. So it is possible that that visible blueness is a real color of one of the two spheres.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage presents an argument about the nature of the blueness of the sky. The opposing view claims that blueness is an optical illusion caused by the depth of a transparent medium, like the sea appearing blue due to depth. The author responds by stating that blueness can also be a real color inherent in bodies, and there is no evidence that it only occurs via the imaginary route. Therefore, it is possible that the blueness we see is actually a real color of one of the celestial spheres.

?

The Objection and Answer: Two Forms Requiring One Place
Objection and answer about two forms requiring one place and avoiding interpenetration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objector argues that two forms requiring the same place leads to interpenetration or rectilinear motion, and how the answer resolves this by noting that the forms do not coexist.

Core Idea
Two forms can require the same place because they never exist together.

This passage tackles a specific objection about the relationship between generated and corrupted forms in celestial matter. The objector claims that if both forms require the same place, then either they interpenetrate or motion occurs. The answer reveals a crucial insight: the forms are not simultaneous, so only one body exists at a time, avoiding both problems. Let's examine the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection scenario
The objector sets up three possibilities for where the generated form can move relative to the corrupted form's place. Read carefully to see how each leads to either rectilinear motion or interpenetration.
The objector assumes different natural places for each form.
2
Grasp the answer's key move
The answer states that the conjunction of the two forms in matter is impossible; one ceases when the other arises. This means there is never a moment when two bodies occupy the same place.
This avoids both interpenetration and unnecessary motion.
3
See the supporting point
The author adds that different forms can share necessary concomitants, like requiring the same place, even if they differ in essence. This further justifies that two forms can require the same place without problem.
If forms are identical in essence, the point is even more obvious.
4
Reflect on the resolution
Because only one body exists at a time in that natural place, none of the objector's feared consequences follow. The objector's error was assuming simultaneous existence of both forms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - الفصل الثاني : القسم الأول : المقصد الثاني : في اتجاهات الجسم المستقيمة على الفلك، وإن تعدد حيزهما الطبيعي لزم أيضاً صحة الحركة المستقيمة عليه، وذلك لأن المادة إنما تلبس الصورة الكائنة حيث تخلع الصورة الفاسدة، فإن كانت الفاسدة في مكانها جاز أن تتحرك الكائنة إلى مكان آخر طبيعي لها، وإن كانت الفاسدة في مكان الكائنة، جاز تحركها حين كانت باقية إلى مكان نفسها، وإن كانت في مكان ثالث جازت الحركة المستقيمة على كل منهما . (والجواب :) بعد تسليم ما مر من امتناع الحركة المستقيمة ( أن الصورتين) أعني الكائنة والفاسدة ( قد تقتضيان حيزاً واحداً وليس يلزم من ذلك صحة التداخل أو الحركة المستقيمة، كما ذكرته ( إذ قولك : لأنهما لا يحصلان فيه إلى آخره فرع اجتماع الصورتين في المادة الفلكية حتى يتحصل هناك جسمان يقتضيان مكاناً واحداً فيقال حينئذ هما معا في ذلك المكان فيلزم التداخل أو ليس شيء منهما أو أحدهما فيه فيلزم صحة الحركة ( وإنه ) أي اجتماع الصورتين في المادة وتحصل جسمين منهما معاً ( محال بل تعدم واحدة ) من الصورتين ( عند ما توجد الأخرى) منهما، فلا يكون هناك إلا جسم واحد حاصل في ذلك المكان الطبيعي في المادة قبل الفساد، كانت فيه الفاسدة ومعه وبعده مع الكائنة فلا يلزم شيء من المحذورين ( ومما يحققه ) أي يحقق ما ذكرناه من جواز اقتضاء الصورتين حيزا واحدا (أن الصورتين مع اختلافهما ) في الماهية النوعية ( لا يمتنع اشتراكهما في لازم واحد وهو اقتضاء ذلك الحيز ) فإن الحقائق المختلفة يجوز اشتراكها في اللوازم، وإن فرض أن الصورتين متفقتان في الماهية كان ذلك الجواز أظهر )

If their natural places were multiple, then the validity of rectilinear motion upon it would also follow, because matter only puts on the generated form where it removes the corrupted form. If the corrupted form is in its place, it is possible that the generated form moves to another natural place for it. And if the corrupted form is in the place of the generated form, it is possible for it to move while it remains to its own place. And if it is in a third place, rectilinear motion is possible from each of them. (The answer:) After admitting what was mentioned of the impossibility of rectilinear motion, (that the two forms), I mean the generated and the corrupted, (may require a single place, and it does not follow from that the validity of interpenetration or rectilinear motion, as you mentioned). Because your statement that 'they do not occur in it, etc.' depends on the conjunction of the two forms in celestial matter such that two bodies arise requiring one place; then it is said that both are together in that place, so interpenetration follows, or neither of them or one of them is in it, so rectilinear motion follows. (And that), i.e., the conjunction of the two forms in matter and the arising of two bodies from them together, (is impossible. Commentary
This passage presents a complex objection-and-answer regarding the rectilinear motion of celestial bodies. It begins by stating the problem: if the generated and corrupted forms each have different natural places, then rectilinear motion from one place to another would be possible. The objector argues that if the two forms require the same place, interpenetration or motion would follow. A supporting point follows: even if the two forms differ in specific essence, they can still share a necessary concomitant like requiring a specific place, as different realities can share attributes. If they are identical in essence, the permissibility is even clearer.

One Body, One Natural Place
A gloss quoting al-Shifa on one body not having two natural places.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a single body cannot have two distinct natural places, and how potential places within a larger whole are possible.

Core Idea
A body has only one actual natural place, but can have potential places within a larger whole.

This gloss from Ibn Sina's al-Shifa clarifies a subtle point in natural philosophy: a single body cannot occupy two different natural places simultaneously. However, there is a nuance concerning potential places within a larger encompassing place, which the gloss explains through the example of a dimension.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Let the author's own words set the stage.
Pay attention to the distinction between actual and potential places.
2
Identify the rule and the exception
Note the central claim: one body cannot have two natural places, except in a specific manner involving potential places within a whole.
The exception involves a 'particularizer' (مخصص) that makes a potential place natural.
3
Understand the reasoning for impossibility
The gloss states that two distinct places are impossible because it contradicts the 'one in person' nature of the body. Reflect on why this is a requirement.
Consider how a body's identity ties to its natural place.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (والجواب إلخ) في الشفاء أنه لا يجوز أن يكون لجسم واحد مكانان طبيعيان إلا على جهة أن في جملة مكان الكل أحيازاً بالقوة؛ إن وقع فيه بسبب مخصص كان طبيعياً له كالمدة فإن أقرب حيز من الأرض يليها هو طبيعي لها. والأبعد حصوله فيه لكان أيضاً أقرب، وكان طبيعياً لها وأما مكانان متباينان فليس يمكن ذلك، فإنه مقتضى الواحد بالشخص من حيث هو واحد بالشخص .اهـ. فعلم من ذلك أنه لا يجوز أن يكون بجسمين مشخصين مكان يصير
His statement: (The answer, etc.) in al-Shifa: It is not permissible for one body to have two natural places except in the manner that in the whole of the place of the whole there are potential places; if it occurs there due to a particularizer, it becomes natural for it, like the dimension, for the nearest place to the earth that is adjacent to it is natural for it. And the farther occurrence in it would also be nearer, and it would be natural for it. As for two distinct places, that is not possible, because it is a requirement of the one in person insofar as it is one in person. End. So it is known from that that it is not permissible for there to be a place for two distinct bodies.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This is a marginal gloss that quotes from al-Shifa (Ibn Sina's work) to clarify the rule about one body having two natural places. It explains that a single body cannot have two distinct natural places; it can only have one place, though it may have a potential place within a larger whole if a particularizer makes it natural. For example, a body may have a nearest natural place (like earth's proximity to the center) and a farther place that is still natural if it becomes the nearest. Distinct places are impossible because the body's requirement of place is one in person. The gloss concludes that two distinct bodies cannot share the same place.

Key Terms
مكان طبيعي
واحد بالشخص
حيز بالقوة
مخصص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A body can have multiple natural places simultaneously.
Correction: A body can only have one actual natural place; other places are only potential within a larger whole.

Checkpoint
According to the gloss from al-Shifa, what is the condition for a body to have what appears to be two natural places?
A) The body must be composite.
B) The places must be distinct and separate.
C) The places are potential within the whole place, and a particularizer makes one natural.
D) It is impossible under any condition.

Key Takeaway
A single body can have only one actual natural place, but potential places within a larger whole are possible through a particularizer.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage says 'the nearest place to the earth is natural for it.' What is the 'nearest place' to your soul? Are you resting in that proximity to God, or are you further away, thinking that distance can also become natural? What particularizer has placed you where you are?

Clarification on Identical Forms and Generation/Corruption
Gloss clarifying that identical forms cannot entail generation and corruption.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why identical forms cannot involve generation and corruption, and how the glossator handles the ambiguity of the previous statement.

Core Idea
Identical forms cannot generate or corrupt because generation requires a change in specific essence.

This gloss digs into a subtle point about forms and essences. The previous speaker claimed that if two forms are identical in essence, the permissibility of a certain point is more apparent. The glossator challenges this by showing that identical forms rule out generation and corruption entirely, and then offers possible interpretations to salvage the statement.

Guided Path
1
Read the gloss carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythmic exposition come through before you interpret.
Notice the glossator's use of 'لا يخفى عليك' (it is not hidden from you) to signal a clear point.
2
Identify the core claim
The gloss states that if two forms are identical in essence, generation and corruption are not conceivable. Understand why: because generation and corruption require a change of the specific form that differs in essence.
This is the key logical point.
3
Examine the glossator's interpretations
The glossator suggests two possible interpretations of the original statement: either 'change' means mere change of form (whether specific or individual), or 'essence' here refers to the common essence (genus). Reflect on how each interpretation affects the original claim.
This shows the glossator's analytical depth.
4
Grasp the difficulty
The glossator then points out that if the latter interpretation is taken, the statement 'that permissibility is even more apparent' becomes problematic. Ask yourself why this would be the case.
This connects to the broader discussion about permissibility of shared concomitants.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وإن فرض أن الصورتين متفقتان في الماهية إلخ) لا يخفى عليك أنه إذا كانت الصورتان متفقتين في الماهية لم يتصور هناك كون وفساد لما مر أنهما لا يكونان إلا بتبدل الصورة النوعية المتخالفة في الماهية فلعله أراد بها مجرد تبدل الصورة نوعية كانت أو شخصية أو أراد بالماهية هاهنا ما يتناول الماهية المشتركة، أعني الجنس، لكن حينئذ لم يكن وجه قوله : كان ذلك بالجواز أظهر ظاهراً كما لا يخفى .
His statement: (And if it is assumed that the two forms are identical in essence, etc.) It is not hidden from you that if the two forms are identical in essence, then generation and corruption are not conceivable there, because it has been mentioned that they occur only through the change of the specific form that differs in essence. Perhaps he meant by it mere change of form, whether specific or individual, or he meant by essence here what includes the common essence, i.e., the genus. But then the meaning of his statement 'that permissibility is even more apparent' would not be obvious, as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This gloss comments on a previous statement that if the two forms are identical in essence, the permissibility of them sharing a necessary concomitant is even more apparent. The glossator notes that if the forms are identical in essence, generation and corruption (which require change of specific form) are not possible. He suggests that the previous speaker might have meant mere change of form (whether specific or individual) or that 'essence' here refers to the common essence (genus). If so, then the statement that the permissibility is even more apparent becomes problematic. The gloss thus highlights a subtlety about the terms 'essence' and 'form' in this context.

Why the Outermost Sphere's Surfaces Cannot Increase or Decrease
Argument for the impossibility of increase/decrease in the outermost sphere's convex and concave surfaces.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the convex and concave surfaces of the outermost sphere cannot undergo quantitative change (increase or decrease) according to the author's reasoning.

Core Idea
The outermost sphere's surfaces cannot change because there is no place to expand into and no matter to fill a contraction.

This passage presents the argument for the impossibility of quantitative motion in the celestial spheres, starting with the outermost sphere. The author argues that its convex surface cannot increase because beyond it is pure non-being, and cannot decrease because that would create a vacuum. The same reasoning applies to its concave surface due to the sphere's simplicity, and then extends to all spheres in contact.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the flow of the argument. Note the key terms: محدب (convex), مقعر (concave), محدّد (outermost sphere).
2
Identify the two cases for the convex
The author first discusses the convex surface. Identify the two scenarios: increase and decrease. Notice the reasoning for each: increase requires an empty place beyond (impossible), decrease leaves an empty place (impossible).
3
Apply the same to the concave
The concave surface is argued to be similar in essence due to the sphere's simplicity. Understand that what applies to the convex applies to the concave.
4
Extend the argument to all spheres
Notice how the author extends the reasoning to the convex of the next sphere (المحوى) and then to all spheres in a chain. This shows the universal application of the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ينتقص أيضاً، لا بالذبول ولا بالتكاثف . ( أما محدبه فإذ لو ازداد، لكان ثمة مكان خال ينتقل محدب المحدد ( إليه ) ويملؤه ذلك الزائد ( وقد علمت أن ما وراءه عدم محض) فلا يتصور هناك مكان خال ( ولو انتقص ) محدب المحدد ( لزم خلو مكانه إذ ليس ثمة شيء ينتقل إليه بدله ليشغله فيبقى خالياً. ( وأما مقعره فلأنه مثل المحدب في الماهية (للبسائط) أي بساطة الفلك المحدد (فيمتنع عليه ما يمتنع على المحدب) من الازدياد والانتقاص ) لأن حكم الشيء حكم مثله، فكذا محدب المحوى المماس لمقعر المحدد لا يزداد ولا ينتقص ( لعدم المكان ) فلا يتصور ازدياده ( وامتناع الخلاء) فلا يتصور انتقاصه، فكذا مقعره) المساوي لمحدبه، وهكذا مسوق الكلام ) إلى أن يستوعب الأفلاك،
It also does not decrease, neither by withering nor by condensation. (As for its convex surface, for if it increased, there would be an empty place to which the convex of the outermost sphere would move and fill it, and you have known that what is beyond it is pure non-being, so an empty place is not conceivable there. And if the convex of the outermost sphere decreased, its place would become empty since there is nothing to move into its place to occupy it, so it would remain empty. (As for its concave surface, because it is similar to the convex in essence) due to the simplicity of the outermost sphere, (so what is impossible for the convex is impossible for it) regarding increase and decrease, because the judgment of a thing is the judgment of its like. Similarly, the convex of the sphere contained, which touches the concave of the outermost sphere, does not increase or decrease (due to lack of place) so its increase is inconceivable, (and the impossibility of vacuum) so its decrease is inconceivable. So too its concave, equal to its convex, and thus the discourse proceeds until it encompasses all spheres.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage provides the main argument for the impossibility of quantitative motion (increase or decrease) in the celestial spheres, specifically focusing on the outermost sphere (al-muhaddid). The argument is twofold: the convex surface cannot increase because there is no empty place beyond it, only non-being; it cannot decrease because its place would become empty with nothing to fill it. The concave surface is argued to be similar in essence due to the sphere's simplicity, so what applies to the convex applies to it. Then the argument extends to the convex of the next sphere in contact, and so on, ultimately covering all spheres. The author emphasizes that the impossibility is due to lack of place and the impossibility of vacuum.

محوى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The concave surface of the outermost sphere could increase or decrease independently of the convex.
Correction: The author argues that due to the sphere's simplicity, the concave shares the same essence as the convex, so the same impossibility applies.

Checkpoint
Why can the convex surface of the outermost sphere not increase?
A) Because it would cause the sphere to break.
B) Because there is no empty place beyond it to fill.
C) Because the matter is too dense.
D) Because the concave surface prevents it.

Key Takeaway
The outermost sphere's surfaces cannot undergo quantitative change because there is no place to expand into and no matter to fill a contraction, and this applies to all celestial spheres.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The outermost sphere cannot increase because there is no place beyond to expand into. Is there an area of your life where you are striving for 'more'—status, possessions, or achievements—that lies beyond what Allah has decreed for you? What emptiness do you risk by ignoring the limit He set? Will you trust that His decree is enough and stop chasing beyond it?
?
The proof of the sphere's inability to decrease relies on the impossibility of a vacuum. Where in your heart is there a vacuum—a lack of trust, gratitude, or obedience—that you try to fill with something other than Allah? What happens if that vacuum remains unfilled by Him? What will you replace your misplaced reliance with?

Why the Convex Cannot Move: Not Intrinsic
Objection against the convex's immobility and its resolution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the impossibility of motion for the convex of the outermost sphere is not due to its nature but due to the lack of external space, and why this argument does not apply to other celestial spheres.

Core Idea
The convex's immobility is caused by lack of place, not its own essence.

The previous argument claimed that the outermost sphere cannot undergo quantitative motion. However, a closer examination reveals that the impediment is not an intrinsic property of the sphere but the absence of external space. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and shows that other spheres remain eligible for quantitative change.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once to grasp the flow.
Notice the parentheses indicating commentary from the original text.
2
Identify the key distinction
The passage distinguishes between the convex (outer surface) and the concave (inner surface). The convex's motion is impossible because there is no place beyond, not because it is inherently inert.
This distinction is crucial for understanding why the concave can adjust.
3
Understand the non-applicability to other spheres
The argument relies on the simplicity of the outermost sphere, but other spheres may be composite. Therefore, they are not bound by the same impossibility.
4
Analyze the objection and answer
The objection posits that concave increase leads to interpenetration and decrease to vacuum. The answer denies this, citing the possibility of adjacent sphere adjustment.
The passage notes this objection is against the philosophers' view, not necessarily ours.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يخفى عليك أن امتناع حركة المحدب) أي محدب المحدد بالزيادة أو النقصان (ليس له لذاته ) حتى يجب مشاركة مقعره له في ذلك، بل لأنه ليس وراءه مكان ولا شيء يملأ مكانه ( فلا يجب ) حينئذ ( مشاركة مقعره له في امتناع الحركة، بل يجوز أن يزداد مقعره وينتقص محدب المحوى بمقدار ازدياده وأن ينتقص ويزداد محدب المحوى بحيث يملأ مكانه ( و) لا يخفى أيضاً ( أنه ( أي الدليل المذكور ( لا يتأتي في سائر الأفلاك ( لابتنائه على البساطة ولم نثبت إلا في المحدد فلو امتنع ازدیاد محدب الثامن وانتقاصه مثلا لم لم يلزم مثل ذلك في مقعره لجواز تركبه بسائط مختلفة من الحقائق والأحكام فإن قلت : يلزم من ازدياد مقعره التداخل ومن انتقاصه الخلاء، قلت : هذا اللزوم ممنوع لجواز انتقاص محدب السابع وازدياده، وهذا الذي أوردنامه من الاعتراض إنما هو على رأيهم . ( وأما على رأينا فالمنع على دليلهم ( ظاهر لجواز الخلاء ) وراء العالم بل مطلقاً، فيجوز ازدياد محدب الفلك الحاوي للكل، إذ هناك مكان يشغله ويجوز انتقاصه وخلو مكانه ( و ) على تقدير امتناع الخلاء. نقول : واحد بالشخص، وإلا لزم توارد العلتين المستقلتين على معلول واحد شخصي، لأن كل واحد من الجسمين مع شرائط حصوله في ذلك الحيز المعين علة تامة له وذلك ممتنع سواء كان بالاجتماع أو بالبديهة، إلا إذا كان وجود أحدهما بحيث يمتنع وجوده بالآخر على ما مر في مباحث العلة. قوله : (أما محدبه إلخ) الأظهر على ما في شرح الإشارات أن الحركة الكمية لا تتحقق إلا بالحركة المستقيمة للأجراء والمحدد يمتنع عليه وكذلك سائر الأفلاك لأن فيها مبدأ الحركة المستديرة، فلا يكون فيها مبدأ الحركة المستقيمة وأما ما ذكره المصنف ففيه بحث، لأن المحدد لا مكان له بمعنى السطح، بل له وضع فإذا تحرك في الكم يحصل له وضع غير ما كان، لأنه يملأ مكاناً عند الازدياد، ويخلو مكان عند الانتقاص . نعم لو كان المكان المجرد كان خلوه عن الشاغل محالاً . بمعنى البعد
And it is not hidden from you that the impossibility of motion of the convex, i.e., the convex of the outermost sphere by increase or decrease, is not due to itself such that its concave must share in that; rather, because there is no place beyond it and nothing to fill its place. So then sharing of its concave in the impossibility of motion is not necessary. Rather, it is possible that its concave increases and the convex of the sphere contained decreases by the amount of its increase, and that it decreases and the convex of the sphere contained increases such that it fills its place. And it is also not hidden that (that argument) does not apply to the other spheres, because it is based on simplicity, and we have only established simplicity in the outermost sphere. For if, for example, the increase and decrease of the convex of the eighth sphere were impossible, why would the same not follow for its concave, given the possibility of its composition from different simple entities in realities and properties? If you say: from the increase of its concave follows interpenetration, and from its decrease follows vacuum, I say: this entailment is denied because the convex of the seventh sphere may decrease and increase. And this objection that we have raised is only according to their view. (As for our view, the objection to their argument is apparent, because vacuum is permissible) beyond the world, indeed absolutely. So the increase of the convex of the sphere encompassing all is permissible, since there is a place to occupy it, and its decrease and the vacancy of its place are permissible. (And) on the assumption of the impossibility of vacuum, we say: it is one in person; otherwise, the concurrence of two independent causes on one individual effect would follow, because each of the two bodies with the conditions of its occurrence in that specific place is a complete cause for it, and that is impossible whether by conjunction or by alternative, unless the existence of one is such that its existence by the other is impossible, as earlier in the discussions of cause. His statement: (As for its convex, etc.) The more apparent, according to what is in Sharh al-Isharat, is that quantitative motion is only realized by rectilinear motion of particles, and it is impossible for the outermost sphere, and likewise for the other spheres, because they contain a principle of circular motion, so they do not contain a principle of rectilinear motion. As for what the author mentioned, there is discussion, because the outermost sphere does not have a place in the sense of surface, but rather a position. So if it moves in quantity, it acquires a position different from what it was, because it fills a place upon increase and empties a place upon decrease. Yes, if place were abstract, its being empty of an occupant would be impossible, in the sense of dimension.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage contains objections and clarifications to the previous argument about quantitative motion. First, it notes that the impossibility of motion for the convex of the outermost sphere is not intrinsic to it but due to lack of place beyond; thus, the concave does not necessarily share the impossibility. The concave could increase while the convex of the sphere inside decreases, and vice versa. Second, the argument does not apply to other spheres because they may not be simple like the outermost sphere; they could be composed of different simple entities. An objection about interpenetration and vacuum is answered by denying the entailment, since the adjacent sphere can adjust. The author distinguishes between the view of the philosophers (their view) and the author's own view (likely Ash'ari or more accepting of vacuum). According to the author's view, vacuum is permissible, so the convex of the encompassing sphere can increase and decrease. Finally, a gloss from Sharh al-Isharat is quoted, stating that quantitative motion requires rectilinear motion of particles, which celestial spheres lack because they have circular motion only. The gloss also critiques the author's (al-Jurjani's) argument by noting that the outermost sphere has position, not place in the sense of surface, so its quantitative motion would change its position. It concludes by noting that if place were abstract (like dimension), vacancy would be impossible.

Key Terms
حركة كمية
محدب
مقعر
بساطة
تركيب
خلاء
تداخل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The impossibility of motion for the convex implies the same for the concave.
Correction: The impossibility is not intrinsic but due to lack of external space; thus the concave can move because it has space outside (the interior of its sphere).

Checkpoint
Why is the impossibility of motion for the convex not intrinsic?
A) Because the convex is not simple.
B) Because there is no place beyond the outermost sphere.
C) Because the concave shares the same nature.
D) Because the sphere is composed of different simple entities.

Key Takeaway
The convex's immobility is context-dependent, not a necessary property; other spheres can undergo quantitative change.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a spiritual struggle (like consistency in prayer or avoiding sin), do you treat it as an intrinsic impossibility ('I can never change') or do you consider that the obstacle might be external and removable? How does this lesson challenge you to examine your own assumptions about your limitations? What specific obstacle will you address today, knowing that immobility is not intrinsic?
?
The philosophers argued from simplicity that the outermost sphere cannot change. In your own life, do you ever assume that because your current state is 'simple' or entrenched, you cannot grow? How might God be using external circumstances (like trials or blessings) to make space for change? Will you ask Him to show you one area where you have mistaken an external limitation for an intrinsic one?

Quantitative Motion and Vacuum: Our View
Author's view on vacuum and quantitative motion, with a cause argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the permissibility of vacuum allows quantitative motion for the outermost sphere in the author's view, and the argument about concurrent causes on the assumption of no vacuum.

Core Idea
If vacuum is allowed, the cosmos can expand; if not, a unique cause argument blocks two bodies occupying the same place.

The philosophers denied vacuum, but the author's tradition permits it. This fundamentally changes the possibility of increase and decrease for the outermost sphere. The lesson also considers the philosophical scenario without vacuum, leading to a detailed argument about two bodies being complete causes for the same effect.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation focusing on the distinction between 'our view' and 'their view'.
The gloss from Sharh al-Isharat offers further critique.
2
Understand the role of vacuum
In our view, vacuum exists beyond the world, so the outermost sphere can expand into it. This solves the previous objection.
3
Examine the hypothetical without vacuum
If vacuum is impossible, the passage presents an argument about two bodies as complete causes for the same location, which is impossible unless one depends on the other.
This argument is dense and requires careful reading.
4
Reflect on the gloss
The gloss from Sharh al-Isharat contends that quantitative motion requires rectilinear motion, which celestial spheres lack; it also critiques the author's view on position vs. place.
This shows ongoing scholarly debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

( وأما على رأينا فالمنع على دليلهم ( ظاهر لجواز الخلاء ) وراء العالم بل مطلقاً، فيجوز ازدياد محدب الفلك الحاوي للكل، إذ هناك مكان يشغله ويجوز انتقاصه وخلو مكانه ( و ) على تقدير امتناع الخلاء. نقول : واحد بالشخص، وإلا لزم توارد العلتين المستقلتين على معلول واحد شخصي، لأن كل واحد من الجسمين مع شرائط حصوله في ذلك الحيز المعين علة تامة له وذلك ممتنع سواء كان بالاجتماع أو بالبديهة، إلا إذا كان وجود أحدهما بحيث يمتنع وجوده بالآخر على ما مر في مباحث العلة. قوله : (أما محدبه إلخ) الأظهر على ما في شرح الإشارات أن الحركة الكمية لا تتحقق إلا بالحركة المستقيمة للأجراء والمحدد يمتنع عليه وكذلك سائر الأفلاك لأن فيها مبدأ الحركة المستديرة، فلا يكون فيها مبدأ الحركة المستقيمة وأما ما ذكره المصنف ففيه بحث، لأن المحدد لا مكان له بمعنى السطح، بل له وضع فإذا تحرك في الكم يحصل له وضع غير ما كان، لأنه يملأ مكاناً عند الازدياد، ويخلو مكان عند الانتقاص . نعم لو كان المكان المجرد كان خلوه عن الشاغل محالاً . بمعنى البعد
And it is not hidden from you that the impossibility of motion of the convex, i.e., the convex of the outermost sphere by increase or decrease, is not due to itself such that its concave must share in that; rather, because there is no place beyond it and nothing to fill its place. So then sharing of its concave in the impossibility of motion is not necessary. Rather, it is possible that its concave increases and the convex of the sphere contained decreases by the amount of its increase, and that it decreases and the convex of the sphere contained increases such that it fills its place. And it is also not hidden that (that argument) does not apply to the other spheres, because it is based on simplicity, and we have only established simplicity in the outermost sphere. For if, for example, the increase and decrease of the convex of the eighth sphere were impossible, why would the same not follow for its concave, given the possibility of its composition from different simple entities in realities and properties? If you say: from the increase of its concave follows interpenetration, and from its decrease follows vacuum, I say: this entailment is denied because the convex of the seventh sphere may decrease and increase. And this objection that we have raised is only according to their view. (As for our view, the objection to their argument is apparent, because vacuum is permissible) beyond the world, indeed absolutely. So the increase of the convex of the sphere encompassing all is permissible, since there is a place to occupy it, and its decrease and the vacancy of its place are permissible. (And) on the assumption of the impossibility of vacuum, we say: it is one in person; otherwise, the concurrence of two independent causes on one individual effect would follow, because each of the two bodies with the conditions of its occurrence in that specific place is a complete cause for it, and that is impossible whether by conjunction or by alternative, unless the existence of one is such that its existence by the other is impossible, as earlier in the discussions of cause. His statement: (As for its convex, etc.) The more apparent, according to what is in Sharh al-Isharat, is that quantitative motion is only realized by rectilinear motion of particles, and it is impossible for the outermost sphere, and likewise for the other spheres, because they contain a principle of circular motion, so they do not contain a principle of rectilinear motion. As for what the author mentioned, there is discussion, because the outermost sphere does not have a place in the sense of surface, but rather a position. So if it moves in quantity, it acquires a position different from what it was, because it fills a place upon increase and empties a place upon decrease. Yes, if place were abstract, its being empty of an occupant would be impossible, in the sense of dimension.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage contains objections and clarifications to the previous argument about quantitative motion. First, it notes that the impossibility of motion for the convex of the outermost sphere is not intrinsic to it but due to lack of place beyond; thus, the concave does not necessarily share the impossibility. The concave could increase while the convex of the sphere inside decreases, and vice versa. Second, the argument does not apply to other spheres because they may not be simple like the outermost sphere; they could be composed of different simple entities. An objection about interpenetration and vacuum is answered by denying the entailment, since the adjacent sphere can adjust. The author distinguishes between the view of the philosophers (their view) and the author's own view (likely Ash'ari or more accepting of vacuum). According to the author's view, vacuum is permissible, so the convex of the encompassing sphere can increase and decrease. Finally, a gloss from Sharh al-Isharat is quoted, stating that quantitative motion requires rectilinear motion of particles, which celestial spheres lack because they have circular motion only. The gloss also critiques the author's (al-Jurjani's) argument by noting that the outermost sphere has position, not place in the sense of surface, so its quantitative motion would change its position. It concludes by noting that if place were abstract (like dimension), vacancy would be impossible.

Misconception: The impossibility of vacuum is universally accepted.
Correction: The author's theological school permits vacuum, so the cosmos can expand or contract.

Checkpoint
According to the author's view, what makes increase of the outermost sphere permissible?
A) Its simplicity.
B) The permissibility of vacuum.
C) Its rectilinear motion.
D) The absence of interpenetration.

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of vacuum is key to allowing quantitative change in the outermost sphere; otherwise, a cause argument blocks two bodies occupying the same place.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows that a single assumption—whether vacuum is permissible—completely changes the argument. What deeply held assumption about your life or faith have you never questioned? Are you willing to examine it today, even if it unsettles you?
?
The cause argument is precise: two independent causes cannot produce one effect. Yet in your daily life, do you attribute outcomes to multiple conflicting causes (e.g., both your effort and Allah's will) without carefully distinguishing their roles? How will you correct this in your next decision?
?
The gloss critiques the author's reasoning. When someone points out an error in your understanding of a religious matter, do you accept it humbly or defend your position? Will you commit today to seeking truth over being right?

The Equinoxes: Where the Sun Crosses the Equator
Defining the equinoctial points via circle intersection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the intersection of the celestial equator and ecliptic defines the two equinoctial points and their seasonal significance.

Core Idea
The two equinoxes are where the sun crosses the equator, balancing day and night.

This lesson introduces the fundamental astronomical principle that any two great circles intersect at two opposite points. Applied to the ecliptic (the sun's path) and the celestial equator, this intersection yields the equinoxes—the points where day and night are equal everywhere except at the poles. These points mark the beginning of spring and autumn in most of the inhabited world.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on great circles and their intersection
Observe the general rule about great circles and how it sets up the specific case of the ecliptic and equator.
2
Identify the two equinoctial points
Find how the text defines the vernal and autumnal equinoxes based on the sun's passage north or south of the equator.
3
Note the exception at the poles
The text mentions that at the poles day and night are not equal—this is an important caveat.

First Observatory – Chapter Two – First Section – Second Purpose: On the directions of the body. And likewise, any two great circles assumed in a sphere must intersect each other in halves, as is shown in the [science of] spheres. The intersection between the ecliptic (mintaqat al-buruj) and the celestial equator (mu‘addal al-nahar) occurs at two points common to both, called the equinoctial points (nuqtatay al-i‘tidal) because night and day are equal in all regions of the earth when the sun is at them, except for two places under the poles. The point the sun passes from these two points north of the equator is the vernal equinox (al-i‘tidal al-rabi‘i), because it is the beginning of spring in most of the inhabited world; the point it passes to the south of the equator is the autumnal equinox (al-i‘tidal al-kharifi), because it is its beginning in most of the inhabited world as well. And at the midpoint of the ecliptic between the equinoxes, on each side (north and south), a point is assumed—where the extreme distance between the two circles occurs—called the solstitial points (nuqtatay al-inqilab). The one on the north side of the equator is the summer solstice (inqilab sayfi), because when the sun is at it, the season turns to summer in most inhabited regions; the one on the south side of the equator is the winter solstice (inqilab shitawi), because the season turns to winter in those regions. By these four points—the equinoxes and solstices—the ecliptic is divided into four equal parts, the duration of the sun’s traversal of one of them being a season (fasl) of the four seasons of the year in most of the inhabited world. Then they divided each of the four parts into three equal sections, so the total—i.e., the entire ecliptic—becomes divided into twelve sections. And they imagined six great circles intersecting at the poles of the ecliptic, each passing through the heads of two opposite sections; then between each two sections a semicircle of those circles separates them, so all the sections are surrounded by six circles as you have learned. They named each of the twelve sections a sign (burj). Then they divided each sign into thirty equal parts and called them degrees (daraj). They divided each degree into sixty equal parts and called them minutes (daqa’iq). They divided each minute into sixty equal parts and called them seconds (thawani). Likewise they divided seconds and called them thirds (thawalith), and divided thirds and called them fourths (rawabi‘), and so on for further subdivisions that can be considered. And just as each piece of the ecliptic lying between two semicircles is called a sign, likewise the pieces on the surface of the highest sphere between the semicircles of those circles, in the shape of a melon skin, are called signs. Thus each sign has a length of thirty degrees from west to east and a breadth of one hundred eighty degrees. They took the names of the twelve well-known signs from images they imagined by connecting lines between fixed stars that were aligned with them at the time of naming; those imagined images shift from alignment with the signs due to the slow motion of the fixed stars, but the names remain because the signs are divisions of the ninth sphere, and those images are on the eighth sphere, so they inevitably depart from alignment due to its slow motion, and thus the [ninth] sphere is higher. He said: (except two places, etc.): one is under the North Pole, the other under the South Pole, because the motion of the great sphere there causes the poles to coincide with the zenith and nadir there due to the sun's motion. He said: (in most of the inhabited world): an exclusion of the equator, for they (the equinoxes) are the beginning of summer there. He said: (most of the inhabited world): an exclusion of the equator, for the season turns there to something when the sun is at them – i.e., the places where the season turns to summer. He said: (most of the inhabited world): an exclusion of the equator, for the duration of the sun’s traversal of one of those sections there is two seasons, as we will learn. He said: (the ecliptic imagined on the surface of the highest sphere), as indicated by the context of the author’s words, and it is explicitly stated in Nihayat al-Idrak and Sharh al-Tadhkira. He said: (except two places under the poles), for in these two places night and day are not equal; rather, the sun may not set for months, or may not appear for months, and detailed discussion will come in the investigations of the equator. He said: (the point it passes): the point the sun passes. He said: (and between each two sections a circle separates them): so the halves considered here are twelve halves, from the six great circles mentioned, and between each two halves of these halves is one section, which is called a sign. He said: (each section): i.e., each quarter of the four quarters of the ecliptic imagined on the surface of the highest sphere. He said: (thirty sections): based on the fact that most fractions come out as integers from it, making calculation easy. He said: (degrees): like the sun, it appears and declines from them; the parts of other circles are called parts. He said: (and just as each piece of the ecliptic, etc.): as indicated by the author's words, for he said: they named each section a sign. He said: (likewise the pieces): this is the well-known usage, and two usages are explicitly given in al-Tadhkira. He said: (and its breadth is one hundred eighty degrees): meaning the breadth between the poles; thus the length of each sign is one-sixth of its breadth, six times its length. He said: (they imagined by connecting lines): meaning if we connect lines between the stars in the sphere of the fixed stars, we imagine images there, some in the form of Aries, some in the form of Taurus, etc., as they detailed in their books.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage is an astronomical exposition from the commentary (sharh) on al-Mawaqif, explaining the division of the celestial sphere, specifically the ecliptic, into parts based on the sun's apparent motion. The author begins by stating a general principle: any two great circles in a sphere intersect each other in halves. This is applied to the ecliptic (the sun's apparent annual path) and the celestial equator. Their intersection defines two points: the equinoxes, where day and night are equal except at the poles. The passage then defines the vernal and autumnal equinoxes as the points where the sun crosses the equator northward or southward, marking the start of spring and autumn in most of the inhabited world. Next, the points halfway between the equinoxes on the ecliptic—where the ecliptic and equator are farthest apart—are defined as the solstices: the summer solstice north of the equator and the winter solstice south, where the season turns to summer or winter. These four points (two equinoxes and two solstices) divide the ecliptic into four equal parts, each traversed by the sun in one season of the year. The text then describes the further division of each of these four parts into three equal parts, yielding twelve equal sections of the ecliptic, called the zodiac signs (buruj). To delimit these twelve signs, six great circles are imagined through the ecliptic poles; each such circle passes through the heads of two opposite signs, and the space between consecutive semicircles defines a sign. The passage then explains the subdivision of each sign into 30 degrees, each degree into 60 minutes, each minute into 60 seconds, etc., emphasizing that this scheme allows for easy calculation because many divisions are integral. The physical shape of a sign is described as a band on the highest sphere (the ninth sphere) between two semicircles, with a length of 30 degrees and a breadth of 180 degrees (the full latitude from pole to pole). Finally, the author notes that the names of the twelve signs (Aries, Taurus, etc.) come from images imagined by connecting fixed stars that were once aligned with the signs; the constellations (images) slowly drift due to precession, but the names remain fixed to the divisions of the highest sphere. The glosses (قوله) provide further clarifications: exceptions at the poles, exclusion of the equator, the fact that the ecliptic is imagined on the ninth sphere, and the basis for choosing 30 parts per sign.

معدل النهار
نقطتا الاعتدال
الاعتدال الربيعي
الاعتدال الخريفي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Day and night are equal everywhere on Earth during equinoxes.
Correction: At the poles, day and night are not equal because the sun's path is different.

Checkpoint
According to the text, at which two places are day and night not equal during the equinoxes?
A) The equator
B) The poles
C) The tropics
D) All places are equal

Key Takeaway
The equinoxes are the two points where the sun's path crosses the celestial equator, marking equal day and night everywhere except at the poles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The equinoxes are moments of perfect balance in creation. In what area of your life is there an imbalance—a neglect of your duty towards Allah or towards others—that you have been ignoring? What will you do today to restore that balance?
?
The passage notes that the poles are exceptions where day and night are not equal at the equinox. Think of an 'exception' in your life where you have made excuses for not following Allah's guidance. How does the precision of the heavens call you to abandon that exception and submit fully to His order?
?
The sun's crossing from south to north marks the start of spring—a time of renewal. What spiritual renewal do you need right now? Write one concrete action you will take this week to turn back to Allah and revive your faith.

The Solstices: Points of Maximum Declination
Defining the summer and winter solstices.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the solstices are defined as the points on the ecliptic farthest from the celestial equator, marking the extremes of seasonal change.

Core Idea
The solstices are the sun's highest and lowest points in the sky, causing winter and summer.

After establishing the equinoxes, the text now defines two other critical points: the solstices. These are located midway between the equinoxes on the ecliptic, where the distance between the ecliptic and equator is greatest. They mark the turning points of the seasons—the summer and winter solstices—in most of the inhabited world.

Guided Path
1
Locate the solstices on the ecliptic
The text places them at the midpoint between the equinoxes, where the ecliptic and equator are farthest apart.
2
Understand the seasonal effect
Notice how the summer solstice is associated with the sun being north of the equator, turning the season to summer, and vice versa for winter.

تنقسم منطقة البروج أربعة أقسام متساوية تكون مدة قطع الشمس واحداً منها فصلاً من الفصول الأربعة التي للسنة في معظم المعمورة ثم قسموا كل قسم من الأقسام الأربعة ثلاثة أقسام متساوية فيكون المجموع أي مجموع منطقة البروج منقسماً إلى اثني عشر قسماً وتوهموا ست دوائر عظام تتقاطع على قطبي البروج وتمر كل واحدة منها برأسي قسمين متقابلين من تلك الأقسام وحينئذ يفصل بين كل قسمين منها نصف دائرة من تلك الدوائر فيحيط بها أي بالأقسام كلها ست دوائر كما عرفت وسموا كل قسم من الاثني عشر برجاً ثم قسموا كل برج ثلاثين قسماً سواء وسموها درجاً وقسموا كل درجة ستين قسماً سواء وسموها دقائق وقسموا الدقائق أي كل واحدة منها ستين قسماً متساوية وسموها ثواني وهكذا قسموا الثواني وسموها ثوالث وقسموا الثوالث وسموها روابع فما زاد مما يمكن اعتباره من الكسور وكما أن كل قطعة من منطقة البروج واقعة بين نصفي دائرتين تسمى برجاً كذلك القطع الواقعة من سطح الفلك الأعلى بين أنصاف تلك الدوائر على هيئة جراب البطيخ تسمى بروجاً فعلى هذا يكون طول كل برج فيما بين المغرب والمشرق ثلاثين درجة وعرضه مائة وثمانين درجة وأخذوا أسماء البروج الاثني عشر المشهورة من صور تخيلوها من وصل الخطوط بين كواكب من الثوابت كانت موازية لها حين التسمية وإنها أي تلك الصور المتخيلة تزول عن موازاة البروج بالحركة البطيئة التي للثوابت والأسماء بحالها فإن البروج أقسام للفلك التاسع ولا شك أن تلك الصور على الفلك الثامن فلا بد من خروجها عن الموازاة بحركته البطيئة فكان الأعلى. قوله: (سوی موضعين إلخ) أحدهما تحت القطب الشمالي والآخر تحت القطب الجنوبي فإن حركة الفلك الأعظم فيهما أحوط الانطباق القطبين على سمتي الرأس والقدم فيهما بحركة الشمس. قوله: ( في معظم المعمورة احتراز عن خط الاستواء فإنهما مبد آن للصيف فيه . قوله: (أكثر المعمورة احتراز عن خط الاستواء فإنه ينقلب الزمان فيه شيئاً إذا حلت الشمس فيهما في تلك المواضع أي المواضع التي انقلب الزمان فيهما صيفا. قوله: (معظم المعمورة) احتراز عن خط الاستواء فإن مدة قطع الشمس واحداً من تلك الأقسام فيه فصلان كما سنقف عليه . قوله : (تنقسم منطقة البروج المتوهمة على سطح الفلك الأعلى كما يدل عليه سياق كلام المصنف وهو المصرح به في نهاية الإدراك وشرح التذكرة . قوله سوى موضعين هما تحت القطبين فإن هذين الموضعين لا يستوي الليل والنهار بل قد يكون الشمس بحيث لا تغيب شهوراً وقد تكون بحيث لا تظهر شهوراً وسيجيء تفصيل الكلام في مباحث خط الاستواء وقوله : فيما يتجاوزها فالنقطة التي تتجاوزها الشمس. قوله: (ويفصل بين كل قسمين (دائرة فيكون هذا الأنصاف المعتبرة هاهنا اثني عشر نصفاً . من دوائر الست العظام المذكورة وما بين كل نصفين من هذه الأنصاف قسم واحد وهو المسمى بالبروج. قوله : ( كل قسم إلخ) أي كل ربع من أرباع منطقة البروج المتوهمة على سطح الفلك الأعلى . قوله : ( ثلاثين قسماً) بناءً على أن أكثر الكسور يخرج منه صحيحاً فيسهل الحساب قوله : (درجاً) كالشمس يظهر فيها ويهبط وأجزاء سائر الدوائر تسمى أجزاء . قوله : ( وكما أن كل قطعة من منطقة إلخ) كما يدل عليه كلام المصنف . فإنه قال : سموا كل قسم برجاً . قوله : ( كذلك القطع (الواقعة) هذا هو الإطلاق المشهور وقد صرح بإطلاقين في التذكرة . قوله : ( وعرضه مائة وثمانون (درجة يعني أن عرض ما بين القطبين، فعلى هذا يكون طول کل برج سدس عرضه ستة أمثال طوله . قوله تخيلوها من وصل (الخطوط يعني إذا وصلنا الخطوط بين الكواكب في فلك الثوابت كنا نتخيل هناك صوراً يكون بعضها في صورة الحمل وبعضها في صورة الثور إلى غير ذلك على ما فصلوا في كتبهم.
First Observatory – Chapter Two – First Section – Second Purpose: On the directions of the body. And likewise, any two great circles assumed in a sphere must intersect each other in halves, as is shown in the [science of] spheres. The intersection between the ecliptic (mintaqat al-buruj) and the celestial equator (mu‘addal al-nahar) occurs at two points common to both, called the equinoctial points (nuqtatay al-i‘tidal) because night and day are equal in all regions of the earth when the sun is at them, except for two places under the poles. The point the sun passes from these two points north of the equator is the vernal equinox (al-i‘tidal al-rabi‘i), because it is the beginning of spring in most of the inhabited world; the point it passes to the south of the equator is the autumnal equinox (al-i‘tidal al-kharifi), because it is its beginning in most of the inhabited world as well. And at the midpoint of the ecliptic between the equinoxes, on each side (north and south), a point is assumed—where the extreme distance between the two circles occurs—called the solstitial points (nuqtatay al-inqilab). The one on the north side of the equator is the summer solstice (inqilab sayfi), because when the sun is at it, the season turns to summer in most inhabited regions; the one on the south side of the equator is the winter solstice (inqilab shitawi), because the season turns to winter in those regions. By these four points—the equinoxes and solstices—the ecliptic is divided into four equal parts, the duration of the sun’s traversal of one of them being a season (fasl) of the four seasons of the year in most of the inhabited world. Then they divided each of the four parts into three equal sections, so the total—i.e., the entire ecliptic—becomes divided into twelve sections. And they imagined six great circles intersecting at the poles of the ecliptic, each passing through the heads of two opposite sections; then between each two sections a semicircle of those circles separates them, so all the sections are surrounded by six circles as you have learned. They named each of the twelve sections a sign (burj). Then they divided each sign into thirty equal parts and called them degrees (daraj). They divided each degree into sixty equal parts and called them minutes (daqa’iq). They divided each minute into sixty equal parts and called them seconds (thawani). Likewise they divided seconds and called them thirds (thawalith), and divided thirds and called them fourths (rawabi‘), and so on for further subdivisions that can be considered. And just as each piece of the ecliptic lying between two semicircles is called a sign, likewise the pieces on the surface of the highest sphere between the semicircles of those circles, in the shape of a melon skin, are called signs. Thus each sign has a length of thirty degrees from west to east and a breadth of one hundred eighty degrees. They took the names of the twelve well-known signs from images they imagined by connecting lines between fixed stars that were aligned with them at the time of naming; those imagined images shift from alignment with the signs due to the slow motion of the fixed stars, but the names remain because the signs are divisions of the ninth sphere, and those images are on the eighth sphere, so they inevitably depart from alignment due to its slow motion, and thus the [ninth] sphere is higher. He said: (except two places, etc.): one is under the North Pole, the other under the South Pole, because the motion of the great sphere there causes the poles to coincide with the zenith and nadir there due to the sun's motion. He said: (in most of the inhabited world): an exclusion of the equator, for they (the equinoxes) are the beginning of summer there. He said: (most of the inhabited world): an exclusion of the equator, for the season turns there to something when the sun is at them – i.e., the places where the season turns to summer. He said: (most of the inhabited world): an exclusion of the equator, for the duration of the sun’s traversal of one of those sections there is two seasons, as we will learn. He said: (the ecliptic imagined on the surface of the highest sphere), as indicated by the context of the author’s words, and it is explicitly stated in Nihayat al-Idrak and Sharh al-Tadhkira. He said: (except two places under the poles), for in these two places night and day are not equal; rather, the sun may not set for months, or may not appear for months, and detailed discussion will come in the investigations of the equator. He said: (the point it passes): the point the sun passes. He said: (and between each two sections a circle separates them): so the halves considered here are twelve halves, from the six great circles mentioned, and between each two halves of these halves is one section, which is called a sign. He said: (each section): i.e., each quarter of the four quarters of the ecliptic imagined on the surface of the highest sphere. He said: (thirty sections): based on the fact that most fractions come out as integers from it, making calculation easy. He said: (degrees): like the sun, it appears and declines from them; the parts of other circles are called parts. He said: (and just as each piece of the ecliptic, etc.): as indicated by the author's words, for he said: they named each section a sign. He said: (likewise the pieces): this is the well-known usage, and two usages are explicitly given in al-Tadhkira. He said: (and its breadth is one hundred eighty degrees): meaning the breadth between the poles; thus the length of each sign is one-sixth of its breadth, six times its length. He said: (they imagined by connecting lines): meaning if we connect lines between the stars in the sphere of the fixed stars, we imagine images there, some in the form of Aries, some in the form of Taurus, etc., as they detailed in their books.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage is an astronomical exposition from the commentary (sharh) on al-Mawaqif, explaining the division of the celestial sphere, specifically the ecliptic, into parts based on the sun's apparent motion. The author begins by stating a general principle: any two great circles in a sphere intersect each other in halves. This is applied to the ecliptic (the sun's apparent annual path) and the celestial equator. Their intersection defines two points: the equinoxes, where day and night are equal except at the poles. The passage then defines the vernal and autumnal equinoxes as the points where the sun crosses the equator northward or southward, marking the start of spring and autumn in most of the inhabited world. Next, the points halfway between the equinoxes on the ecliptic—where the ecliptic and equator are farthest apart—are defined as the solstices: the summer solstice north of the equator and the winter solstice south, where the season turns to summer or winter. These four points (two equinoxes and two solstices) divide the ecliptic into four equal parts, each traversed by the sun in one season of the year. The text then describes the further division of each of these four parts into three equal parts, yielding twelve equal sections of the ecliptic, called the zodiac signs (buruj). To delimit these twelve signs, six great circles are imagined through the ecliptic poles; each such circle passes through the heads of two opposite signs, and the space between consecutive semicircles defines a sign. The passage then explains the subdivision of each sign into 30 degrees, each degree into 60 minutes, each minute into 60 seconds, etc., emphasizing that this scheme allows for easy calculation because many divisions are integral. The physical shape of a sign is described as a band on the highest sphere (the ninth sphere) between two semicircles, with a length of 30 degrees and a breadth of 180 degrees (the full latitude from pole to pole). Finally, the author notes that the names of the twelve signs (Aries, Taurus, etc.) come from images imagined by connecting fixed stars that were once aligned with the signs; the constellations (images) slowly drift due to precession, but the names remain fixed to the divisions of the highest sphere. The glosses (قوله) provide further clarifications: exceptions at the poles, exclusion of the equator, the fact that the ecliptic is imagined on the ninth sphere, and the basis for choosing 30 parts per sign.

Key Terms
نقطتا الانقلاب
انقلاب صيفي
انقلاب شتوي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Solstices occur when the sun crosses the equator.
Correction: Solstices occur when the sun is at its maximum declination, not when crossing the equator (that's equinox).

Checkpoint
What happens when the sun reaches the summer solstice according to the text?
A) Day and night become equal
B) The season turns to winter
C) The season turns to summer in most inhabited regions
D) The sun crosses the equator

Key Takeaway
The solstices are the points on the ecliptic farthest from the celestial equator, marking the beginning of summer and winter.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The word 'inqilab' means a turning or reversal. When was the last time your life experienced a dramatic turning point that you can now see was for a better direction, like the seasons turning from winter to summer?
?
The sun's path is fixed and precise — its farthest points are known and ordered. Are there areas in your life where you've drifted from the path of righteousness, and how can you 'turn' back like the solstice turns the season?
?
If the solstice marks the extreme distance from the equator, consider the extremes of your own spiritual state: Are you in a spiritual 'summer' or 'winter'? What would it take for you to begin the slow return toward balance (equinox)?

intermediate
7 min
From Seasons to Zodiac Signs
Dividing the ecliptic into twelve zodiac signs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the four seasons are divided into twelve zodiac signs using six great circles.

Core Idea
The zodiac is a twelve-part division of the ecliptic, each part corresponding to a month of the solar year.

Having identified the four cardinal points (equinoxes and solstices), the text now shows how these divide the ecliptic into four seasons of equal length. Each season is further divided into three parts, yielding twelve sections that correspond to the signs of the zodiac. To delimit these sections, six great circles are imagined through the poles of the ecliptic.

Guided Path
1
From four seasons to twelve sections
The text first divides the ecliptic into four quarters (seasons), then each quarter into three, making twelve equal sections.
2
The six bounding circles
Imagine six great circles through the poles of the ecliptic, each passing through two opposite section points. These circles create the boundaries of the twelve signs.
3
Naming the sections as signs
Each of the twelve sections is called a burj (sign), the zodiacal designations.

First Observatory – Chapter Two – First Section – Second Purpose: On the directions of the body. And likewise, any two great circles assumed in a sphere must intersect each other in halves, as is shown in the [science of] spheres. The intersection between the ecliptic (mintaqat al-buruj) and the celestial equator (mu‘addal al-nahar) occurs at two points common to both, called the equinoctial points (nuqtatay al-i‘tidal) because night and day are equal in all regions of the earth when the sun is at them, except for two places under the poles. The point the sun passes from these two points north of the equator is the vernal equinox (al-i‘tidal al-rabi‘i), because it is the beginning of spring in most of the inhabited world; the point it passes to the south of the equator is the autumnal equinox (al-i‘tidal al-kharifi), because it is its beginning in most of the inhabited world as well. And at the midpoint of the ecliptic between the equinoxes, on each side (north and south), a point is assumed—where the extreme distance between the two circles occurs—called the solstitial points (nuqtatay al-inqilab). The one on the north side of the equator is the summer solstice (inqilab sayfi), because when the sun is at it, the season turns to summer in most inhabited regions; the one on the south side of the equator is the winter solstice (inqilab shitawi), because the season turns to winter in those regions. By these four points—the equinoxes and solstices—the ecliptic is divided into four equal parts, the duration of the sun’s traversal of one of them being a season (fasl) of the four seasons of the year in most of the inhabited world. Then they divided each of the four parts into three equal sections, so the total—i.e., the entire ecliptic—becomes divided into twelve sections. And they imagined six great circles intersecting at the poles of the ecliptic, each passing through the heads of two opposite sections; then between each two sections a semicircle of those circles separates them, so all the sections are surrounded by six circles as you have learned. They named each of the twelve sections a sign (burj). Then they divided each sign into thirty equal parts and called them degrees (daraj). They divided each degree into sixty equal parts and called them minutes (daqa’iq). They divided each minute into sixty equal parts and called them seconds (thawani). Likewise they divided seconds and called them thirds (thawalith), and divided thirds and called them fourths (rawabi‘), and so on for further subdivisions that can be considered. And just as each piece of the ecliptic lying between two semicircles is called a sign, likewise the pieces on the surface of the highest sphere between the semicircles of those circles, in the shape of a melon skin, are called signs. Thus each sign has a length of thirty degrees from west to east and a breadth of one hundred eighty degrees. They took the names of the twelve well-known signs from images they imagined by connecting lines between fixed stars that were aligned with them at the time of naming; those imagined images shift from alignment with the signs due to the slow motion of the fixed stars, but the names remain because the signs are divisions of the ninth sphere, and those images are on the eighth sphere, so they inevitably depart from alignment due to its slow motion, and thus the [ninth] sphere is higher. He said: (except two places, etc.): one is under the North Pole, the other under the South Pole, because the motion of the great sphere there causes the poles to coincide with the zenith and nadir there due to the sun's motion. He said: (in most of the inhabited world): an exclusion of the equator, for they (the equinoxes) are the beginning of summer there. He said: (most of the inhabited world): an exclusion of the equator, for the season turns there to something when the sun is at them – i.e., the places where the season turns to summer. He said: (most of the inhabited world): an exclusion of the equator, for the duration of the sun’s traversal of one of those sections there is two seasons, as we will learn. He said: (the ecliptic imagined on the surface of the highest sphere), as indicated by the context of the author’s words, and it is explicitly stated in Nihayat al-Idrak and Sharh al-Tadhkira. He said: (except two places under the poles), for in these two places night and day are not equal; rather, the sun may not set for months, or may not appear for months, and detailed discussion will come in the investigations of the equator. He said: (the point it passes): the point the sun passes. He said: (and between each two sections a circle separates them): so the halves considered here are twelve halves, from the six great circles mentioned, and between each two halves of these halves is one section, which is called a sign. He said: (each section): i.e., each quarter of the four quarters of the ecliptic imagined on the surface of the highest sphere. He said: (thirty sections): based on the fact that most fractions come out as integers from it, making calculation easy. He said: (degrees): like the sun, it appears and declines from them; the parts of other circles are called parts. He said: (and just as each piece of the ecliptic, etc.): as indicated by the author's words, for he said: they named each section a sign. He said: (likewise the pieces): this is the well-known usage, and two usages are explicitly given in al-Tadhkira. He said: (and its breadth is one hundred eighty degrees): meaning the breadth between the poles; thus the length of each sign is one-sixth of its breadth, six times its length. He said: (they imagined by connecting lines): meaning if we connect lines between the stars in the sphere of the fixed stars, we imagine images there, some in the form of Aries, some in the form of Taurus, etc., as they detailed in their books.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage is an astronomical exposition from the commentary (sharh) on al-Mawaqif, explaining the division of the celestial sphere, specifically the ecliptic, into parts based on the sun's apparent motion. The author begins by stating a general principle: any two great circles in a sphere intersect each other in halves. This is applied to the ecliptic (the sun's apparent annual path) and the celestial equator. Their intersection defines two points: the equinoxes, where day and night are equal except at the poles. The passage then defines the vernal and autumnal equinoxes as the points where the sun crosses the equator northward or southward, marking the start of spring and autumn in most of the inhabited world. Next, the points halfway between the equinoxes on the ecliptic—where the ecliptic and equator are farthest apart—are defined as the solstices: the summer solstice north of the equator and the winter solstice south, where the season turns to summer or winter. These four points (two equinoxes and two solstices) divide the ecliptic into four equal parts, each traversed by the sun in one season of the year. The text then describes the further division of each of these four parts into three equal parts, yielding twelve equal sections of the ecliptic, called the zodiac signs (buruj). To delimit these twelve signs, six great circles are imagined through the ecliptic poles; each such circle passes through the heads of two opposite signs, and the space between consecutive semicircles defines a sign. The passage then explains the subdivision of each sign into 30 degrees, each degree into 60 minutes, each minute into 60 seconds, etc., emphasizing that this scheme allows for easy calculation because many divisions are integral. The physical shape of a sign is described as a band on the highest sphere (the ninth sphere) between two semicircles, with a length of 30 degrees and a breadth of 180 degrees (the full latitude from pole to pole). Finally, the author notes that the names of the twelve signs (Aries, Taurus, etc.) come from images imagined by connecting fixed stars that were once aligned with the signs; the constellations (images) slowly drift due to precession, but the names remain fixed to the divisions of the highest sphere. The glosses (قوله) provide further clarifications: exceptions at the poles, exclusion of the equator, the fact that the ecliptic is imagined on the ninth sphere, and the basis for choosing 30 parts per sign.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Subdivisions of the Zodiac: Degrees, Minutes, and Shape
Describing the division of signs into degrees and smaller units, and the sign's geometry.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sexagesimal subdivision of each zodiac sign into degrees and smaller units, and the physical shape of a sign on the highest sphere.

Core Idea
Each zodiac sign is 30 degrees long, and degrees are subdivided into 60 minutes, 60 seconds, etc.

After defining the twelve zodiac signs, the text explains how they are further divided for precise measurement. Each sign is split into 30 degrees, each degree into 60 minutes, and so on, following a sexagesimal system. The text also describes the physical representation of a sign as a band on the ninth sphere, with a length of 30 degrees and a breadth spanning from pole to pole (180 degrees).

Guided Path
1
Understand the sexagesimal subdivisions
Note the hierarchy: sign → degree (30 parts), degree → minute (60 parts), minute → second (60), etc. This system makes calculations easier as many fractions are whole numbers.
2
Visualize the shape of a sign
A sign is not just a line but a region on the celestial sphere, shaped like a melon slice, with length 30° along the ecliptic and breadth 180° from pole to pole.

First Observatory – Chapter Two – First Section – Second Purpose: On the directions of the body. The intersection between the ecliptic (mintaqat al-buruj) and the celestial equator (mu‘addal al-nahar) occurs at two points common to both, called the equinoctial points (nuqtatay al-i‘tidal) because night and day are equal in all regions of the earth when the sun is at them, except for two places under the poles. The author begins by stating a general principle: any two great circles in a sphere intersect each other in halves. Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Each zodiac sign is only a 30° arc along the ecliptic.
Correction: Each sign also has a breadth of 180° from pole to pole, forming a band on the sphere.

intermediate

Sign Names and the Precession of the Equinoxes
Explaining the origin of zodiac sign names and their drift from constellations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the zodiac signs are fixed divisions of the ninth sphere, while the named constellations in the eighth sphere slowly drift due to precession.

Core Idea
The zodiac signs are fixed mathematical divisions; the constellations are drifting images on a lower sphere.

This final passage addresses the origin of the names of the zodiac signs. The names were taken from constellations of fixed stars that once aligned with the signs. However, because the fixed stars slowly shift (precession of the equinoxes), the constellations no longer match the signs. The signs remain fixed because they are part of the ninth (primum mobile) sphere, while the constellations are on the eighth sphere.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the two different spheres
The text distinguishes between the ninth sphere (where the signs are fixed) and the eighth sphere (where the star patterns are). The fixed stars slowly move.
2
Understand the naming convention
The names were originally based on constellations that coincided with the signs, but over time they drifted apart. The names remain assigned to the fixed signs.
