المرصد الأول - الفصل الثاني : القسم الأول : المقصد الثاني : في اتجاهات الجسم. وكذلك كل دائرتتين عظيمتين تفرضان في كرة فإنه يجب تقاطعهما على التناصف لما بين في الأكر والتقاطع بين منطقة البروج ومعدل النهار يكون على نقطتين مشتركتين بينهما وتسميان نقطتي الاعتدال لاستواء الليل والنهار في جميع نواحي الأرض إذا حلت الشمس فيهما سوى موضعين هما تحت القطبين فما تتجاوزه الشمس من هاتين النقطتين إلى الشمال من المعدل هو الاعتدال الربيعي لأنه مبدأ الربيع في معظم المعمورة وما تتجاوزه إلى الجنوب من المعدل هو الاعتدال الخريفي لأنه مبدؤه في معظم المعمورة أيضاً ويفرض على منتصفها أي منتصف منطقة البروج فيما بين الاعتدالين في كل جانب من الشمال والجنوب نقطة وهي حيث تكون غاية البعد بين المنطقتين تسميان أي هاتان النقطتان المفروضتان على المنتصفين نقطتي الانقلابين فالتي في طرف الشمال من المعدل هي انقلاب الصيفي لأن الشمس إذا حلت فيها انقلب الزمان صيفاً في أكثر المواضع المعمورة والتي في طرف الجنوب من المعدل هي الانقلاب الشتوي لانقلاب الزمان إلى الشتاء في تلك المواضع وبهذه النقط الأربع أعني الاعتدالين والانقلابين تنقسم منطقة البروج أربعة أقسام متساوية تكون مدة قطع الشمس واحداً منها فصلاً من الفصول الأربعة التي للسنة في معظم المعمورة ثم قسموا كل قسم من الأقسام الأربعة ثلاثة أقسام متساوية فيكون المجموع أي مجموع منطقة البروج منقسماً إلى اثني عشر قسماً وتوهموا ست دوائر عظام تتقاطع على قطبي البروج وتمر كل واحدة منها برأسي قسمين متقابلين من تلك الأقسام وحينئذ يفصل بين كل قسمين منها نصف دائرة من تلك الدوائر فيحيط بها أي بالأقسام كلها ست دوائر كما عرفت وسموا كل قسم من الاثني عشر برجاً ثم قسموا كل برج ثلاثين قسماً سواء وسموها درجاً وقسموا كل درجة ستين قسماً سواء وسموها دقائق وقسموا الدقائق أي كل واحدة منها ستين قسماً متساوية وسموها ثواني وهكذا قسموا الثواني وسموها ثوالث وقسموا الثوالث وسموها روابع فما زاد مما يمكن اعتباره من الكسور وكما أن كل قطعة من منطقة البروج واقعة بين نصفي دائرتين تسمى برجاً كذلك القطع الواقعة من سطح الفلك الأعلى بين أنصاف تلك الدوائر على هيئة جراب البطيخ تسمى بروجاً فعلى هذا يكون طول كل برج فيما بين المغرب والمشرق ثلاثين درجة وعرضه مائة وثمانين درجة وأخذوا أسماء البروج الاثني عشر المشهورة من صور تخيلوها من وصل الخطوط بين كواكب من الثوابت كانت موازية لها حين التسمية وإنها أي تلك الصور المتخيلة تزول عن موازاة البروج بالحركة البطيئة التي للثوابت والأسماء بحالها فإن البروج أقسام للفلك التاسع ولا شك أن تلك الصور على الفلك الثامن فلا بد من خروجها عن الموازاة بحركته البطيئة فكان الأعلى. قوله: (سوی موضعين إلخ) أحدهما تحت القطب الشمالي والآخر تحت القطب الجنوبي فإن حركة الفلك الأعظم فيهما أحوط الانطباق القطبين على سمتي الرأس والقدم فيهما بحركة الشمس. قوله: ( في معظم المعمورة احتراز عن خط الاستواء فإنهما مبد آن للصيف فيه . قوله: (أكثر المعمورة احتراز عن خط الاستواء فإنه ينقلب الزمان فيه شيئاً إذا حلت الشمس فيهما في تلك المواضع أي المواضع التي انقلب الزمان فيهما صيفا. قوله: (معظم المعمورة) احتراز عن خط الاستواء فإن مدة قطع الشمس واحداً من تلك الأقسام فيه فصلان كما سنقف عليه . قوله : (تنقسم منطقة البروج المتوهمة على سطح الفلك الأعلى كما يدل عليه سياق كلام المصنف وهو المصرح به في نهاية الإدراك وشرح التذكرة . قوله سوى موضعين هما تحت القطبين فإن هذين الموضعين لا يستوي الليل والنهار بل قد يكون الشمس بحيث لا تغيب شهوراً وقد تكون بحيث لا تظهر شهوراً وسيجيء تفصيل الكلام في مباحث خط الاستواء وقوله : فيما يتجاوزها فالنقطة التي تتجاوزها الشمس. قوله: (ويفصل بين كل قسمين (دائرة فيكون هذا الأنصاف المعتبرة هاهنا اثني عشر نصفاً . من دوائر الست العظام المذكورة وما بين كل نصفين من هذه الأنصاف قسم واحد وهو المسمى بالبروج. قوله : ( كل قسم إلخ) أي كل ربع من أرباع منطقة البروج المتوهمة على سطح الفلك الأعلى . قوله : ( ثلاثين قسماً) بناءً على أن أكثر الكسور يخرج منه صحيحاً فيسهل الحساب قوله : (درجاً) كالشمس يظهر فيها ويهبط وأجزاء سائر الدوائر تسمى أجزاء . قوله : ( وكما أن كل قطعة من منطقة إلخ) كما يدل عليه كلام المصنف . فإنه قال : سموا كل قسم برجاً . قوله : ( كذلك القطع (الواقعة) هذا هو الإطلاق المشهور وقد صرح بإطلاقين في التذكرة . قوله : ( وعرضه مائة وثمانون (درجة يعني أن عرض ما بين القطبين، فعلى هذا يكون طول کل برج سدس عرضه ستة أمثال طوله . قوله تخيلوها من وصل (الخطوط يعني إذا وصلنا الخطوط بين الكواكب في فلك الثوابت كنا نتخيل هناك صوراً يكون بعضها في صورة الحمل وبعضها في صورة الثور إلى غير ذلك على ما فصلوا في كتبهم.
First Observatory – Chapter Two – First Section – Second Purpose: On the directions of the body. And likewise, any two great circles assumed in a sphere must intersect each other in halves, as is shown in the [science of] spheres. The intersection between the ecliptic (mintaqat al-buruj) and the celestial equator (mu‘addal al-nahar) occurs at two points common to both, called the equinoctial points (nuqtatay al-i‘tidal) because night and day are equal in all regions of the earth when the sun is at them, except for two places under the poles. The point the sun passes from these two points north of the equator is the vernal equinox (al-i‘tidal al-rabi‘i), because it is the beginning of spring in most of the inhabited world; the point it passes to the south of the equator is the autumnal equinox (al-i‘tidal al-kharifi), because it is its beginning in most of the inhabited world as well. And at the midpoint of the ecliptic between the equinoxes, on each side (north and south), a point is assumed—where the extreme distance between the two circles occurs—called the solstitial points (nuqtatay al-inqilab). The one on the north side of the equator is the summer solstice (inqilab sayfi), because when the sun is at it, the season turns to summer in most inhabited regions; the one on the south side of the equator is the winter solstice (inqilab shitawi), because the season turns to winter in those regions. By these four points—the equinoxes and solstices—the ecliptic is divided into four equal parts, the duration of the sun’s traversal of one of them being a season (fasl) of the four seasons of the year in most of the inhabited world. Then they divided each of the four parts into three equal sections, so the total—i.e., the entire ecliptic—becomes divided into twelve sections. And they imagined six great circles intersecting at the poles of the ecliptic, each passing through the heads of two opposite sections; then between each two sections a semicircle of those circles separates them, so all the sections are surrounded by six circles as you have learned. They named each of the twelve sections a sign (burj). Then they divided each sign into thirty equal parts and called them degrees (daraj). They divided each degree into sixty equal parts and called them minutes (daqa’iq). They divided each minute into sixty equal parts and called them seconds (thawani). Likewise they divided seconds and called them thirds (thawalith), and divided thirds and called them fourths (rawabi‘), and so on for further subdivisions that can be considered. And just as each piece of the ecliptic lying between two semicircles is called a sign, likewise the pieces on the surface of the highest sphere between the semicircles of those circles, in the shape of a melon skin, are called signs. Thus each sign has a length of thirty degrees from west to east and a breadth of one hundred eighty degrees. They took the names of the twelve well-known signs from images they imagined by connecting lines between fixed stars that were aligned with them at the time of naming; those imagined images shift from alignment with the signs due to the slow motion of the fixed stars, but the names remain because the signs are divisions of the ninth sphere, and those images are on the eighth sphere, so they inevitably depart from alignment due to its slow motion, and thus the [ninth] sphere is higher. He said: (except two places, etc.): one is under the North Pole, the other under the South Pole, because the motion of the great sphere there causes the poles to coincide with the zenith and nadir there due to the sun's motion. He said: (in most of the inhabited world): an exclusion of the equator, for they (the equinoxes) are the beginning of summer there. He said: (most of the inhabited world): an exclusion of the equator, for the season turns there to something when the sun is at them – i.e., the places where the season turns to summer. He said: (most of the inhabited world): an exclusion of the equator, for the duration of the sun’s traversal of one of those sections there is two seasons, as we will learn. He said: (the ecliptic imagined on the surface of the highest sphere), as indicated by the context of the author’s words, and it is explicitly stated in Nihayat al-Idrak and Sharh al-Tadhkira. He said: (except two places under the poles), for in these two places night and day are not equal; rather, the sun may not set for months, or may not appear for months, and detailed discussion will come in the investigations of the equator. He said: (the point it passes): the point the sun passes. He said: (and between each two sections a circle separates them): so the halves considered here are twelve halves, from the six great circles mentioned, and between each two halves of these halves is one section, which is called a sign. He said: (each section): i.e., each quarter of the four quarters of the ecliptic imagined on the surface of the highest sphere. He said: (thirty sections): based on the fact that most fractions come out as integers from it, making calculation easy. He said: (degrees): like the sun, it appears and declines from them; the parts of other circles are called parts. He said: (and just as each piece of the ecliptic, etc.): as indicated by the author's words, for he said: they named each section a sign. He said: (likewise the pieces): this is the well-known usage, and two usages are explicitly given in al-Tadhkira. He said: (and its breadth is one hundred eighty degrees): meaning the breadth between the poles; thus the length of each sign is one-sixth of its breadth, six times its length. He said: (they imagined by connecting lines): meaning if we connect lines between the stars in the sphere of the fixed stars, we imagine images there, some in the form of Aries, some in the form of Taurus, etc., as they detailed in their books.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage is an astronomical exposition from the commentary (sharh) on al-Mawaqif, explaining the division of the celestial sphere, specifically the ecliptic, into parts based on the sun's apparent motion. The author begins by stating a general principle: any two great circles in a sphere intersect each other in halves. This is applied to the ecliptic (the sun's apparent annual path) and the celestial equator. Their intersection defines two points: the equinoxes, where day and night are equal except at the poles. The passage then defines the vernal and autumnal equinoxes as the points where the sun crosses the equator northward or southward, marking the start of spring and autumn in most of the inhabited world. Next, the points halfway between the equinoxes on the ecliptic—where the ecliptic and equator are farthest apart—are defined as the solstices: the summer solstice north of the equator and the winter solstice south, where the season turns to summer or winter. These four points (two equinoxes and two solstices) divide the ecliptic into four equal parts, each traversed by the sun in one season of the year. The text then describes the further division of each of these four parts into three equal parts, yielding twelve equal sections of the ecliptic, called the zodiac signs (buruj). To delimit these twelve signs, six great circles are imagined through the ecliptic poles; each such circle passes through the heads of two opposite signs, and the space between consecutive semicircles defines a sign. The passage then explains the subdivision of each sign into 30 degrees, each degree into 60 minutes, each minute into 60 seconds, etc., emphasizing that this scheme allows for easy calculation because many divisions are integral. The physical shape of a sign is described as a band on the highest sphere (the ninth sphere) between two semicircles, with a length of 30 degrees and a breadth of 180 degrees (the full latitude from pole to pole). Finally, the author notes that the names of the twelve signs (Aries, Taurus, etc.) come from images imagined by connecting fixed stars that were once aligned with the signs; the constellations (images) slowly drift due to precession, but the names remain fixed to the divisions of the highest sphere. The glosses (قوله) provide further clarifications: exceptions at the poles, exclusion of the equator, the fact that the ecliptic is imagined on the ninth sphere, and the basis for choosing 30 parts per sign.

حركة بطيئة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The zodiac signs are identical to the constellations of the same name, and they remain aligned.
Correction: The signs are fixed divisions of the ninth sphere; the constellations are moving images on the eighth sphere that slowly drift due to precession.

Checkpoint
Why do the zodiac signs keep their names even though the constellations have moved?
A) The constellations are fixed on the ninth sphere
B) The signs are fixed divisions of the ninth sphere, independent of the constellations
C) The sun's path changes
D) The names were changed to match the current constellations

Key Takeaway
The zodiac signs are fixed divisions of the ninth sphere; the constellations of the same name have drifted due to precession.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The constellations drift slowly and imperceptibly, yet their names remain fixed to the unmoving signs. In your own life, what names or identities do you cling to that may have drifted away from their original truth? Will you have the courage to realign yourself?
?
The ancient astronomers connected dots to form pictures in the sky. Are you projecting your own assumptions onto the world to create a comfortable narrative? What would it take to see the stars as they truly are—without your constructed images?
?
The slow motion of the stars is inevitable, but most people never notice it. What slow, creeping changes in your character have you ignored? Today, commit to one course correction before the drift becomes permanent.

Classification of Zodiac Signs and the Order of al-Tawālī
Explains the division of zodiac signs into seasons and defines the order of al-Tawālī.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the zodiac signs are grouped by seasons and the definition of the term al-Tawālī (west-to-east order) and its opposite, Khilāf al-Tawālī.

Core Idea
The zodiac signs follow a west-to-east order (al-Tawālī) matching planetary motions, not daily east-to-west motion.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif explains the classification of the zodiac signs into four seasonal groups and introduces the key astronomical terms al-Tawālī and Khilāf al-Tawālī. Understanding this foundation helps in grasping how celestial motions are tracked and regulated.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze, then read the translation. Notice the seasonal groupings and the technical terms introduced.
2
Identify the four seasonal groups
The passage lists three signs for each season: spring, summer, autumn, winter. Note the equinox and solstice boundaries.
The cycle starts at the vernal equinox because of its connection to growth.
3
Understand the names of the order
The passage defines two terms: al-Tawālī (west-to-east) and Khilāf al-Tawālī (east-to-west). Note that al-Tawālī matches planetary proper motions.
This contrasts with the daily apparent motion from east to west.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وابتدؤوا في اعتبار البروج وافتتاح الدور بما يلي الاعتدال الربيعي من جانب الشمال لأن الشمس إذا وصلت إلى هذا الاعتدال ظهر في المركبات من أنواع النباتات نشو ونماء وبدا فيها مبادئ الثمار فهو أولى بالاعتبار إلى أن يتم الدور بما يليه من جانب الجنوب فصارت ثلاثة منها بين نقطتي الاعتدال الربيعي والانقلاب الصيفي هي الحمل والثور والجوزاء وتسمى بروجاً ربيعية لأن الربيع في معظم المعمورة عبارة عن زمان كون الشمس فيها وثلاثة منها بين الانقلاب الصيفي والاعتدال الخريفي هي السرطان والأسد والسنبلة وتسمى بروجاً صيفية لمثل ما وثلاثة منها بين الاعتدال الخريفي والانقلاب الشتوي هي الميزان والعقرب والقوس وتسمى بروجاً خريفية وثلاثة منها بين الانقلاب الشتوي والاعتدال الربيعي هي الجدي والدلو والحوت وتسمى بروجاً شتوية وهذا الترتيب الذي ذكرناه فيما بين البروج يسمى التوالي وهو من المغرب إلى المشرق وإنما اعتبروه كذلك إذ المقصود ضبط حركات الكواكب أعني حركاتها الخاصة وهي من المغرب إلى المشرق وعكسه يسمى خلاف التوالي وهو من المشرق إلى المغرب
And they began considering the signs (al-burūj) and opening the cycle with what follows the vernal equinox from the northern side, because when the sun reaches this equinox, growth and development appear in compounds from the types of plants, and the beginnings of fruits appear in them, so it is more worthy of consideration until the cycle is completed with what follows it from the southern side. So three of them (i.e., of the signs) between the two points of the vernal equinox and the summer solstice are Aries, Taurus, and Gemini, and they are called spring signs because spring in most of the inhabited world is the time when the sun is in them. And three of them between the summer solstice and the autumnal equinox are Cancer, Leo, and Virgo, and they are called summer signs similarly. And three of them between the autumnal equinox and the winter solstice are Libra, Scorpio, and Sagittarius, and they are called autumn signs. And three of them between the winter solstice and the vernal equinox are Capricorn, Aquarius, and Pisces, and they are called winter signs. This arrangement which we have mentioned among the signs is called al-Tawālī (the order of succession), and it is from west to east. They considered it thus only because the purpose is to regulate the motions of the planets, I mean their proper motions, which are from west to east. And its opposite is called Khilāf al-Tawālī (the opposite of succession), and it is from east to west.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification. It begins by stating that the cycle of zodiac signs starts from the vernal equinox moving northward because the sun's arrival there brings growth. Then it lists the three signs for each season: spring (Aries, Taurus, Gemini), summer (Cancer, Leo, Virgo), autumn (Libra, Scorpio, Sagittarius), winter (Capricorn, Aquarius, Pisces). Then it introduces the technical term 'al-Tawālī' for the order from west to east, which corresponds to the planets' proper motions, and 'Khilāf al-Tawālī' for the opposite direction from east to west. The passage emphasizes that the signs are ordered to regulate planetary motions.

توالي
خلاف التوالي
اعتدال ربيعي
انقلاب صيفي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the zodiac order is from east to west due to daily motion.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the order of signs (al-Tawālī) is from west to east, corresponding to planetary proper motions.

Checkpoint
What is the term for the order of the zodiac signs from west to east according to this passage?
A) Khilāf al-Tawālī
B) al-Tawālī
C) al-I'tibār
D) al-Dawr

Key Takeaway
The zodiac signs are classified into seasonal groups based on the sun's position, and their order from west to east is called al-Tawālī, corresponding to planetary motions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage says the cycle of signs begins at the vernal equinox because that is when growth and fruit appear. When you observe the renewal of life in spring, do you consciously recognize Allah's power and wisdom in setting this precise order? How can you turn this recognition into a regular act of gratitude and worship?
?
Al-Tawālī is the west-to-east order that matches the planets' proper motions, while its opposite is east to west. Is the 'order' of your daily life aligned with the sunnah of Allah in creation, or are you sometimes moving in the opposite direction (east to west) against His design? What specific area of your routine needs realignment?
?
The seasonal grouping of the signs is not arbitrary but tied to the sun's position at equinoxes and solstices. When you plan your year, do you consciously integrate the natural rhythms Allah has placed in creation, or do you live disconnected from these signs? What would change if you deliberately synchronised your life with these celestial markers?

The Circle Passing Through the Four Poles
Defines the circle passing through the four poles and deduces its poles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the circle passing through the four poles and why its poles are the equinox points.

Core Idea
The circle passing through the four poles has its own poles at the equinoxes.

In celestial astronomy, a unique great circle is defined by passing through both the poles of the celestial equator and the poles of the ecliptic. This circle necessarily passes through the points of maximum declination. Understanding this circle helps clarify the geometric relationship between the equator and ecliptic.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the key terms: الأقطاب الأربعة, المنطقتان, الإعتدالان.
The passage contains both matn and commentary; focus on the core definition first.
2
Identify the circle's definition
The passage defines a circle that passes through the four poles (poles of equator and ecliptic) and necessarily passes through the extreme distances between the two zones.
This circle is a third great circle after the equator and ecliptic.
3
Understand the geometric reasoning
The passage explains that if a circle intersects two others at right angles, its poles lie in those circles. Since this circle intersects both equator and ecliptic at right angles, its poles must lie in both, meaning they are the equinox points (where the equator and ecliptic intersect).
This is a key insight: the poles are not the celestial poles but the equinoxes.

ثم توهموا دائرة مارة بالأقطاب الأربعة أعني قطبي معدل النهار وقطبي فلك البروج وسموها بهذا الاسم ولا بد أن تمر هذه الدائرة بغاية البعد بين المنطقتين كما بين في الأكر قوله: (فصارت إلخ) أي صارت دائرة ثالثة بعد المنطقتين. قوله: (وسموها بهذا الاسم) أي سموها بالدائرة المارة بالأقطاب الأربعة وقد يطلق عليها اسم المارة وحده كما ذكره بقوله: فإذا تقاطعت كذلك إلخ يعني أن كل دائرة إذا تقاطعت كذلك أي قاطعت على قوائم دائرتين وجب أن يكون قطباها موضع أيضاً أن تقاطعهما ووجب مارة بأقطابهما الأربعة وهاتان الدائرتان اللتان قاطعتهما هذه الدائرة كذلك إما أن تكونا متقاطعتين على قوائم كدائرة نصف النهار ودائرة أول السماوات فإنهما متقاطعتان على قوائم وقد قاطعتهما دائرة الأفق على قوائم أيضاً كما سيجيء إن شاء الله تعالى وإما أن تكونا متقاطعتين لا على قوائم كمنطقتي المعدل وفلك البروج فإنهما كانتا متقاطعتين لا على قوائم على ما سيجيء لكن الدائرة المارة قاطعتهما على قوائم كما ذكره. قوله: (فمن المعدل إلخ) فمن المعدل تمر بالانقلابين ومن المنطقة بنظيرهما. والصحيح عكس ذلك لأن الانقلابين على منطقة البروج كما صرح به فنظيراهما على المعدل. ولا يخفى عليك أن هذه الدائرة هي إحدى الدوائر الست المذكورة في قسمة البروج إلا أنها امتازت عن سائرها بمرورها بالأقطاب وغايتي البعدين فصارت بعد المنطقتين ثالثة للدوائر العظام وقطبا هذه الدائرة الاعتدالان إذ يجب أن يقعا أي قطباها في الدائرتين أي المنطقتين لأنها مقاطعة لهما على قوائم لمرورها بأقطابهما وكل دائرة تقاطع أخرى على قوائم فيكون قطب كل منهما نقطة من الأخرى فإذا قاطعت كذلك دائرتين كالمارة وجب أن يكون قطباها واقعين في كل منهما والواقع فيهما أي في منطقتي المعدل وفلك البروج هو موضع تقاطعهما وهما الاعتدالان فيكونان قطبين للمارة بالأقطاب الأربعة
Then they hypothesized a circle passing through the four poles, namely the poles of the celestial equator and the poles of the ecliptic, and they named it by this name. It is necessary that this circle passes through the extreme distance between the two zones (al-mintaqatayn) as explained in the spheres. [Commentary:] His statement: (it became, etc.) meaning it became a third circle after the two zones. His statement: (and they named it by this name) meaning they named it the circle passing through the four poles, and it may be called by the name 'the passing (circle)' alone as he mentioned in his statement: 'If they intersect similarly, etc.' That is, every circle that intersects two circles at right angles must have its poles also at their intersection, and it must pass through their four poles. These two circles that this circle intersects in this manner are either intersecting at right angles, like the meridian circle and the circle of the first heaven, for they intersect at right angles, and the horizon circle also intersects them at right angles, as will come if God wills; or they intersect not at right angles, like the two zones of the equator and the ecliptic, for they intersect not at right angles as will come, but the passing circle intersects them at right angles as he mentioned. His statement: (from the equator, etc.) So from the equator it passes through the solstices, and from the zone (the ecliptic) through their counterparts. But the correct is the opposite, because the solstices are on the ecliptic, as he stated, so their counterparts are on the equator. And it is not hidden from you that this circle is one of the six circles mentioned in the division of the signs, except that it is distinguished from others by passing through the poles and the two extreme distances, so it becomes after the two zones a third of the great circles. And the poles of this circle are the equinoxes, because it is necessary that its poles lie in the two circles (i.e., the two zones) because it intersects them at right angles due to its passage through their poles. And every circle that intersects another at right angles has its pole as a point of the other. So if it intersects two circles in this manner like the passing circle, it is necessary that its poles lie in each of them. And what lies in them (i.e., in the two zones of the equator and the ecliptic) is the point of their intersection, and they are the equinoxes. So they become the poles of the circle passing through the four poles.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage defines a great circle called the circle passing through the four poles. It begins by stating that they hypothesized a circle passing through the poles of the celestial equator and the ecliptic, which necessarily passes through the extreme distances between the two zones (the greatest declination). The commentary then explains that this circle is a third great circle after the equator and ecliptic. It elaborates on the condition that if a circle intersects two other circles at right angles, its poles lie in those circles. This is applied to the two zones (equator and ecliptic) which intersect at an angle, but the passing circle intersects them perpendicularly. Therefore, its poles are the equinox points where the equator and ecliptic intersect. The passage also clarifies that the solstices are on the ecliptic, not the equator, so the circle passes through them on the equator? There is a correction: 'the correct is the opposite' meaning the circle actually passes through the solstices on the ecliptic and their counterparts on the equator. The passage establishes the geometric necessity of the poles being the equinoxes.

Misconception: The poles of this circle are the celestial poles (i.e., north and south poles).
Correction: The poles of this circle are actually the equinox points, because the circle intersects the equator and ecliptic at right angles, so its poles lie at their intersection points.

The Circle of Declination in Astronomy
Defines the circle of declination and its arcs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what the circle of declination is, how it is defined, and what it measures.

Core Idea
The circle of declination measures the angular distance of celestial bodies from the equator.

The circle of declination is a fundamental concept in classical astronomy, used to track the position of stars and planets. In this passage, the author defines this great circle and explains how it measures declination from the celestial equator. Pay attention to the key terms and the precise wording used in the definition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage slowly, then the English translation. Get a feel for the concepts without overthinking.
Notice the use of technical terms like 'ميل' and 'معدل النهار'.
2
Identify the circle's components
The passage defines the circle as passing through the poles of the equator and a point on the ecliptic or a star. Identify what each of these elements is.
3
Understand how declination is measured
The arc of this circle between the equator and the celestial body gives its declination. The maximum declination is at the solstices.
This is a key astronautical measurement.
4
Consider the grammatical note
The author comments on the use of 'or' (أو) instead of 'and' (و). Why is this important? Reflect on the inclusivity of the definition.
This prevents the false assumption that the circle must pass through both a point and a star simultaneously.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتوهموا دائرة أخرى من العظام تمر بقطبي معدل النهار وجزء ما من منطقة البروج أو بكوكب من الكواكب وسميت هذه الدائرة دائرة الميل إذ يعرف بها ميل أجزاء منطقة البروج عن المعدل الذي ينسب إليه الاستقامة كما قال والقوس الواقعة من هذه الدائرة بين المعدل وبين ذلك الجزء من المنطقة ميل ذلك الجزء عن المعدل وأعظم ميول أجزائها هو ميل الانقلابين والقوس الواقعة منها بينه وبين الكواكب يعني وبين طرف خط يخرج من مركز العالم إلى سطح الفلك الأعلى ماراً بمركز الكواكب بعده أي بعد الكوكب عن المعدل وهذه الدائرة أعم مطلقاً من الدائرة المارة بالأقطاب قوله: (أعم مطلقاً من الدائرة المارة بالأقطاب) فإنها دائرة ميل الانقلابين. قوله: (أو بكوكب إلخ) أورد كلمة أو هاهنا دون الواو تنبيهاً على أن في دائرة الميل يعتبر مرورها تارة بجزء من أجزاء المنطقة وتارة يعتبر مرورها بكوكب من الكواكب ولم يعتبر فيها مرورها بالجزء والكوكب معاً كما توهم، ولما كان المعتبر في دائرة العرض مثل ما ذكر هاهنا أورد هناك أيضاً كلمة أو فقال: أو بكوكب ما ولم يقل بكوكب ما بالواو
And they hypothesized another great circle passing through the poles of the celestial equator and some part of the ecliptic or a star, and it is called the circle of declination (dā'irat al-mayl), because by it the declination (mayl) of parts of the ecliptic from the equator is known. As he said: 'And the arc of this circle between the equator and that part of the ecliptic is the declination of that part from the equator.' And the greatest of the declinations of its parts is the declination of the solstices. And the arc of it between it (the equator) and the stars—meaning between the equator and the extremity of a line extending from the center of the world to the surface of the highest sphere passing through the center of the star—is its distance, i.e., the distance of the star from the equator. And this circle is absolutely more general than the circle passing through the four poles. His statement: (absolutely more general than the circle passing through the four poles) for it is the circle of declination of the solstices. His statement: (or a star, etc.) He used the word 'or' here without 'and' to indicate that in the circle of declination, its passage is considered sometimes through a part of the ecliptic and sometimes through a star, and he did not consider it passing through both the part and the star together, as was supposed. And since what is considered in the circle of latitude is similar to what is mentioned here, he also used the word 'or' there and said: 'or a star' and did not say 'and a star' with a waw.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage defines the circle of declination. It begins by stating that it is a great circle that passes through the poles of the celestial equator and any point on the ecliptic (or a star). It is used to measure the declination (angular distance from the equator) of that point. The arc between the equator and the point on the ecliptic is that point's declination. The maximum declination is at the solstices. For a star, the declination is the arc from the equator along this circle to the line from Earth's center through the star. This circle is more general than the four-pole circle because it can be drawn for any point. The commentary then explains the use of 'or' (أو) instead of 'and' (و), meaning it can pass through either a point on the ecliptic or a star, not both simultaneously. It also notes that the same wording is used in the definition of the circle of latitude.

منطقة البروج
انقلابان

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the circle of declination always passes through the solstices.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the circle can pass through any point on the ecliptic (not just the solstices) or a star.

Checkpoint

B) The star's declination

Key Takeaway

The Circle of Latitude
Defines the circle of latitude and its function.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what the circle of latitude is and how it measures angular distance from the ecliptic.

Core Idea
The circle of latitude measures a celestial object's distance from the ecliptic.

In this passage, the author introduces another great circle used in ancient astronomy: the circle of latitude. This circle, passing through the poles of the ecliptic, allows astronomers to measure how far a star or point on the celestial equator is from the ecliptic. It is the counterpart of the circle of declination, which measures distance from the celestial equator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice how the definition is presented.
The Arabic uses the verb 'توهموا' indicating a hypothesized construction.
2
Identify the components
The circle passes through the poles of the ecliptic and a point on the celestial equator or a star. These are the key elements.
3
Understand the measurement
The arc between the ecliptic and that point is the latitude. This is the angular distance from the ecliptic.
Latitude here is celestial latitude, not terrestrial.
4
Contrast with declination
Declination measures distance from the celestial equator using poles of the equator; latitude uses poles of the ecliptic.
This distinction is crucial in astronomy.
5
Reflect on the term
Note that 'latitude' (عرض) in this context is an astronomical term, distinct from its use in geography.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتوهموا دائرة أخرى من العظام مارة بقطبي منطقة البروج وبجزء ما من أجزاء معدل النهار أيضاً أو بكوكب ما وسموها دائرة العرض والقوس الواقعة منها بين المنطقة وبين ذلك الجزء من المعدل أو ذلك الكوكب عرض ذلك الجزء
And they hypothesized another great circle passing through the poles of the ecliptic and some part of the celestial equator or a star, and they named it the circle of latitude (dā'irat al-'arḍ). And the arc of it between the ecliptic and that part of the equator or that star is the latitude ('arḍ) of that part.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage defines the circle of latitude. It states that it is a great circle that passes through the poles of the ecliptic and any point on the celestial equator (or a star). The arc between the ecliptic and the point on the equator (or star) is the latitude of that point, i.e., its angular distance from the ecliptic. This is the counterpart of the circle of declination, but measuring distance from the ecliptic instead of the equator.

Correction: The circle of latitude passes through the poles of the ecliptic, not the equator, and measures distance from the ecliptic.

The Circle Passing Through the Four Poles
Defining the solstitial colure and proving its poles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the solstitial colure (the circle passing through the four poles) and the geometric proof that its poles are the equinoxes.

Core Idea
The equinoxes are the poles of the circle that passes through the solstices and celestial poles.

This passage examines a specific great circle in the celestial sphere, known as the solstitial colure. It corrects a common misstatement about which circles it passes through and then presents a geometric proof that its poles are the equinoxes, using the principle of intersecting great circles at right angles. Let's read the original text and unpack its logic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the author begins by quoting and then correcting the text.
2
Identify the correction
The text says 'from the celestial equator passes through the solstices, and from the ecliptic through their counterparts.' But the correct is the opposite. Why? Because the solstices are on the ecliptic, not on the equator.
This correction is important for the definition.
3
Understand the definition
This circle is one of the six great circles of the zodiac. It is distinguished by passing through the poles and the extreme distances (solstices), making it the third great circle after the two tropics.
4
Follow the proof
The proof shows that the equinoxes are its poles. Because the circle intersects the celestial equator and ecliptic at right angles (since it passes through their poles), its poles must lie on both circles. The only common points are the equinoxes.
Each step of the proof builds on the previous one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( فمن المعدل) تمر ( بالانقلابين ومن المنطقة بنظيرهما ) . والصحيح عكس ذلك لأن الانقلابين على منطقة البروج كما صرح به فنظیراهما على المعدل. ولا يخفى عليك أن هذه الدائرة هي إحدى الدوائر الست المذكورة في قسمة البروج إلا أنها امتازت عن سائرها بمرورها بالأقطاب وغايتي البعدين فصارت بعد المنطقتين ثالثة للدوائر العظام ) وقطبا هذه الدائرة الاعتدالان إذ يجب أن يقعا ) أي قطباها (في الدائرتين ) أي المنطقتين ( لأنها مقاطعة لهما على قوائم ) لمرورها بأقطابهما ( وكل دائرة تقاطع أخرى على قوائم فيكون قطب كل منهما (نقطة من الأخرى) فإذا قاطعت كذلك دائرتين كالمارة وجب أن يكون قطباها واقعين في كل منهما ) والواقع فيهما أي في منطقتي المعدل وفلك البروج ) هو موضع تقاطعهما وهما الاعتدالان ) فيكونان قطبين للمارة بالأقطاب الأربعة
From the celestial equator (al-muʿaddil) passes through the solstices, and from the ecliptic (al-minṭaqa) through their counterparts. The correct is the opposite, because the solstices are on the ecliptic, so their counterparts are on the celestial equator. It is not hidden from you that this circle is one of the six circles mentioned in the division of the zodiac, except that it is distinguished from others by passing through the poles and the two extreme distances, so it becomes, after the two tropics, the third of the great circles. The poles of this circle are the equinoxes, because it must be that its poles lie in the two circles (i.e., the two tropics) because it intersects them at right angles due to its passing through their poles. Every circle that intersects another at right angles has its pole a point on the other. So if it similarly intersects two circles, it is necessary that its poles lie in each of them. That which lies in them (i.e., in the tropics of the celestial equator and the ecliptic) is their point of intersection, which are the equinoxes. Therefore they are the poles of the circle passing through the four poles.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage defines the circle passing through the four poles (the solstitial colure) and proves that its poles are the equinoxes. The author begins by quoting the original text (matn): 'From the celestial equator passes through the solstices, and from the ecliptic through their counterparts.' The commentary immediately corrects this: the correct view is the opposite, because the solstices lie on the ecliptic, so their counterparts (the equinoxes) lie on the celestial equator. The circle in question is one of the six great circles of the zodiac division but is unique in passing through the poles and the solstices (the two extreme distances), making it the third great circle after the two tropics. The logical proof follows: the equinoxes must be its poles because the circle intersects the celestial equator and ecliptic at right angles (since it passes through their poles). For any two circles intersecting at right angles, the pole of one lies on the other. Since this circle intersects both tropics at right angles, its poles must lie on each of them. The only points common to both tropics are the equinoxes (the intersection points of the celestial equator and ecliptic). Therefore, the equinoxes are the poles of the circle passing through the four poles.

Key Terms
المعدل
المنطقة
الانقلابان
الاعتدالان
المارة بالأقطاب الأربعة
المقاطعة على قوائم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The circle passes through the solstices from the celestial equator and through their counterparts from the ecliptic.
Correction: Actually, the solstices are on the ecliptic, so the circle passes through the solstices from the ecliptic and through their counterparts (equinoxes) from the celestial equator.

Checkpoint
What are the poles of the circle passing through the four poles?
A) The solstices
B) The equinoxes
C) The celestial poles
D) The ecliptic poles

Key Takeaway
The equinoxes are the poles of the solstitial colure because it intersects the celestial equator and ecliptic at right angles, so its poles lie on those circles at their intersection points.

Circle of Declination and Its Properties
Defining the circle of declination and explaining its use and generality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the circle of declination, how it measures declination and astral distances, and why it is more general than the circle through the poles.

Core Idea
The circle of declination measures a star's distance from the celestial equator.

In this passage, the author defines an auxiliary celestial circle used to quantify the separation of ecliptic points and stars from the celestial equator. Understanding this circle is essential for grasping how medieval astronomers computed celestial coordinates. The passage also clarifies a subtle point about the circle's generality and the correct terminology.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of the circle
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overview of the circle of declination and its purpose.
The circle is defined as passing through the poles of the celestial equator and a point on the ecliptic or a star.
2
Identify the two cases of its use
Note that the circle can be imagined through a part of the ecliptic or through a star. This is indicated by the word 'aw' (or) instead of 'wa' (and).
The author explains that this shows the circle is not required to pass through both simultaneously.
3
Understand the arcs as declination or distance
The arc between the celestial equator and the ecliptic point is the declination of that point. The arc to a star gives the star's distance from the equator.
The maximum declination is at the solstices.
4
Examine the generality claim
The circle of declination is said to be absolutely more general than the circle through the poles because it can pass through any star or ecliptic point, not just the solstices.
5
Clarify terminology confusion
The passage discusses that the same arc can be called 'declination' (mayl) when measured from the equator or 'latitude' (ʿarḍ) when measured from the ecliptic, but due to the equator being the reference for rectitude, 'declination' is preferred.
This resolves a common misconception about mixing terms.

And they imagined another great circle passing through the poles of the celestial equator and a part of the ecliptic or a star, and they named this circle the circle of declination, for by it is known the declination of the parts of the ecliptic from the celestial equator, to which rectitude is attributed. As he said: 'The arc of this circle between the celestial equator and that part of the ecliptic is the declination of that part' from the celestial equator. The greatest of its declinations is the declination of the solstices. And the arc of it between it (the celestial equator) and the stars, meaning between the line from the center of the world to the surface of the highest sphere passing through the center of the stars, is its distance, i.e., the distance of the star from the celestial equator. And this circle is absolutely more general than the circle passing through the poles.

His saying: 'Absolutely more general than the circle passing through the poles'—for it is the declination circle of the solstices.

His saying: 'or a star, etc.'—he used the word 'or' here without 'and' to indicate that in the circle of declination, its passage is considered sometimes through a part of the ecliptic and sometimes through a star, and it is not considered through both a part and a star together as was imagined. And since what is considered in the circle of latitude is like what was mentioned here, he also used the word 'or' there, saying 'or a star' and did not say 'and a star'.

Or the star: 'As for that arc being the latitude of the stars from the ecliptic, it is correct without doubt. As for it being the latitude of that part of the celestial equator from it, there is that although it is correct in meaning, yet rectitude, as we indicated, is attributed to the celestial equator, so it is not said that it is inclined from the ecliptic, nor are its parts said to have declinations or latitudes from it. Hence you see them calling that arc the latitude of a part of the ecliptic from the celestial equator, and they also call it the second declination of it from the celestial equator. And this circle is also absolutely more general than the circle passing through the poles.

His saying: 'latitude of a part of the ecliptic from the celestial equator'—the word 'min' here is partitive, and the word 'an' is attached to his saying 'latitude of a part'. And his saying 'the second declination'—as for the first declination, it is an arc from the circle of declination as previously mentioned, and the pronoun in 'lahu' refers to that part. His saying 'from the celestial equator' is attached to 'declination'.

His saying: 'And this circle is also absolutely more general, etc.'—for the circle passing through the solstices is like the second that expels the solstices.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage defines the circle of declination (dā'irat al-mayl) and explains its properties. The matn states that another great circle is imagined, passing through the poles of the celestial equator and a point on the ecliptic or a star; it is called the circle of declination. Its function is to measure the declination of ecliptic points from the celestial equator. The arc between the celestial equator and a point on the ecliptic is that point's declination, with the maximum being the solstices. Similarly, the arc from the celestial equator to a star gives the star's distance (declination). The circle is absolutely more general than the circle through the four poles because it can pass through any star or ecliptic point, not just the solstices. The glosses that follow clarify textual details: the use of 'aw' (or) instead of 'wa' (and) to indicate that the circle may pass through either a part or a star, not both simultaneously. There is also a discussion about terminology: the arc from the celestial equator to a point on the ecliptic can be called either 'declination' (mayl) or 'latitude' (ʿarḍ) depending on perspective, but since rectitude is attributed to the celestial equator, it is more proper to call it 'declination' when measured from it and 'latitude' when measured from the ecliptic. The final gloss explains that this circle's generality means it can coincide with the circle through the poles when passing through the solstices, but otherwise not.

The Circle of Latitude and Its Generality
Definition and generality of the circle of latitude

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the circle of latitude is defined in Islamic astronomy and why it is considered more general than the fixed circle through the four poles.

Core Idea
The circle of latitude changes with the star's position, making it more general than the unique circle through the poles.

In this passage, the author defines the circle of latitude as a great circle passing through the poles of the ecliptic and a point on the celestial equator or a star. He then explains its perpendicular intersection with the celestial equator and ecliptic, and finally demonstrates that this circle is absolutely more general than the circle through the four poles. The key insight is that the circle of latitude is not fixed; its orientation depends on the star's position, whereas the circle through the poles is unique.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Pay attention to the author's definition of the circle of latitude and the description of intersections.
Notice the technical terms: دائرة العرض (circle of latitude), منطقة البروج (ecliptic), معدل النهار (celestial equator).
2
Identify the definition
The circle of latitude is defined as a great circle passing through the poles of the ecliptic and a point on the celestial equator or a star. The arc from that point to the ecliptic is the latitude.
3
Understand the intersection properties
The passage states that this circle intersects both the celestial equator and the ecliptic at right angles. It also notes that the horizon does the same.
This is analogous to the modern concept of a coordinate system.
4
Grasp the generality argument
The gloss explains that the circle of latitude can coincide with the circle through the four poles only when the star or part lies on that fixed circle. Otherwise, it is a different circle. Since the star moves, the circle of latitude can take many positions, making it more general.
The fixed circle through the poles is unique; the circle of latitude is not.
5
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to confirm you understand when the two circles coincide.
The answer is based solely on the gloss's explanation.

And they imagined another great circle passing through the poles of the ecliptic and a part of the celestial equator also, or a star, and they named it the circle of latitude. The arc between that part of the celestial equator or that star is the latitude of that part from it (the ecliptic). They said: it intersects the two circles (the celestial equator and ecliptic) at right angles, and the horizon also intersects them at right angles, as will come. Or they may intersect not at right angles, like the celestial equator and the ecliptic, for they intersect not at right angles, but the passing circle intersects them at right angles as mentioned.

His saying: 'And this circle is absolutely more general, etc.'—for this circle, when the star or part moves such that it becomes at a position where it passes through the four poles, it coincides with the circle through the four poles. Then if it passes beyond this position by the motion of the star or part, at that time it does not coincide with the passing circle. Then if it reaches the mentioned position again, it coincides with the passing circle again. And similarly is the statement regarding the circle of latitude being absolutely more general than the passing circle.

Commentary
This passage defines the circle of latitude (dā'irat al-ʿarḍ). The matn states that another great circle is imagined, passing through the poles of the ecliptic and a point on the celestial equator or a star; the arc between that point and the celestial equator (or the star) is the latitude of that part (or star) from the ecliptic. The commentary then discusses how this circle intersects the celestial equator and ecliptic at right angles, similar to the horizon. The gloss further explains that the circle of latitude is absolutely more general than the circle through the four poles. The reason is that depending on the position of the star or the part of the ecliptic, the circle of latitude may coincide with the circle through the poles (when the star or part lies on that circle) or not. As the star moves, the circle of latitude changes, whereas the circle through the poles is fixed. Hence, the circle of latitude is more general because it can assume many positions, while the circle through the poles is unique.

The Five Great Circles and Three Identical Ones
Classifying the five great circles and proving three are identical in person.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classification of the five great circles in spherical astronomy and the proof that three of them are identical in person.

Core Idea
Three great circles are identical in person: the equator, ecliptic, and the circle through the four poles.

In this passage, the author classifies the imagined circles on the celestial sphere into five great circles. He then focuses on three that are considered identical in person despite apparent differences in motion. The argument relies on principles of spherics and the impossibility of two great circles intersecting at points less than half a rotation apart.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five circles and the three identical ones
Read the passage and note that there are five great circles, of which three are identical in person: the celestial equator, the ecliptic, and the circle through the four poles.
2
Understand why the equator and ecliptic are identical in person
The identity of the first two is 'apparent' because each sphere has only one such circle; multiplicity is impossible.
The commentary explains that sound imagination testifies to this.
3
Grasp the proof for the circle through the four poles
The proof uses spherics: it is impossible for two great circles to intersect at two points whose distance is less than half a rotation, implying uniqueness.
The gloss elaborates that motion does not affect personal identity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(فهي) أي الدوائر المذكورة) خمس دوائر) عظام (توهموها ) على الفلك ( لا) بالنسبة إلى السفليات ثلاثة) منها ( متحددة بالشخص، هي معدل النهار والمنطقة والمارة بالأقطاب الأربعة ) أما وحدة الأوليين بالشخص فظاهرة ، وأما وحدة الثالثة كذلك فلما بين في الأكر من أنه يستحيل أنه تتقاطع دائرتان عظيمتان على نقطتين بينهما أقل من نصف الدور فلا

قوله : ( فظاهرة لامتناع تعدد المنطقة لفلك واحد كما يشهد به التخيل الصحيح فإن هاهنا دائرة واحدة تتحرك بحركة قطبيها حد قطبي المعدل لا دائرتان تنطبقان تارة وتفترقان أخرى في شرح التذكرة للحصري وكذا تماسها عليهما أي القطبين محال وإلا لزم إحاطة المستقيمين بسطح وكذا الانطباق ببعض سطح أحدهما على بعض سطح الأخرى فما بين القطبين محال وإلا لزم اتصال شرط واحد مستقيم ليسا في سمت واحد

قوله : ( وأما وحدة الثالثة كذلك أي بالشخص فلما بين إلخ . نعم كانت هذه الدائرة الثالثة بحيث تتحرك مارة بالأقطاب الأربعة على قطبي معدل النهار وتتحرك قطباها اللذان هما الاعتدالان على محور المعدل أيضاً. وتتحرك قطبا المنطقة أيضاً بالحركة اليومية على قطبي المعدل وهذه الحركة اليومية لا تنافي الوحدة الشخصية كما لا يخفى .

قوله : (دائرتان عظيمتان وإنما قال عظيمتان لأن الدائرتين إذا كانت إحداهما صغيرة والأخرى عظيمة يجوز تقاطعهما على نقطتين بحيث يكون بعد ما بين النقطتين أقل من نصف
So these (i.e., the mentioned circles) are five great circles which they imagined on the sphere. Three of them are identical in person: the celestial equator, the ecliptic, and the circle passing through the four poles. As for the identity in person of the first two, it is apparent. As for the identity in person of the third, it is as was explained in the spherics: it is impossible that two great circles intersect at two points whose distance is less than half the rotation, so...

His saying: 'it is apparent'—because it is impossible for there to be multiple ecliptics for one sphere, as sound imagination testifies. For here there is one circle that moves with the motion of its poles to the limit of the poles of the celestial equator, not two circles that sometimes coincide and sometimes separate. In the commentary on al-Tadhkira by al-Husari, similarly, their tangency to the two poles is impossible, otherwise it would necessitate the enclosure of straight lines by a surface, and similarly the coincidence of part of the surface of one with part of the surface of the other. So what is between the poles is impossible, otherwise it would necessitate the connection of two straight lines that are not in one direction.

His saying: 'And as for the identity of the third similarly, i.e., in person, it is as was explained, etc.' Yes, this third circle is such that it moves as a circle passing through the four poles on the poles of the celestial equator, and its poles, which are the equinoxes, also move on the axis of the celestial equator. The poles of the ecliptic also move with the daily motion on the poles of the celestial equator, and this daily motion does not contradict personal identity, as is not hidden.

His saying: 'two great circles'—and he only said 'great circles' because if one of the two circles is small and the other great, it is permissible for them to intersect at two points such that the distance between the two points is less than half the rotation.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage classifies the imagined circles into five great circles, of which three are identical in person (muttaḥida bi al-shakhṣ): the celestial equator (muʿaddil al-nahār), the ecliptic (al-minṭaqa), and the circle through the four poles (al-mārra bi al-aqṭāb al-arbaʿa). The identity of the celestial equator and ecliptic is evident because each sphere has only one such circle. The third circle's identity is proven by spherics: it is impossible for two great circles to intersect at two points less than half a rotation apart, implying uniqueness. The glosses further explain: for the ecliptic, multiplicity is impossible because it would lead to contradictions. For the third circle, its motion (it rotates with the daily motion around the celestial poles) does not affect its personal identity; it remains one circle because its poles (the equinoxes) move along the celestial equator. The final gloss discusses why the author specified 'great circles' in the proof: only great circles cannot intersect at points less than half a rotation apart; small circles can.

Correction: They are uniquely defined by the actual motion of the spheres; multiplicity would lead to contradictions.

The circle through the four poles moves yet remains identical in person. Consider a commitment or duty in your life that has changed circumstances but should remain the same in essence. Are you keeping that essence fixed, or have you let the changes dilute it?

The proof that two great circles cannot intersect improperly points to an underlying order in creation. Reflect: in your personal habits or thoughts, where have you acted as if order does not apply? Are you aligning your inner life with the orderly design Allah has placed in the universe?

Planetary Latitude and the Two Inclinations
Defines the arc of planetary latitude relative to the ecliptic and distinguishes first and second inclination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why planetary latitude is measured from the ecliptic rather than the equator, and the difference between the first and second inclination.

Core Idea
Planetary latitude is measured from the ecliptic, not the equator.

This passage from a classical astronomical work clarifies a key technical point: how astronomers define the arc that represents a planet's distance from the ecliptic. Although one might think this arc relates to the celestial equator, the author explains why it is correctly understood relative to the ecliptic and introduces the crucial distinction between the first and second inclinations. Understanding this distinction is foundational for celestial coordinate systems.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to the technical terms for ecliptic, equator, and inclination.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the two types of inclination
Find where the passage defines 'first inclination' and 'second inclination'. Note their relationship to the celestial equator and ecliptic.
The first inclination relates to the declination circle; the second is the arc from the ecliptic.
3
Understand why the ecliptic is used
The author argues that 'straightness' is attributed to the equator, so it would be incorrect to say parts are inclined from it. Instead, the arc is latitude from the ecliptic.
This is a conceptual clarification.
4
Examine the terminological notes
The marginal note clarifies the grammar: 'min' is partitive, 'an' is attached to 'latitude'. Also, note the distinction between first and second inclination.
This helps in reading other texts.

المرصد الأول - الفصل الثاني : القسم الأول : المقصد الثاني : في اتجاهات الجسم أو الكوكب ( أما أن تلك القوس هي عرض الكواكب عن منطقة البروج فصحيح بلا شبهة وأما كونها عرض ذلك الجزء من المعدل عنها، ففيه أنه وإن كان صحيحاً بحسب المعنى إلا أن الاستقامة كما أشرنا إليها منسوبة إلى المعدل فلا يقال : إنه مائل عن منطقة البروج ولا يقال لأجزائه أنها ذوات ميول أو عروض عنها ومن ثمة تراهم يسمون تلك القوس عرض جزء من المنطقة عن المعدل ويسمونها أيضاً الميل الثاني له عن المعدل وهذه الدائرة أيضاً أعم مطلقاً من المارة بالأقطاب
قوله : ( عرض جزء من المنطقة عن المعدل كلمة من هاهنا تبعيضية وكلمة عن بقوله : عرض جزء وقوله : الميل الثاني. وأما الميل الأول فهو قوس من دائرة الميل على ما مر والضمير في له راجع إلى ذلك الجزء . وقوله : عن المعدل متعلق بالميل .
The First Observatory - Chapter Two - Section One - Second Goal: On the directions of the body or planet. (As for that arc being the latitude of the planets from the ecliptic, it is correct without doubt. As for it being the latitude of that part from the equator regarding it, then in it that although it is correct in meaning, yet straightness as we have indicated is attributed to the equator, so it is not said that it is inclined from the ecliptic, nor is it said of its parts that they have inclinations or latitudes from it. Hence you see them calling that arc 'the latitude of a part of the ecliptic from the equator' and also calling it 'the second inclination of it from the equator.' And this circle is also absolutely more general than the circle passing through the poles.
His saying: ('the latitude of a part of the ecliptic from the equator') — the word 'min' here is partitive, and the word 'an' is by his saying 'latitude of a part', and his saying 'the second inclination'. As for the first inclination, it is an arc of the declination circle as has passed, and the pronoun 'lahu' refers to that part. And his saying 'from the equator' is attached to 'inclination'.)
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage defines the arc that represents a planet's celestial latitude. It begins by asserting that the arc is correctly understood as the planet's distance from the ecliptic (منطقة البروج), not from the celestial equator (معدل النهار). Although interpreting it as distance from the equator could be meaningful, the author explains that the concept of 'straightness' (استقامة) is associated with the equator, so it would be misleading to say a planet is 'inclined' relative to the ecliptic. Therefore, scholars name this arc 'the latitude of a part of the ecliptic from the equator' and also call it 'the second inclination' of that part from the equator. The author then notes that this circle (the one defining latitude) is more general than the circle passing through the poles (الدارة بالأقطاب). The marginal note clarifies terminology: the word 'part' (جزء) indicates partiality, and the preposition 'from' (عن) in 'from the equator' is attached to 'latitude'. It distinguishes between the first inclination (الميل الأول), which is an arc of the declination circle, and the second inclination (الميل الثاني) mentioned here, with the pronoun referring to that part.

Misconception: The arc of planetary latitude is measured from the celestial equator.
Correction: The passage clarifies that it is measured from the ecliptic, and any interpretation involving the equator is only acceptable with specific terminological adjustments, not as a direct measure.

Checkpoint

C) Because the ecliptic is always used in Islamic astronomy.
D) Because the arc is actually the first inclination.

Key Takeaway
The arc of planetary latitude is defined relative to the ecliptic, and the second inclination is the latitude of a part of the ecliptic from the equator, distinct from the first inclination of the declination circle.

How can you ensure that your understanding and speech about such matters are both accurate and properly aligned with the straight path (al-ṣirāṭ al-mustaqīm)? What specific step will you take this week to clarify a truth you take for granted?

Classification of the Five Great Circles on the Celestial Sphere
Classifies five great circles into three unique and two infinite families, with geometric proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the five great circles are classified into three identical in person and two identical in species, and the geometric proof for the uniqueness of the circle through the four poles.

Core Idea
The five great circles split into three unique circles and two infinite families.

In this passage, the author classifies the five great circles hypothesized on the celestial sphere. He distinguishes three circles that are one of a kind from two that come in infinite varieties. The geometric reasoning behind the uniqueness of the circle passing through the four poles is particularly insightful. Let's explore how this classification works.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overview of the classification. Notice the author's logical flow.
Pay attention to the terms 'identical in person' and 'identical in species'.
2
Identify the two categories
Find the three circles called 'identical in person' and the two called 'identical in species'. Understand what each category means.
The three are the equator, ecliptic, and circle through the four poles. The two are declination and latitude circles.
3
Understand the proof for the pole circle
Focus on the argument why only one great circle can pass through the four poles. It uses the principle that two great circles cannot intersect at points less than half a revolution apart.
The distance between the two poles in one direction is less than 24 parts, so no two different circles can both pass through them.
4
Reflect on the infinite nature
Consider why declination and latitude circles are infinite in number. They depend on points on the ecliptic and sphere surface, which are continuous, so there is no indivisible part.
5
Connect to astronomy
Appreciate how these circles are fundamental for locating celestial objects. The pole circle acts as a reference for both declination and latitude circles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهي أي الدوائر المذكورة خمس دوائر عظام توهموها على الفلك لا بالنسبة إلى السفليات ثلاثة منها متحددة بالشخص، هي معدل النهار والمنطقة والمارة بالأقطاب الأربعة ) أما وحدة الأوليين بالشخص فظاهرة ، وأما وحدة الثالثة كذلك فلما بين في الأكر من أنه يستحيل أنه تتقاطع دائرتان عظيمتان على نقطتين بينهما أقل من نصف الدور فلا يتصور أن تمر دائرتان بالأقطاب الأربعة لأن البعد بين القطبين الذين في جهة واحدة أقل من أربعة وعشرين جزءاً فلا يجوز تقاطعهما عليهما. وأما توهم الانطباق فيما بينهما ثم الافتراق فالتخيل الصحيح شاهد ببطلانه ( واثنتان ( منها ( متحدتان بالنوع لا يتناهى أشخاصهما وهما دائرتا الميل والعرض فإنهما يتعددان بحسب النقط المفروضة على منطقة البروج وسطح الفلك وتلك النقط غير متناهية لامتناع الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ ( وكل واحدة منهما قد تنطبق وتتحد بالمارة بالأقطاب ) وذلك ( إذا كان الكوكب ) الذي له بعد عن المعدل أو عرض عن المنطقة (أو الجزء) الذي له ميل أول أو ميل ثان واقعاً ( عليها ) أي على المارة، وقد نبهناك على أن المارة في كل واحد من إحدى دائرتي الميل والعرض )
قوله : (أو الجزء إلخ) أي النصفين تقريباً فإن النصف الظاهر أكثر من الخفي بمقدار نصف قطب الأرض وقامة الرائي يتميز نقطتي الأفق لنقطتي المعدل وفي عرض تسعين متحدان ولا محذور في خروجه إذ هي في عرض تسعين لا يتعين في الوضع فلا يترتب عليه الفوائد الباعثة على اعتبارها .
So they (i.e., the mentioned circles) are five great circles which they hypothesized on the celestial sphere, not with respect to the sublunary world. Three of them are identical in person: the equator, the ecliptic, and the circle passing through the four poles. As for the unity of the first two in person, it is apparent; and as for the unity of the third likewise, it is due to what was shown in the [book of] spheres: that it is impossible for two great circles to intersect at two points between which is less than half a revolution. So it is inconceivable that two circles pass through the four poles because the distance between the two poles in one direction is less than twenty-four parts, so their intersection at them is not permissible. As for the supposition of their coinciding between them then separating, correct intuition testifies to its falsehood. And two of them are identical in species, whose individuals are infinite: the circles of declination and latitude. For they are multiplied according to the points assumed on the ecliptic and the surface of the sphere, and those points are infinite due to the impossibility of an indivisible part. And each of them may coincide and unite with the circle passing through the poles, and that is when the planet which has a distance from the equator or latitude from the ecliptic, or the part which has a first inclination or second inclination, lies on it (i.e., on the circle passing through the poles). And we have alerted you that the circle passing through the poles is in each one of the two circles of declination and latitude.
His saying: ('or part etc.') i.e., approximately the two halves, for the visible half is more than the hidden by the amount of half the earth's pole and the observer's height; the two points of the horizon are distinguished from the two points of the equator, and at latitude ninety they are united, and there is no harm in its exiting, for at latitude ninety it is not determined in position, so the benefits motivating its consideration do not follow from it.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage classifies the five great circles on the celestial sphere. It states that these circles were hypothesized with respect to the celestial sphere itself, not the sublunary world. Three circles are 'identical in person' (متحددة بالشخص), meaning they are unique: the celestial equator (معدل النهار), the ecliptic (المنطقة), and the circle passing through the four poles (المارة بالأقطاب الأربعة). The unity of the first two is obvious; the unity of the third is proved by a geometric argument from the science of spheres (الأكر): it is impossible for two different great circles to intersect at two points that are less than half a revolution apart. Since the distance between the two poles (one of the equator, one of the ecliptic) in one direction is less than 24 degrees (a fraction of a full circle), no two different circles can both pass through the four poles. Likewise, imagining coincidence then separation is false. The other two circles are 'identical in species' (متحدتان بالنوع): the circle of declination (دائرة الميل) and the circle of latitude (دائرة العرض). They have infinite individuals because they vary based on the infinite number of points assumed on the ecliptic and sphere surface, since there is no indivisible part. These circles can coincide with the circle passing through the poles when a planet or a part lies on it. The note explains that 'or part' refers to the two halves approximately, with technical details about the visible horizon.

متحددة بالشخص
متحدتان بالنوع
معدل النهار
المنطقة
المارة بالأقطاب
دائرة الميل
دائرة العرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The circle passing through the four poles could be multiple different circles.
Correction: The passage proves that only one such great circle can exist because two different great circles cannot intersect at points less than half a revolution apart, and the distance between the two poles in one direction is less than 24 parts.

Checkpoint
Why can there not be two different great circles both passing through the four poles?
A) Because the distance between the two poles is exactly half a revolution.
B) Because the distance between the two poles in one direction is less than 24 parts, making intersection impossible.
C) Because the circles are not great circles.
D) Because the poles are not fixed.

Key Takeaway
The five great circles are classified into three unique circles (equator, ecliptic, pole circle) and two infinite families (declination, latitude), with the pole circle's uniqueness proved by geometric constraints.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author uses correct intuition to reject the notion of two circles coinciding and then separating. Where in your own life have you clung to an imagined falsehood about Allah's creation or His attributes, despite clear reasoning? Will you actively seek to correct such a belief today?
?
This passage shows that even celestial circles are either unique or infinitely many by necessity. Reflect on the wisdom behind Allah's creation: the equator and ecliptic are fixed, while declination circles are infinite to accommodate all bodies. How does this order compel you to trust that nothing in Allah's decree is random? What area of your life do you need to submit to His precise plan?
?
The proof that only one circle can pass through the four poles relies on a geometric impossibility. Consider a time when you felt that multiple paths were equally valid for you, but in truth, there was only one right way. Are you willing to accept that Allah's way is singular and unique, even when other options seem appealing? Commit to following that singular path today.

The Two Circles of Inclination and Latitude
Al-Jurjani defines the circles of inclination and latitude and explains their infinite individuals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the circles of inclination and latitude, why their individuals are infinite, and how they relate to the great circle passing through the poles.

Core Idea
The circles of inclination and latitude are infinite due to the impossibility of indivisible parts in the ecliptic's points.

In this passage, al-Jurjani introduces two special circles among those hypothesized on the celestial sphere: the circles of inclination (dā'irat al-mayl) and latitude (dā'irat al-'arḍ). He explains that they are of the same type and have infinite individual circles because they are determined by the infinite number of hypothetical points on the ecliptic and sphere's surface. He also clarifies when these circles align with the great circle passing through the poles.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the key terms: 'دائرة الميل', 'دائرة العرض', 'منطقة البروج', 'الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ', 'المارة بالأقطاب'.
2
Understand the infinity claim
The passage states that the individuals of these circles are infinite because they multiply based on points on the ecliptic, which are infinite due to the impossibility of indivisible parts. Grasp this reasoning.
3
Examine the condition of alignment
Notice that each of these circles can coincide with the great circle passing through the poles when a star or point with the relevant measure falls upon it. This shows the relation between these circles and the main great circles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واثنتان ( منها ( متحدتان بالنوع لا يتناهى أشخاصهما وهما دائرتا الميل والعرض فإنهما يتعددان بحسب النقط المفروضة على منطقة البروج وسطح الفلك وتلك النقط غير متناهية لامتناع الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ ( وكل واحدة منهما قد تنطبق وتتحد بالمارة بالأقطاب ) وذلك ( إذا كان الكوكب ) الذي له بعد عن المعدل أو عرض عن المنطقة (أو الجزء) الذي له ميل أول أو ميل ثان واقعاً ( عليها ) أي على المارة، وقد نبهناك على أن المارة في كل واحد من إحدى دائرتي الميل والعرض )
And two of them, of the same type, their individuals are infinite, and they are the circles of inclination and latitude. For they are multiplied according to the points hypothesized on the ecliptic and the surface of the sphere, and those points are infinite due to the impossibility of the indivisible part. And each of them may coincide and unite with the passing through the poles. That is when the star that has a distance from the equator or a latitude from the ecliptic, or the part that has a first inclination or a second inclination, falls upon it, i.e., upon the passing one. And we have alerted you that the passing one in each of the two circles of inclination and latitude.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage defines two specific celestial circles among those hypothesized on the sphere. They are the circles of inclination (dā'irat al-mayl) and latitude (dā'irat al-'arḍ). These two are of the same type (muttaḥidatān bi al-naw') and their individuals are infinite. The reason for their infinity is that they are determined by the infinite number of points on the ecliptic and the sphere's surface, and since indivisible parts are impossible (imtina' al-juz' alladhi la yatajazza'), the points are infinite. The author then adds a condition: each of these circles may coincide with the great circle passing through the poles (al-mārah bi al-aqṭāb) when a star or a point with a specific measure lies on it. This clarifies the relation between these circles and the main great circles.

Correction: The text explicitly states that they are infinite because they are based on the infinite points on the ecliptic, as indivisible parts are impossible.
