The Horizon Circle: A Relative Boundary
Al-Jurjani defines the horizon circle as the dividing line between the visible and invisible halves of the sphere, emphasizing its relativity to the observer's location.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the horizon circle is not a fixed universal circle but varies with the observer's location on earth.

Core Idea
The horizon is relative to each locality.

In this passage, al-Jurjani introduces the horizon circle as one of five additional circles on the celestial sphere. He explains that what is visible or invisible depends on where you stand, so each place has its own horizon. This lesson unpacks that concept and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that al-Jurjani begins by stating there are five circles, then focuses on the first: the horizon circle.
2
Identify the key definition
Find where al-Jurjani defines the horizon circle as the dividing line between the visible and invisible halves of the sphere.
This is the core definition.
3
Understand the relativity
The text says visibility/invisibility are relative to inhabitants of a locality. Why does that matter? Because it means the horizon is not a single circle for everyone.
Each locality on earth has its own horizon.
4
Learn the poles of the horizon
The poles of the horizon are the zenith (سمت الرأس) and nadir (سمت القدم) for that locality.
These are also local.
5
Connect to the other four circles
The passage mentions four other circles that pass through these poles, hinting at further circles defined relative to the local horizon.
We'll explore those in upcoming lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتوهموا على الفلك أيضاً خمس دوائر أخر بالنسة إلى السفليات حديها الدائرة الفاصلة بين النصف الظاهر والنصف الخفي من الفلك وتسمى هذه الدائرة ( دائرة الأفق ) ولا شك أن الظهور والخفاء أمران بالإضافة إلى سكان بقعة من بقاع الأرض فيكون الأفق بملاحظة السفليات ) وتختلف بحسب اختلاف ( البقاع ) فإن كل بقعة على الأرض لها أفق على حده ) وقطباها سمت الرأس والقدم في تلك البقعة ( وأربعة ) من ( تمر بقطبيها ) أي بقطبي الأفق فتكون هي أيضاً بملاحظة السفليات .
And they imagined on the sphere also five other circles with respect to sublunary things. The first of them is the circle dividing between the visible half and the invisible half of the sphere, and this circle is called the 'horizon circle'. There is no doubt that visibility and invisibility are two matters relative to the inhabitants of a locality of the earth, so the horizon is in view of sublunary things. And it differs according to the difference of localities, for every locality on earth has a horizon of its own. And its poles are the zenith and the nadir in that locality. And four of them pass through its poles, so they are also in view of sublunary things.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage defines the horizon circle as one of five additional circles considered in relation to sublunary phenomena. It specifically describes the horizon as the dividing line between the visible and invisible halves of the celestial sphere. The author emphasizes that visibility and invisibility are relative terms depending on the observer's location on earth, thus each locality has its own horizon with its poles being the local zenith and nadir. He then notes that four other circles pass through these poles, indicating that these too are relative to sublunary observers.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The horizon is a single, fixed circle that is the same for everyone on Earth.
Correction: The horizon is relative to the observer's location; each locality on Earth has its own horizon, as al-Jurjani explains.

The Meridian Circle: Definition and Function
Al-Jurjani defines the meridian circle and its function in celestial motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the meridian circle, its role in determining midday and midnight, and how it divides the celestial sphere into ascending and descending halves.

Core Idea
The meridian circle is the sky's noontime marker and the divider of celestial motion.

Al-Jurjani now turns to the second of the five great circles: the meridian, or circle of noon. This circle is not just a timekeeper for midday; it also organizes the entire daily motion of the stars, separating the rising arc from the setting arc. Understanding this circle is key to grasping how celestial bodies appear to move from east to west.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overview of the meridian's definition.
Notice the key terms like 'دائرة نصف النهار' and 'وسط السماء'.
2
Identify the key properties
Note that the meridian passes through the poles of the horizon and the poles of the celestial equator, and that it marks noon and midnight.
This connects the circle to daily timekeeping.
3
Trace the motion of a star
Follow Al-Jurjani's description: a star rises, reaches its highest point at the meridian, then sets, and continues below the horizon to its lowest point at the meridian below.
This illustrates the continuous cycle of altitude changes.
4
Understand the division
See how the meridian splits the sphere into the ascending (eastern) half and the descending (western) half.
The ascending half goes from lowest to highest altitude; the descending half from highest to lowest.
5
Reflect on the poles
Recognize that the meridian's poles are the east and west points on the horizon, where the equator intersects.
This ties the meridian to the horizon coordinate system.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( فالثانية : ) منها ( تمر بقطبي الأفق وبقطبي معدل النهار وهي دائرة وسط السماء ) وتسمى دائرة نصف النهار لأن منتصف النهار هو حين وصول الشمس إليها فوق الأفق، كما أن منتصف الليل هو حين وصولها إليها تحته ) وتفصل ) هذه الدائرة ( بين الصاعد والهابط من الفلك وبين النصف الشرقي والغربي منه فإن الكوكب إذا طلع من الأفق يتزايد ارتفاعه شيئاً فشيئاً إلى أن يبلغ نصف النهار فهناك غاية ارتفاعه عن الأفق وإذا انحط منها يتناقص ارتفاعه إلى غروبه، وإذا غرب ينحط عن الأفق متزايداً انحطاطه إلى أن يبلغ نصف النهار تحت الأرض، فهناك غاية انحطاطه عنه ، ثم إنه يأخذ في التقارب منه متناقصاً انحطاطه إلى أن يبلغ الأفق من جهة الشرق ثانياً، فمن غاية الانحطاط تحت الأفق إلى غاية الارتفاع فوقه على خلاف توالي البروج هو النصف الصاعد من الفلك بالقياس إلى الحركة الأولى ويسمى النصف الشرقي أيضاً. ومن غاية الارتفاع إلى غاية الانحطاط هو النصف الهابط منه والنصف الغربي أيضاً. وقطباها نقطتا المشرق والمغرب من الأفق أعني نقطتي تقاطعه مع المعدل وذلك لمرورها بأقطابهما فيما يمران بقطبيهما لما مر )
Then the second of them: it passes through the poles of the horizon and the poles of the celestial equator, and it is the circle of the middle of the sky, and it is called the circle of noon because midday is when the sun reaches it above the horizon, and midnight is when it reaches it below the horizon. And this circle separates between the ascending and descending parts of the sphere and between its eastern and western halves. For when a star rises from the horizon, its altitude increases gradually until it reaches the meridian, there it is at its greatest altitude above the horizon, and when it declines from it, its altitude decreases until it sets. And when it sets, it descends below the horizon with increasing descent until it reaches the meridian below the earth, there it is at its greatest descent from it. Then it begins to approach it with decreasing descent until it reaches the horizon from the east again. So from the point of greatest descent below the horizon to the point of greatest altitude above it, contrary to the order of the signs, is the ascending half of the sphere relative to the prime motion, and it is also called the eastern half. And from the point of greatest altitude to the point of greatest descent is the descending half and also the western half. And its poles are the points of the east and west of the horizon, i.e., the two points of its intersection with the equator, because of its passage through their poles, as they pass through its poles, as mentioned.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage defines the meridian circle (also called the circle of noon) as the second of the five circles. It passes through the poles of the horizon and the poles of the celestial equator. The author explains the significance of this circle for daily motion: it marks midday when the sun reaches it above the horizon and midnight when the sun reaches it below. Moreover, the meridian divides the sphere into ascending and descending halves; the author describes the motion of a star relative to the meridian and the horizon, tracing its altitude changes from rising to culmination to setting, and then below the horizon to lower culmination, and back to rising. The ascending half is the eastern half, and the descending half is the western half. The poles of the meridian are the east and west points on the horizon where the equator intersects it.

وسط السماء
الصاعد والهابط
النصف الشرقي
النصف الغربي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The meridian is only for measuring noon.
Correction: The meridian also defines the eastern and western halves and the ascension/descension of celestial bodies.

Checkpoint

A) The visible and invisible halves
B) The ascending and descending halves of the sphere
C) The northern and southern hemispheres
D) The ecliptic and equator

Key Takeaway
The meridian circle is essential for understanding daily celestial motion, as it marks noon, midnight, and divides the sky into rising and setting halves.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see the sun at its zenith, do you pause to consider that this moment is not random but a precise sign from Allah, marking the division of your day? How does this awareness change the way you spend the hours before and after noon?
?
If every star must reach its highest point and then descend, what does this pattern teach you about the rhythm of your own life? Where are you right now in your personal ascension or descent, and how does knowing this help you submit to Allah's decree?
?
The meridian circle connects the horizon and the equator, linking your local perspective to the universal order. In what areas of your life are you consciously aligning your local, daily actions with the greater divine plan? What step will you take today to live in harmony with that order?

The Third Circle: Passing Through Cardinal Points
Al-Jurjani defines the third circle through horizon and meridian poles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition and significance of the third celestial circle that passes through the poles of the horizon and the meridian.

Core Idea
The third circle intersects the axes of horizon and meridian, defining the cardinal directions.

In this short passage, al-Jurjani describes the third of the five circles used in celestial coordinate systems. This circle is unique because it passes through both the poles of the horizon (zenith and nadir) and the poles of the meridian (east and west). Understanding this circle helps clarify how Islamic astronomers mapped the sky relative to an observer's location.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the translation. Notice the key terms: سمت الرأس (zenith), سمت القدم (nadir), المشرق (east), المغرب (west).
The passage is incomplete at the end; the full name of the circle is missing.
2
Identify the circle's defining features
The third circle is defined by two conditions: it passes through the poles of the horizon (zenith and nadir) and also through the poles of the meridian (east and west). This means it is a great circle orthogonal to both the horizon and the meridian.
In coordinate terms, this circle is the prime vertical.
3
Connect to common knowledge
The points mentioned (zenith, nadir, east, west) are familiar in daily life. This circle is the vertical circle that passes through the east-west axis, dividing the sky into northern and southern halves.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( والثالثة : ) منها ( تمر بقطبي الأفق و تمر أيضاً ( بقطبي هذه الدائرة ( أعني وسط السماء ) المسماة في المشهور بنصف النهار فتكون مارة بسمتي الرأس والقدم وبنقطتي المشرق والمغرب ( وتسمى ) هذه
And the third of them: it passes through the poles of the horizon and also passes through the poles of this circle, I mean the middle of the sky, which is commonly called the noon circle, so it passes through the zenith and the nadir and through the two points of the east and the west, and it is called...
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage describes the third circle of the five. It passes through the poles of the horizon (which are the zenith and nadir) and also through the poles of the meridian circle. Since the meridian is defined as the circle of noon, its poles are the east and west points. Therefore, this third circle passes through all four cardinal points: the zenith, the nadir, the east, and the west. This circle is thus significant in coordinate systems.

Key Terms

المشرق

Key Takeaway
The third circle is defined by its passage through the poles of both the horizon and the meridian, thus encompassing the four cardinal directions.

The Meridian Circle: Definition and Function
Defines the meridian circle and its role in dividing the sphere.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the meridian circle, why it is named so, and how it separates the celestial sphere into ascending and descending halves.

Core Idea
The meridian circle splits the sky into rising and falling halves.

In this passage, the author defines the second great circle of the celestial sphere—the meridian. He explains its name, its relationship to the horizon and celestial equator, and its crucial role in dividing the sphere into ascending (eastern) and descending (western) halves. This concept is fundamental for understanding daily star motion.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation to grasp the flow of the definition.
Pay attention to the terms for meridian and equator.
2
Understand the meridian's definition and naming
Identify that the meridian passes through the poles of the horizon and equator, and note the reason for its name: the sun reaches it at noon and midnight.
3
Analyze the star's motion and division of the sphere
Trace the star's journey from rising to setting and below the horizon, noting the two key points: upper culmination (maximum altitude) and lower culmination (maximum descent). See how the meridian separates the ascending half (east) from the descending half (west).
The ascending half is from lower to upper culmination; the descending half is from upper to lower.
4
Identify the poles of the meridian
Learn that the poles of the meridian are the east and west points on the horizon, which are the intersections of the horizon with the celestial equator.
This is because the meridian passes through the poles of the horizon and equator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالثانية : ) منها ( تمر بقطبي الأفق وبقطبي معدل النهار وهي دائرة وسط السماء ) وتسمى دائرة نصف النهار لأن منتصف النهار هو حين وصول الشمس إليها فوق الأفق، كما أن منتصف الليل هو حين وصولها إليها تحته ) وتفصل ) هذه الدائرة ( بين الصاعد والهابط من الفلك وبين النصف الشرقي والغربي منه فإن الكوكب إذا طلع من الأفق يتزايد ارتفاعه شيئاً فشيئاً إلى أن يبلغ نصف النهار فهناك غاية ارتفاعه عن الأفق وإذا انحط منها يتناقص ارتفاعه إلى غروبه، وإذا غرب ينحط عن الأفق متزايداً انحطاطه إلى أن يبلغ نصف النهار تحت الأرض، فهناك غاية انحطاطه عنه ، ثم إنه يأخذ في التقارب منه متناقصاً انحطاطه إلى أن يبلغ الأفق من جهة الشرق ثانياً، فمن غاية الانحطاط تحت الأفق إلى غاية الارتفاع فوقه على خلاف توالي البروج هو النصف الصاعد من الفلك بالقياس إلى الحركة الأولى ويسمى النصف الشرقي أيضاً. ومن غاية الارتفاع إلى غاية الانحطاط هو النصف الهابط منه والنصف الغربي أيضاً. وقطباها نقطتا المشرق والمغرب من الأفق أعني نقطتي تقاطعه مع المعدل وذلك لمرورها بأقطابهما فيما يمران بقطبيهما لما مر
The second: from them passes through the poles of the horizon and the poles of the celestial equator, and it is the circle of the middle of the sky, and it is called the meridian circle because midday is when the sun reaches it above the horizon, and midnight is when it reaches it below the horizon. And this circle separates the ascending and descending parts of the sphere and the eastern and western halves. For when a star rises from the horizon, its altitude increases gradually until it reaches the meridian; there is its maximum altitude above the horizon. And when it declines from it, its altitude decreases until its setting. And when it sets, it descends from the horizon with increasing descent until it reaches the meridian below the earth; there is its maximum descent. Then it begins to approach it with decreasing descent until it reaches the horizon from the east again. So from the point of maximum descent below the horizon to the point of maximum altitude above it, contrary to the order of the signs, is the ascending half of the sphere with respect to the first motion, and it is also called the eastern half. And from the point of maximum altitude to the point of maximum descent is the descending half and also the western half. Its poles are the points of east and west on the horizon, i.e., the points of its intersection with the equator, because it passes through their poles as they pass through its poles, as mentioned.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage defines the second circle, the meridian (dā'irat niṣf al-nahār). It passes through the poles of the horizon and the poles of the celestial equator, making it the circle of the middle of the sky. The author explains its name: midday is when the sun crosses it above the horizon, midnight when it crosses below. The meridian divides the sphere into ascending and descending halves (or eastern and western halves) with respect to the daily motion. The description details a star's motion: rising, culminating at the meridian, setting, reaching lower culmination, then rising again. The ascending half is from lower culmination to upper culmination (eastern), the descending half from upper to lower (western). The poles of the meridian are the east and west points on the horizon, which are the intersections of the horizon with the celestial equator.

Key Terms
دائرة نصف النهار
معدل النهار
الصاعد
الهابط
النصف الشرقي
النصف الغربي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the meridian only divides east-west halves of the sky, without affecting the vertical motion of stars.
Correction: The meridian also separates the ascending (rising) and descending (setting) halves of a star's path, as detailed in the passage: the star's altitude increases until the meridian, then decreases.

Checkpoint
What does the meridian circle separate according to this passage?
A) Day and night
B) The ascending and descending halves of the sphere
C) The northern and southern hemispheres
D) The real and sensory horizons

Key Takeaway
The meridian circle divides the celestial sphere into ascending (eastern) and descending (western) halves based on a star's altitude relative to the horizon.
